Introduction 
ORGANIZATION, JURISDICTION AND POWERS OF THE COURTS CONCERNED WITH CIVIL LITIGATION
Court of Appeal (PART OF SUPREME COURT)
S3 of SCJA
- Court of Appeal is a superior court of record
- Part 5 SCJA stipulates the jurisdiction of the CA

General Division of the High Court (PART OF SUPREME COURT)
S3 of SCJA
- General Division of High Court is a superior court of record 
- Has both appellate jurisdiction (s20-22 SCJA) and original jurisdiction – over matters commenced for the first time (s16-17 and s17A)

General Civil Jurisdiction 
S16 of SCJA 
- Gen Div of High Court has jurisdiction to hear and try any action in personam where: 
a. Df served with originating claim or any other originating process in Singapore (acco [s 16(1)(a)(i) pursuant to ROC/FJR) or overseas (according to ROC/FJR): Df is served with an originating claim or any other originating process: 
(i) In Singapore in the manner prescribed by ROC or FJR 
(ii) Outside Singapore in the circumstances authorized by and in the manner prescribed by ROC or FJR 
b. Df submits to the jurisdiction of the Gen Div  [s 16(1)(b)]

Specific Civil Jurisdiction 
S17 of SCJA 
- Gen Div civil jurisdiction includes: 
a. Jurisdiction relating to divorce and matrimonial causes (except Muslim marriages; section 17A)
b. Jurisdiction relating to matters of admiralty  (Section 3, HCAJA)
c. Jurisdiction relating to bankruptcy or to companies 
d. Jurisdiction to appoint and control guardians of infants and generally over persons and property of infants
e. Jurisdiction to appoint and control guardians and keepers of the persons and estates of idiots, mentally disordered persons and persons of unsound mind;
f. jurisdiction to grant probates of wills and testaments, letters of administration of the estates of deceased persons and to alter or revoke such grants;
g. jurisdiction under the Mediation Act 2017 to record a mediated settlement agreement made in a mediation, in relation to a dispute for which no proceedings have been commenced in a court, as an order of court; and
h. jurisdiction under the Singapore Convention on Mediation Act 2020 to grant the applications provided for in that Act in respect of international settlement agreements.

GDHC has inherent jurisdiction to hear and punish cases of contempt of Court
Li Shengwu v AG 
- High Court and Court of Appeal possess inherent jurisdiction to hear and punish cases of contempt of Court because of their status as superior courts of law 
- Reason: Court’s jurisdiction to hear and punish for contempt is a natural and immutable consequence of its existence as the institution charged with the role of administering and dispensing justice 
Concurrent Civil Jurisdiction with Syariah Court in certain matters
S17A of SCJA:
- Gen Div has no jurisdiction to hear any civil proceedings involving matters within jurisdiction of Syariah Court or of Administration of Muslim Law Act in which all parties are Muslim or where parties were married under provisions of Muslim law
- This is with exception to matters relating to: 
a. Maintenance for any wife or child
b. Custody of any child
c. Disposition or division of property on divorce 
GDHC Power to decide matter without oral hearing 
S17B of SCJA
- Gen Div of High Court has power to decide any matter in its original civil jurisdiction without hearing oral arguments 
- Exceptions (where oral hearing is still required): 
a. Not for matters prescribed by Rules of Court or Family Justice Rules 
b. Does not allow any part of a proceeding where oral evidence is given to be conducted without an oral hearing unless all parties consent --> i.e. all parties must consent to dispensing with an oral hearing
GDHC Power to summarily dismiss certain matters 
S17C of SCJA
- Conditions to summarily dismiss appeal: Judge sitting in Gen Div of High Court may on his motion summarily dismiss any appeal made against a decision of Registrar relating to civil proceedings if he is satisfied that: 
a. Every issue in the appeal has already been decided by Gen Div, Appellate Division or Court of Appeal in an earlier matter in which appellant was involved and the current appeal thus has no merit 
b. Conditions prescribed by ROC or FJR are met 
- Procedure prior to summarily dismissing appeal: Before summarily dismissing an appeal, judge must:
a. Give appellant a reasonable opportunity to show cause why appeal should not be summarily dismissed; and 
b. Consider any representations of appellant 
GDHC Other powers
First Schedule of SCJA
- Prerogative orders
o Mandatory Order (Mandamus)
o Prohibiting Order (Prohibition)
o Quashing Order (Certiorari)
o Order for Review of Detention (a writ of habeas corpus)
- Power to partition land and to direct a sale in lieu of partition
- Power to charge or mortgage in lieu of sale of land
- Power to grant relief by way of interpleader
- Power to preserve subject matter, evidence and assets to satisfy judgment
- Power to direct interest to be paid of damages, debts, judgement debts etc.
- Power to enlarge or abridge the time prescribed by any written law (but does not affect laws relating to limitation)
- Power to enforce a judgment of the court
- Power to stay or transfer proceedings
- Power to allow a defence of set-off
- Power to order discovery and interrogatories
- Power to award costs
- Power to grant all reliefs and remedies at law and in equity
- Power to order a party in a pending proceeding to make interim payments to another party or stakeholder or into court in account of any damages, debt or other sum
- Power to award damages for personal injuries
- Power to order periodic payments
- Power to attach deposits with a financial institution 
- Power to order a medical examination
- Power to appoint a registered medical practitioner, psychologist, counsellor, social worker or mental health professional to examine and assess the child or person (as the case may be) for the purposes of preparing expert evidence for use in those proceedings; and to prohibit from being adduced in those proceedings any evidence arising out of an examination or assessment of the child or person by any person who is not so appointed.
- Power to order any party to undergo such mediation or counselling (family support programme or activity)
- Power to require persons to pay a late filing charge
- Power to order any party to any proceedings to attempt to resolve any dispute by amicable resolution –
o settlement of an action or appeal relating to a dispute; or
o resolution of a dispute other than by litigation, whether in whole or in part.

District Court (PART OF STATE COURTS)
General Civil Jurisdiction
S19(2) of State Courts Act 1970
- District Court has all the jurisdiction of the Gen Div of High Court to hear and try any action in personam where: 
a. Df served with originating claim or any other originating process in Singapore (according to ROC/FJR) or overseas (according to ROC/FJR): Df is served with an originating claim or any other originating process: 
(i) In Singapore in the manner prescribed by ROC or FJR 
(ii) Outside Singapore in the circumstances authorized by and in the manner prescribed by ROC or FJR 
b. Df submits to the jurisdiction of the District Court 

District Court’s jurisdiction does not include certain matters
S19(3) of State Courts Act
- District Court’s jurisdiction does not include: 
a. Any supervisory jurisdiction or revisionary jurisdiction 
b. Any jurisdiction relating to JR of any act done or decision made by any person or authority and the issue of Mandatory Orders, Prohibiting Orders, Quashing Orders, Orders for Review of Detention 
c. Any jurisdiction vested exclusively in Gen Div of High Court, Family Court, Youth Court or any other State Court, or in any judicial, quasi-judicial or administrative tribunal, by written law 
d. Any jurisdiction to hear and try an action in passing off 
e. Any jurisdiction expressly excluded by written law 

District Court Limit – $250k 
S2 of State Courts Act
- District Court limit is $250k 

If amount of claim exceeds 250k => Claimant can abandon excess amount or that remedy  
S22 of State Courts Act
- If amount claimed exceeds District Court limit of $250k, claimant may abandon the excess amount or that remedy or relief and the District Court would have jurisdiction to hear and try the action 

If action is for recovery of immovable property => Value of property must not exceed District Court Limit  
S25 of State Courts Act
- District Court will have jurisdiction to hear and try an action for recovery of immovable property with or without a claim for rent or mesne profits and whether or not the title to the property is in dispute where:
a. Annual value of property appearing in Valuation List prepared under s10 of Property Tax Act does not exceed District Court Limit; OR
b. Annual rent payable by tenant does not exceed District Court limit or monthly rent payable by tenant does not exceed one-twelfth of District Court Limit; OR 
c. If annual value, annual rent or monthly rent cannot be ascertained, the last transacted price of the property does not exceed 10 times the District Court limit 

Exception: Jurisdiction by agreement even though exceed District Court Limit
S23 of State Courts Act 
- District Court will have jurisdiction to hear a case if parties to an action agree so even though: 
a. Amount claimed in action exceeds District Court limit; or 
b. Any remedy or relief sought in the action is in respect of a subject matter the value of which exceeds the District Court limit 

Specific Civil Jurisdiction 
Jurisdiction in equity 
S26 of State Courts Act 
- District Court has jurisdiction to hear and try any of the following proceedings:
a. deleted
b. Proceedings for execution of any trust or for a declaration that a trust subsists where the amount or value of the property subject or alleged to be the subject, to the trust does not exceed the District Court limit 
c. Proceedings for foreclosure or redemption of any mortgage or for enforcing any change or lien where the amount owing in respect of the mortgage, charge or lien does not exceed the District Court limit 
d. Proceedings for the specific performance, rectification, delivery up or cancellation of any agreement for the sale, purchase or lease of any property where the amount or value of property does not exceed the District Court limit 
e. deleted 
f. Proceedings for the dissolution or winding up of any partnership (other than a limited liability partnership registered under LLP Act) whether or not the existence of the partnership is in dispute, where the amount or value of all the assets of the partnership does not exceed the District Court limit
g. Proceedings for relief against fraud or mistake where the damage sustained or amount or value of estate or fund in respect of which relief is sought does not exceed the District Court limit 

Jurisdiction to issue writ of distress
S28 of State Courts Act
- District Court has jurisdiction under Distress Act 1934 in all cases where the amount of rent distrained or to be distrained does not exceed the District Court limit 

Jurisdiction to grant relief by interpleader 
S29 of State Courts Act

Power of District Court same as power of GDHC
S31 of State Courts Act
- District Court, as regards any action within its jurisdiction and in any proceedings before it, has power: 
a. To grant relief, redress or remedy or a combination either absolute or conditional as ought to be granted in the like action by the Gen Div of High court 
b. To give effect to every ground of defense or counterclaim equitable or legal as ought to be given in the like action by the Gen Div of High Court

- District Court also has power to: 
a. Grant injunction whether or not any other relief is or could be claimed
b. Make binding declarations of rights whether or not any consequential relief is or could be claimed
c. Order medical examination of a person who is a party to any proceedings where the physical or mental condition of the person is relevant to any matter in question in the proceedings 

Magistrate’s Court (PART OF STATE COURTS)
Magistrate’s Court Limit – $60k 
S2 of State Courts Act
- Magistrate Court limit is $60k 

General Civil Jurisdiction
S52(1) of State Courts Act 
- Magistrate Court has all the jurisdiction of the Gen Div of High Court to hear and try any action in personam where: 
a. Df served with originating claim or any other originating process in Singapore (according to ROC/FJR) or overseas (according to ROC/FJR): Df is served with an originating claim or any other originating process: 
(i) In Singapore in the manner prescribed by ROC or FJR 
(ii) Outside Singapore in the circumstances authorized by and in the manner prescribed by ROC or FJR 
b. Df submits to the jurisdiction of the Magistrate Court 

Magistrate Court’s jurisdiction does not include certain matters
S52(1A) of State Courts Act
- Magistrate Court’s jurisdiction does not include: 
a. Any action where there is no claim for any sum of money 
b. Any action in passing off
c. Any action where the amount claimed exceeds the Magistrate Court’s limit of $60k 
d. Any action where the remedy or relief sought in the action in addition or as an alternative to the amount claimed in the action is in respect of a subject matter the value of which exceeds the Magistrate’s Court limit 

Judgement and Order of Magistrate’s Court same as District Court 
S54 of State Courts Act
- Every judgement or order of a Magistrate’s Court in the exercise of its civil jurisdiction has same effect and is to be enforced in the same manner as if it were given or made by a District Court 

Small Claims Tribunals 
Small Claims Tribunal limit – Prescribed limit is $20k and Prescribed extended limit is $30k 
S2 of Small Claims Tribunal Act 
- Prescribed limit: $20k 
- Prescribed extended limit: $30k 

General Civil Jurisdiction 
S5(1) of Small Claims Tribunal Act 
- Small Claims Tribunal has jurisdiction to hear and determine any action which: 
a. Determine any Specified claim: Is a specified claim; and 
b. Action served on Respondent in Singapore (s19(1) of SCT): Is served in Singapore on the respondent under s19(1) of Small Claims Tribunal Act 

Definition of “Specified claim”
The Schedule of Small Claims Tribunal Act 
1. The following are specified claims for the purposes of section 5(1)(a):
a. A claim relating to a contract for the sale of goods or the provision of services;
b. A claim in tort relating to damage caused to any property (other than a claim mentioned in section  4 of the Community Disputes Resolution Act 2015);
c. A claim relating to a contract for the lease of residential premises that does not exceed 2 years;
d. A claim by an owner developer for the recovery of any charge or interest mentioned in section 22(1) of the Building Maintenance and Strata Management Act 2004;
e. A claim by a management corporation for the recovery of any contribution or interest mentioned in section 40(6) of the Building Maintenance and Strata Management Act 2004;
f. A claim by the Housing and Development Board for the recovery of any improvement contribution, interest or penalty mentioned in section 83 of the Housing and Development Act 1959;
g. A claim by the Council of the Singapore Business Federation for the recovery of any subscription mentioned in section 12(4) of the Singapore Business Federation Act 2001;
h. A claim by a Town Council for the recovery of any improvement contribution, interest or penalty mentioned in section 36 of the Town Councils Act 1988;
i. A claim by a Town Council for the recovery of any charge, fee, expense or penalty mentioned in section 73 of the Town Councils Act 1988.

SCT jurisdiction does not include four matters
S5(2) of Small Claims Tribunal Act 
- SCT jurisdiction does NOT include: 
a. Claims in respect of damage caused to property by the use of a motor vehicle: Claims in respect of damage caused to any property by an accident arising out of or in connection with the use of a motor vehicle 
b. Claims which State Courts have no jurisdiction to hear: Claims which the State Courts have no jurisdiction to hear and determine 

S5(3) of Small Claims Tribunal Act
- Unless SCT Act expressly provides so, SCT jurisdiction does NOT apply to: 
a. Claims which value exceeds $20k => However: Parties can agree to grant SCT jurisdiction to hear claims with prescribed extended limit of $30k: Claims where the value of which exceeds prescribed limit of $20k; or 
=> S5(4): SCT will have jurisdiction to hear and determine claims up to the prescribed extended limit of $30k if parties to the claim so agree by a memorandum signed by them 
b. Claims after expiry of 2 years from date which cause of action accrued: Claims after the expiry of 2 years after the date on which the cause of action accrued

Appeals against SCT decision: Appeal to GDHC 
2 Grounds of appeal 
S38(1) of Small Claims Tribunal Act
- Any party to proceedings before SCT may appeal to Gen Div of High Court: 
a. Appeal on ground involving question of law: On any ground involving a question of law; 
b. Appeal on ground that claim is outside SCT jurisdiction: On the ground that the claim was outside the jurisdiction of the SCT 

Party needs approval from District Court to appeal to GDHC 
S38(2) of Small Claims Tribunal Act
- Party can only appeal to the Gen Div of High Court if permission is given by the District Court 

If s38(1) is unsatisfied => District Court can order matter be remitted to SCT for consideration or order a rehearsing of matter by a different tribunal 
S38(3) of Small Claims Tribunal Act
- If District Court is not satisfied that the proposed appeal satisfies s38(1), District Court can refuse to give permission to appeal and also: 
a. Order that the matter be remitted to the SCT for its consideration; OR 
b. Order a rehearing of the matter by a different tribunal 

General Powers of the Court 
Power when there is non-compliance with Rules of Court 2021 
O 3 r 2(4) ROC 2021
- Where there is non-compliance with Rules of Court, any written law, Court’s orders or directions or practice directions, the Court may exercise all or any of the following powers: 
a. Waive non-compliance of Rule, PD… etc. (if written law allows such a waiver): Waive non-compliance of the Rule, written law, Court order or direction or PD (if the written law allows such waiver) 
b. Disallow filing or use of document: Disallow or reject the filing or use of any document 
c. Refuse to hear any matter/Dismiss without hearing: Refuse to hear any matter or dismiss it without a hearing 
d. Dismiss, stay or set aside proceedings even if non-compliance can be compensated by costs: Dismiss, stay or set aside any proceedings and give appropriate judgment or order even though the non-compliance could be compensated by costs (if the non-compliance is inconsistent with any of the Ideals in a material way) 
e. Impose late filing fee of $50 for each day of non-compliance: Impose a late filing fee of $50 for each day that a document remains unfiled after the expiry of the period within which the document is required to be filed, excluding non-court days
f. Make adverse costs orders: Make costs orders or any other others that are appropriate 

Power to revoke judgement, order or anything which was done 
Grounds for revocation 
O 3 r 2(8)
- Court can revoke any judgement or order obtained or set aside anything which was done:
a. Anything done without notice to party affected: Without notice to, or in the absence of the party affected
b. Anything done without compliance with ROC or Court order: Without complying with these Rules or any order of court
c. Anything done contrary to law: Contrary to any written law
d. Anything done by fraud or misrepresentation: By fraud or misrepresentation

Party affected must make application for Court to revoke order within 14 days after he know that grounds for revocation exist
O 3 r 2(8)
- Any party affected can make an application for the Court to revoke judgement but he must do so within 14 days after the date he knows or should have known that any of the grounds to do so exist 

SOURCES OF CIVIL PROCEDURAL LAW 
Statutes 
- SCJA
- SCA

Rules of Court 2021
- Came into operation from 1 April 2022 
- Applies to Civil proceedings commenced in Supreme Court or State Courts on or after 1 April 2022 (O 1 r 2(3)(a))
- Applies to appeals to the Court of Appeal and Appellate Division on or after 1 April 2022 (O 1 r 2(3)(b))
- First Schedule to ROC 2021 states saving and transitional provisions and continued relevance of the revoked ROC 2014 to cases commenced before 1 April 2022 

*5 Ideals of the Rules of Court 2021
O 3 r 1 ROC 2021 – rules are to be given a purposive interpretation
- The Rules of Court 2021 seek to achieve the following Ideals in civil procedure:
a. Fair access to justice,
b. Expeditious proceedings,
c. Cost-effective work proportionate to:
(i) Nature and importance of action
(ii) Complexity of claim and difficulty or novelty of issues and questions it raises
(iii) Amount or value of claim 
d. Efficient use of court resources,
e. Fair and practical results suited to the needs of the parties.

Prof: not able to achieve all these ideals all of the time especially as they can conflict. Court will weigh up the diff ideals on a case-by-case basis and decide where to strike the balance.
- E.g. Df asks for more time to prepare defense as they are a layperson, need to find a lawyer, need to find documents [Ideals (1) vs (2); Court may thus choose to give some extra time but maybe not the full period that Df is asking for]


Practice Directions
- Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 (*note that the forms for various court forms are contained in Appendix A to the PDs)

Practice Circulars 
- Practice Circulars from the Courts and Law Society of Singapore 

Civil Justice Reforms (background reading) 

Miscellaneous Issues 
Calculation of Time 
Order 3 r 3
(3)  “Within 6 days after X” – 6 days not including X => Where an act is required to be done within a specified period after or from a specified date, the period begins immediately after that date.
(4) “Within 6 days before X” – 6 days not including X => Where an act is required to be done within or not less than a specified period before a specified date, the period ends immediately before that date.
(5)  Where an act is required to be done a specified number of clear days before or after a specified date, at least that number of days must intervene between the day on which the act is done and that date.
(6)  If the period in question is 6 days or less, any day that is a non?court day is to be excluded in the calculation of time.
(7)  Where the time prescribed by these Rules, or by any judgment, order or direction, for doing any act expires on a non?court day, the act is in time if done on the next day, not being a non?court day.

Applications to Court in an action 
O 3 r 5 process
After action has started => Any application must be made by Summons supported by affidavit
O 3 r 5(1)  
- Subject to these Rules, all applications to the Court in an action must be made by summons in Form 1 or Form 2, whichever is appropriate, and supported by affidavit.

When summons has to be served => Summons in Form 1 
O 3 r 5(2)
- Form 1 is to be used when the summons has to be served.

Contents of affidavit 
O 3 r 5(7)
- An affidavit must contain all necessary evidence in support of or in opposition (as the case may be) to the application, and may contain statements of information or belief with their sources and grounds clearly stated.

Applicant must serve summons and affidavit at least 14 days before hearing 
O 3 r 5(4)
- The applicant must serve the summons and the affidavit at least 14 days before the hearing of the summons.

If Respondent wishes to contest, must file and serve Respondent affidavit within 14 days after being served summons and affidavit 
O 3 r 5(5) 
- If any party wishes to contest the application, the party must file and serve his or her affidavit within 14 days after being served with the application and affidavit.

After that, no affidavits unless Court allows 
O 3 r 5(8) 
- Except in a special case, the Court will not allow further affidavits to be filed after the other party files his or her affidavit under paragraph (5).
After action has started => Any application must be made by Summons supported by affidavit 
O 3 r 5(1)  
- Subject to these Rules, all applications to the Court in an action must be made by summons in Form 1 or Form 2, whichever is appropriate, and supported by affidavit.

Where summons need not be served => Form 2 
O 3 r 5(3) 
- Form 2 is to be used when the summons need not be served on anyone and only where these Rules allow

Contents of affidavit 
O 3 r 5(7)
- An affidavit must contain all necessary evidence in support of or in opposition (as the case may be) to the application, and may contain statements of information or belief with their sources and grounds clearly stated.


Pre-action considerations – starting action – initial steps after starting 
*MATTERS TO BE CONSIDERED BEFORE COMMENCING PROCEEDINGS 
General considerations 
First contact with client: Collect documents from client 
Managing the relationship with client 
1. Document terms of engagement 
2. Ensuring there are no conflicts of interests 
3. Run anti-money laundering checks 
4. Take and document client’s instructions (to have record in case of dispute)

Issue: Advising client on considerations prior to commencing an action 
1. Is there a cause of action?
2. Is the action time barred? (i.e. is the limitation period still current?)
3. Can there be an amicable resolution? (positive duty to consider amicable resolution) 
4. Which Court do you sue in? (jurisdictional limitations) 
5. Are there any dispute resolution clauses (e.g. exclusive/non-exclusive jurisdiction clauses, arbitration clauses) that are applicable?
6. Are there any pre-action protocols? (medical negligence claims) [NB: BACKGROUND INFO ONLY]
7. Are there any special considerations due to status of your client or the opposing party? [NB: BACKGROUND INFO ONLY]

Issue: Advising client on considerations prior to commencing an action  
Step 1: Is there a cause of action? 

Step 2: Is the action time barred? 
Actions which are time-barred 6 years after date on which cause of action accrue 
S6 of Limitation Act 1959
- Subject to the Limitation Act, the following actions shall NOT be brought after expiration of 6 years from date on which cause of action accrued: 
a. Actions founded on a contract or on tort 
b. Actions to enforce a recognizance 
c. Actions to enforce an award
d. Actions to recover any sum recoverable by virtue of any written law other than a penalty or forfeiture or sum by way of penalty or forfeiture 

For latent personal injuries + Negligence, nuisance, breach of duty actions – Limitation period of 3 years
S24A(2) of Limitation Act 
- For action for damages for negligence, nuisance or breach of duty + Damages claimed consists or include damages for personal injuries to the claimant or any other person => Action shall not be brought after the expiration of: 
a. 3 years from the date on which the cause of action accrued; OR 
b. 3 years from the earliest date on which the claimant has the knowledge required for bringing an action for damages in respect of the relevant injury, if that period expires later than the period mentioned in s24A(2)(a)

For latent non-personal damage + Negligence, nuisance, breach of duty actions – Limitation period of 6 years or 3 years under s24A(3)
S24A(3) of Limitation Act 
- For action for damages for negligence, nuisance or breach of duty + Damages claimed consists or include damages that are NOT for personal injuries to the claimant => Action shall not be brought after the expiration of: 
a. 6 years from the date on which the cause of action accrued; OR 
b. 3 years from the earliest date on which the claimant has the knowledge required for bringing an action for damages in respect of the relevant injury and a right to do so, if that period expires later than the period mentioned in s24A(3)(a)

Overriding time limit for all actions – 15 years from “starting date”
S24B(1) of Limitation Act 
- Any action for damages for negligence, nuisance or breach of duty to which s24A applies shall not be brought after the expiration of 15 years from the starting date 

Definition of “starting date”: Date on which act or omission constituting negligence/nuisance/breach of duty occurred 
S24B(2) of Limitation Act
- Starting date: The date on which there occurred any act or omission which is alleged to constitute negligence, nuisance or breach of duty and to which the injury or damage claimed is alleged to be attributable to in whole or in part 

For fraud or mistake – Limitation period will only begin to run when claimant could with reasonable diligence have discovered the fraud or mistake
S29(1) of Limitation Act 
- Where: 
a. Action is based on Df’s fraud OR: The action is based upon the fraud of the Df or his agent or of any person through whom he claims or his agent; or 
b. Right of action concealed by fraud of Df OR: Right of action is concealed by the fraud of any such person; OR 
c. Action for relief from consequence of mistake: Action is for relief from the consequences of a mistake 
The period of limitation shall not begin to run until the claimant has discovered the fraud or mistake, as the case may be, or could with reasonable diligence have discovered it 

Definition of “fraud” 
Chua Teck Chew Robert v Goh Eng Wah 
- Fraud under s29(1)(b) of LA includes: 
a. Unconscionability in the form of a deliberate act of concealment of a right of action by the wrongdoer 
b. Situation where wrongdoer had knowingly or recklessly committed a wrongdoing in secret without telling the aggrieved party 

Definition of “reasonable diligence”
Chua Teck Chew Robert v Goh Eng Wah 
- Reasonable diligence means the doing of that which an ordinary prudent person would do having regard to all the circumstances and it does not mean doing of everything possible or using any means at one’s disposal 
a. Peco Arts Inc v Hazlitt Gallery: A buyer would not be unreasonable for choosing to not undertake an independent evaluation 
b. E.g. Where a party has made every inquiry and exertion that is within their ordinary ability to ascertain 

Claim by way of set-off or counterclaim are separate actions commencing on same date as the main action is pleaded
S31 of Limitation Act 
- Any claim by way of set-off or counterclaim shall be deemed to be a separate action and to have been commenced on the same date as the action in which the set-off or counterclaim is pleaded

Step 3: Can there be an amicable resolution?
Option 1: Parties have a positive duty to consider amicable resolution before and during action  
O 5 r 1(1) of ROC 2021
- Parties have a duty to consider amicable resolution before commencement and during the course of any action 

Claimant must make offer of amicable resolution before commencing action => Unless reasonable grounds not to
O 5 r 1(2)
- A party has to make an offer of amicable resolution before commencing action unless the party has reasonable grounds not to do so 

Contents of offer of amicable resolution 
O 5 r 1(3)
- Offer of amicable resolution in Order 5 means: 
* Making an offer to settle the action or appeal; OR 
* Making an offer to resolve the dispute other than by litigation, whether in whole or in part 

Defendant must not reject an offer of amicable resolution => Unless reasonable grounds to do so 
O 5 r 1(4) 
- A party to any proceedings must not reject an offer of amicable resolution unless the party has reasonable grounds to do so

What are reasonable grounds? 
- E.g. there is an urgent time bar issue – if you make offer of amicable resolution then you’ll be time-barred 
- E.g. claiming for fraud – doesn’t make sense to offer to amicably resolve with someone who is defrauding you 
- E.g. real risk of Df dissipating his assets – if an offer is made the Df might be alerted

Terms of amicable resolution 
Offer and rejection must be in writing 
O 5 r 2(1) 
- An offer of amicable resolution and any rejection must be in writing

Offer must be open for acceptance for at least 14 days unless parties agree otherwise 
O 5 r 2(2) 
- An offer of amicable resolution must be open for acceptance within a reasonable period of time and in any case, for at least 14 days, unless the parties otherwise agree

Offer without expiry date will expire once Court determines merits of action => Unless stated otherwise 
O 5 r 2(4) 
- Any offer of amicable resolution which does not state an expiry date expires once the Court has determined the merits of the action or appeal to which it relates unless the offeror has stated otherwise.

Court must not rely on rejected offers until Court has determined action and is dealing with issue of costs 
O 5 r 2(3)
- The terms of an offer that has been made and not accepted must not be relied upon or made known to the Court until after the Court has determined the merits of the action or appeal and is dealing with the issue of costs

Cost consequences for failure to consider amicable resolution 
O 21 r 4(c)
- If a party has not discharged its duty to consider amicable resolution of the dispute => Court may:
* Disallow or reduce a successful party’s costs; or 
* Order the successful party to pay costs 

No other consequences – proceedings can’t be struck out or anything. Only costs can be ordered. 
- The fact that the other party breached this duty is something that the party seeking costs can raise, in order to argue why costs should be awarded against the breaching party. Just something that goes towards the reasonableness of the party’s conduct. 

Option 2: Court can order parties to attempt an amicable resolution  
Court has power to order parties to attempt to resolve dispute by amicable resolution 
O 5 r 3(1) 
- The Court may order the parties to attempt to resolve the dispute by amicable resolution.
=> In exercising power, Court will have regard to ideals and other relevant circumstances including whether any of the parties have refused to attempt to resolve dispute by amicable resolution (O 5 r 3(2) ROC)

If party informs Court that he does not wish to attempt to resolve dispute by amicable resolution => Court may order party to submit sealed document setting out reasons for refusal => Sealed document will only be opened by Court after determination of action and dealing with issue of costs 
O 5 r 3(3)
- Without affecting the Court’s power under paragraph (1), if a party informs the Court that the party does not wish to attempt to resolve the dispute by amicable resolution, the Court may order the party to submit a sealed document setting out the party’s reasons for such refusal.

O 5 r 3(4) 
- The sealed document will only be opened by the Court after the determination of the merits of the action or appeal and its contents may be referred to on any issue of costs.


Step 4: Which Court do you sue in? 
Consider dispute resolution clauses 
- For contracting parties – Consider dispute resolution clauses like arbitration clauses, exclusive jurisdiction clauses and non-exclusive jurisdiction clauses 

Consider jurisdictional limitations of Courts 
- Summary of jurisdictional limitations: 
a. Small Claims Tribunal: <$20k, but <$30k with agreement from opposing party
b. Magistrate’s Court: <$60k (i.e. between $20 or 30-60K)
c. District Court: <$250k (i.e. between $60-250K)
d. High Court: >$250k 



Step 5: Are there any pre-action protocols which must be carried out? [NB: BACKGROUND INFO ONLY]
For Medical Negligence Claims – Must fill up Form 1 in Appendix H  
Paragraph 163 read with Appendix H of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Basically must fill up some Form 1 in Appendix H of the Practice Directions
- Purpose of protocol: Streamline the production of documents before action begins and to facilitate the exchange of information prior to filing of an OC with a view to resolving medical negligence disputes without protracted litigation 

Consequence of non-compliance: Court may make adverse costs orders
Para 163(2) of Supreme Court PD 2021
- If non-compliance with protocol has led to unnecessary costs, Court can make the following orders: 
a. An order disallowing a defaulting party his costs, or some part of his costs, even if he succeeds 
b. An order that defaulting party pay the other party their costs of proceedings or part of those costs
c. An order that defaulting party pay those costs on an indemnity basis 

Consequence of non-compliance: Court may consider non-compliance when deciding amount of interest payable
Para 163(3) of Supreme Court PD 2021
- Court will consider non-compliance with protocol in exercising discretion when deciding amount of interest payable and may make the following orders:
a. An order awarding successful party who has complied with protocol interest from a date earlier than the date from which he would otherwise have been entitled to obtain interest 
b. An order depriving a successful party who has not complied with the protocol of interest in respect of such period as may be specified

Step 6: Are there any special considerations due to status of your client or the opposing party?  [NB: BACKGROUND INFO ONLY]
a. Bankrupt 
b. Person below 21 
c. Person lacking capacity under MCA
d. Group of persons 
e. Dead person

Bankrupt cannot commence, continue or defend action without previous sanction of Official Assignee => Unless 2 exceptions 
S401(1) of IRDA 
- Bankrupt who has not obtained his discharge: 
a. Unless the bankrupt has obtained the previous sanction of the Official Assignee, he cannot commence, continue or defend: 
(i) Any action other than: 
A. Exception 1: Action for damages in respect of personal injury to bankrupt and appeals: An action for damages in respect of an injury to the bankrupt’s person; or 
B. Exception 2: Matrimonial proceedings and appeals: A matrimonial proceeding; or 
(ii) An appeal arising from (A) or (B) 
b. The bankrupt must not leave, or remain or reside outside Singapore without the previous permission of the Official Assignee 

If Bankrupt does not seek Official Assignee sanction => Bankrupt will be deemed as lacking locus standi 
Aarthar Ah Kong v OUE 
- Bankrupt must seek Official Assignee’s sanction prior to commencing an action including filing of a notice of appeal 
- Effect of not doing so:
a. Bankrupt will be deemed as lacking locus standi to commence action 

Official Assignee cannot grant retrospective sanction of an action already commenced
Aarthar Ah Kong v OUE 
- Official Assignee cannot grant retrospective sanction i.e. sanction cannot be granted ipso facto 

Person below 21 must be represented by litigation representative => Unless person above 18 (and below 21) and s36 of CLA applies  
O 4 r 2(a) of ROC 2021
- The following persons must be represented by a litigation representative in proceedings: A person who is below 21 years of age and who does not come under O 4 r1(a) 

Exception: Person above 18 but below 21 can represent himself to bring, defend, conduct or intervene in legal proceedings specified in Schedule of CLA 
O 4 r 1(a)
- Person who is 18 years of age or older but below 21 and where s36 of the Civil Law Act applies can be a party to proceedings in his own name 

S36 of CLA 
(1) Notwithstanding any other written law, a minor who has attained the age of 18 years and who is not otherwise under any legal disability:
a. May, in his own name and without a litigation representative, bring, defend, conduct or intervene in any legal proceeding or action specified in the Schedule as if he were of full age; and
b. Shall not be considered to be a person under disability on account of his age for the purpose of any such legal proceeding or action or for the purpose of any written law regulating the limitation of actions.
(2) The Minister may, at any time, by order published in the Gazette, amend the Schedule.

The Schedule of CLA
1. Actions arising from S35 contracts entered into by a minor (18 to 21 years old): Any legal proceeding or action arising from or in connection with a contract to which the minor is a party and which, by virtue of section 35, has effect as if the minor were of full age.
2. Actions arising from minor acting as director of company or manager of LLP: Any legal proceeding or action arising from or in connection with the minor acting as:
a. A director of a company; or
b. A manager of a limited liability partnership.
3. Actions arising from bill of exchange drawn or indorsed by minor: Any legal proceeding or action arising from or in connection with a bill of exchange that has been drawn or indorsed:
a. By the minor; or
b. By another person in favour of the minor.
4. Where minor registered under Business Names Registration Act to carry on business as sole proprietor/partner – Actions arising from carrying on of such business: Where the minor is registered under the Business Names Registration Act 2014 to carry on any business (whether as a sole proprietor or in partnership with one or more other persons):
a. Any legal proceeding or action arising from or in connection with his carrying on of such business; or
b. [Deleted by Act 29 of 2014]
5. Where minor carries on business in Singapore as registered limited partnership – Actions arising from carrying on of such business: Where the minor carries on business in Singapore as a limited partnership registered under the Limited Partnerships Act 2008 (whether as a general partner or a limited partner):
a. Any legal proceeding or action arising from or in connection with his carrying on of such business; or
b. Any legal proceeding or action arising from or in connection with his acting as a person responsible for the management of such business (as defined in section 39(2) of that Act).

Requirements for litigation representative 
- O 4 r 2(2): Litigation representative must be a person who can be a party to proceedings in his own name 
- O 4 r 2(3): Litigation representative must not have any interest adverse to that of the person he is representing 

Person cannot bring, make a claim in, defend, make a counterclaim, intervene, appear in proceedings except by his litigation representative 
O 44 r 3 of ROC 2021
- Person under disability may not bring, make a claim in, defend, make a counterclaim in, or intervene in any proceedings or appear in any proceedings under a judgement or order notice of which has been served on him or her, except by his litigation representative 
a. O 44 r 3(2): Litigation representative must and may do everything in the ordinary conduct of proceedings required or authorized by the Rules of Court 2021 on behalf of the person under disability 
b. O 44 r 3(3): Litigation representative must act by a solicitor

Meaning of “person under disability”
O 44 r 1
- “Person under disability” means, subject to O 4 r 1:
a. A person who is a minor; OR 
b. A person lacking capacity

Person who lacks capacity under MCA must be represented by litigation representative 
O 4 r 2(b) of ROC 2021
- The following persons must be represented by a litigation representative in proceedings: A person who lacks capacity within the meaning of the Mental Capacity Act in relation to matters concerning the person’s property and affairs 

Requirements for litigation representative 
- O 4 r 2(2): Litigation representative must be a person who can be a party to proceedings in his own name 
- O 4 r 2(3): Litigation representative must not have any interest adverse to that of the person he is representing 

Person cannot bring, make a claim in, defend, make a counterclaim, intervene, appear in proceedings except by his litigation representative 
O 44 r 3 of ROC 2021
- Person under disability may not bring, make a claim in, defend, make a counterclaim in, or intervene in any proceedings or appear in any proceedings under a judgement or order notice of which has been served on him or her, except by his litigation representative 
a. O 44 r 3(2): Litigation representative must and may do everything in the ordinary conduct of proceedings required or authorized by the Rules of Court 2021 on behalf of the person under disability 
b. O 44 r 3(3): Litigation representative must act by a solicitor

Meaning of “person under disability”
O 44 r 1
- “Person under disability” means, subject to O 4 r 1:
a. A person who is a minor; OR 
b. A person lacking capacity

Group of persons with common interest in proceedings may sue or be sued as a group => One of them will represent the group 
O 4 r 6 of ROC 2021
- Where numerous persons have a common interest in proceedings, such persons may sue or be sued as a group with one or more of them representing the group 

Procedure for group to sue 
All group members must give consent in writing to representative to represent them => OA or OC sent by representative must include list of group members 
O 4 r 6(2) 
- Where a group of persons is suing under this rule, all members in the group must give their consent in writing to the representative to represent all of them in the action and they must include a list of claimants attached to the OA or OC

Procedure when group is sued 
When group is sued => Court will ask group members to give consent and appoint one representative from group => Order of Court will include list of group members
O 4 r 6(3)
- Where a group of persons is sued under this rule, Court may appoint one or more of them as representative to represent those in the group who have given their consent in writing to the representative in the action and they must include a list of defendants attached to the Order of Court 

Procedure when class of person is sued 
When class of person is sued => Court will appoint one representative to represent the class => Order of Court will include list of all known member of the class 
O 4 r 6(4)
- Court can appoint one or more persons to represent the entire class or part of the class and all the known members and the class must be included in a list of defendants attached to the order of Court 

Judgement or Order binding on person listed in O 4 r 6(3) and 6(4)
O 4 r 6(5)
- A judgment or order given in such an action is binding on all the persons and the class named in the respective lists stated in paragraphs (3) and (4).

Where client dies but cause of action survives => No grant of probate or LOA => Action can be commenced or brought against client’s estate – “Personal representatives of (client’s name) deceased”
O 4 r 4(1) of ROC 2021
- Where defendant has died but a cause of action against him survives and no grant of probate or letters of administration has been made, the action may be brought against the estate of the deceased which is to be described as “personal representatives of (defendant’s name) deceased” 

Instructions for Claimant suing estate of deceased person 
O 4 r 4(2) of ROC 2021 
- In any action brought against the estate of a deceased person:
a. During period of validity for service of OC/OA – Claimant must apply to Court for: The claimant must, during the period of validity for service of the originating claim or originating application, apply to the Court for:
(i) Order appointing person to represent deceased’s estate: An order appointing a person to represent the deceased’s estate for the purpose of the proceedings; or
(ii) If grant of probate or LOA made – Order that PR of deceased by made a party to proceedings: If a grant of probate or letters of administration has been made — an order that the personal representative of the deceased be made a party to the proceedings,
and in either case for an order that the proceedings be carried on against the person appointed or (as the case may be) against the personal representative, as if he or she had been substituted for the estate; and
b. The Court may, at any stage of the proceedings and on any terms that the Court thinks just and either of its own motion or on application, make any order mentioned in sub?paragraph (a) and allow amendments (if any) to be made, and make any other order that the Court thinks necessary in order to ensure that all matters in dispute in the proceedings may be effectually and completely determined and adjudicated upon.

If Public Trustee appointed under O 4 r 4(2) => Claimant must serve OC/OA on Public Trustee => Public Trustee only needs to accept service unless Public Trustee consents to take further steps on behalf of estate
O 4 r 4(3)
- Where the Public Trustee has been appointed by an order under paragraph (2) to represent the deceased’s estate, the originating claim or originating application for the action must be served on the Public Trustee, whose appointment is limited to accepting service of the originating claim or originating application unless the Public Trustee consents to take further steps in the proceedings on behalf of the estate of the defendant.

If no grant of probate or LOA => Any judgement or order will bind estate as if grant had been made
O 4 r 4(4)
- Where no grant of probate or letters of administration has been made, any judgment or order given or made in the action binds the estate to the same extent as if a grant had been made and a personal representative of the deceased had been a party to the action.

Issue: What if your client does not have enough evidence to commence proceedings? 
- Pre-action production of documents (pre-action discovery) 
- Pre-action production of information (pre-action interrogatories)



*PRE-ACTION PRODUCTION OF DOCUMENTS AND INFORMATION – AGAINST 3RD PARTY, IF PLAINTIFF NOT IN POSITION TO SUE YET BECAUSE IT NEEDS MORE INFO
Applicant can apply to Court for an order for non-party (need not be the main Df) to produce documents and information  
O 11 r 11(1) of ROC
- Court may order production of documents and information before commencement of proceedings or against a non-party to identify possible parties to any proceedings, to enable a party to trace the party’s property or for any other lawful purpose, in the interest of justice 

Rationale – to ensure that you have enough information to sue a party, instead of randomly suing people just so that you get evidence of who has actually wronged you. 
- Because of this, the respondent to this application need not be the actual defendant in the potential suit. But they must have some kind of nexus to the action – must have facilitated the wrongdoing somehow 
- The facilitation of the wrongdoing doesn’t need to be intentional (can be negligent, etc.) 
- This cannot be a complete fishing expedition – there must be a prima facie case of wrongdoing 

Ching Mun Fong v Standard Chartered Bank 
- Purpose of pre-action discovery: 
a. Accommodate the situation where a potential plaintiff did not have sufficient facts to commence proceedings
b. It is NOT meant to obtain evidence to boost the plaintiff’s case as that is achieved through post-action discovery 
c. **Pre-action discovery is unnecessary where potential plaintiff is in a position to commence proceedings

Court must not order production of document if production cannot be compelled in law 
O 11 r 11(2) of ROC 
- The Court must not order a document to be produced if its production cannot be compelled in law
=> E.g. If document is protected by privilege 

Applicant will be liable for reasonable costs arising out of application 
O 11 r 11(3) of ROC
- Non-party is entitled to all reasonable costs arising out of such an application 
* Non-party is "any counterparty who you are ordering pre-action discovery against", doesn’t matter who you eventually sue => TLDR Applicant pays all costs

Procedure for Pre-action production of documents and information
Part 1A: Applicant must establish credible evidence of a Singapore nexus to the alleged 3rd party’s wrongdoing to obtain order of pre-action production of documents against alleged 3rd party 

Part 1B: Alternatively: Applicant can also establish a likely prospect of subsequent proceedings being commenced in Singapore 
ED&F Man Capital Markets Ltd v Straits (Singapore) Pte Ltd 
- Applicant for pre-action disclosure must establish credible evidence of a Singapore nexus to the alleged third-party wrongdoing to obtain an order for pre-action disclosure/must establish a likely prospect of subsequent proceedings being commenced in Singapore 
a. Reason: Pre-action disclosure is an intrusive and compulsive process and Court’s powers do not extend to pre-action disclosure in aid of foreign proceedings 
- However, there will inevitably be situations where post-disclosure might cause another forum to emerge as a more appropriate forum and in that case, applicant for pre-action disclosure is free to commence proceedings there but he would not be permitted to use the documents and information obtained in the course of the pre-action disclosure application 

Part 2: Applicant must satisfy 2 requirements for Court to order pre-action production of documents 
Ching Mun Fong v Standard Chartered Bank 
- Requirements for pre-action discovery:
1. Requirement of relevance: Documents under pre-action discovery must be relevant 
2. Requirement of necessity: Pre-action discovery must be necessary 
3. Reasonable prima facie case of wrongdoing by intended Df – purpose of pre-action production is not to strengthen your case, but to get information to make out your case

Requirement of relevance
Ching Mun Fong v Standard Chartered Bank 
- Pre-action production of documents to enable applicant to have sufficient facts to commence proceedings

Dorsey James Michael v World Sport Group Pte Ltd
- Test of relevance is a threshold test 

Requirement of necessity 
Dorsey James Michael v World Sport Group Pte Ltd 
- Respondent to the application must have facilitated the wrongdoing (but not necessarily culpable in any way). All he needs to have done is facilitated the wrongdoing which may be done innocently, e.g. bank transferring funds from Pf’s account to Df’s account – so the bank has the information and documents and facilitated the transfer of funds
- Test for necessity: Are the pre-action interrogatories applied for necessary to ascertain if his cause of action is viable?
a. Proportionality,
b. Availability of alternative avenues to obtain information 
c. How intrusive the interrogatories are especially where there are serious allegations of wrongdoing that attract public interest
d. Whether an applicant has a complete cause of action against an identified party, then pre-action disclosure is not necessary  

Ching Mun Fong v Standard Chartered Bank
- Pre-action production of documents is necessary if the documents, from their inception, are within the exclusive possession of the respondent
a. If potential plaintiff knew the contents of the documents, then pre-action discovery is unnecessary => Potential plaintiff would be entitled to discovery of the documents after commencing proceedings

Part 3: Court ought to question whether it is just for the Applicant to secure information sought even before commencement of proceedings 
Dorsey James Michael v World Sport Group Pte Ltd
- Final analysis: Court ought to question whether it is just for the applicant to secure the information sought even before commencement of proceedings
a. Applicant must show circumstances such that the case differs from the normal
b. Court must ensure that the application is not employed as an instrument to embarrass or oppress others

- Principles:
a. Court should only make an order if it has been provided with sufficient information to adequately address the necessity of the disclosure 
b. It is easier for Court to ascertain relevance if claimant is able to put a clear cause of action before the Court
c. If proceedings can be commenced against an already identifiable party, reasons ought to be given why it is neither convenient nor just for the information to be sought only after commencement of proceedings 



*MODES OF COMMENCING CIVIL PROCEEDINGS IN THE GENERAL DIVISION OF THE HIGH COURT
What’s the difference between Originating Claim and Originating Application?
1. Originating Claim (formerly Writ of Summons): Generally used for cases where material facts are in dispute 
o Accompanied by statement of claim, OR
o With a general endorsement – special case where the limitation period for the cause of action will expire within 14 days after the OC is issued, then a concise description of the claim is sufficient (but a statement of claim will still need to be filed eventually)
o For claim for personal injury, need to include an annex with the medical report and statement of special damages to the OC

2. Originating Application (formerly Originating Summons): Generally used for cases where material facts are not in dispute and the primary issue is a question of construction of written law, instrument or document or a question of law
o With or without notice 
o Supporting affidavit 

There are some kinds of actions that may only be commenced by certain kind of application – specified in the rules for these specific situations 
- E.g. setting aside of arbitral award or winding up application – must be OA 

How do I know whether there are material facts in dispute? 
- No need to ask the other party 
- Use your own judgment as the client’s lawyer – when you send letter of demand, can assess the counterparty’s response to see whether there are material facts in dispute 
o If the counterparty responds to an OA with lots of factual disputes, there is a procedure to convert the OA to an OC. 


Issue: Advising client to commence civil proceedings: whether to commence civil proceedings by Originating Claim or Originating Application? 
Originating Claim: Where material facts are in dispute 
O 6 r 1(2)
- Claimant must commence proceedings by an OC where the material facts are in dispute

Originating Application: (1) Where ROC/Written law requires (2) Proceedings concern application to Court (3) Proceedings concern construction of written law, instrument, document + Material facts not in dispute
O 6 r 1(3)
- Claimant must commence proceedings by an OA where: 
a. ROC or any written law requires it; or 
b. Proceedings concern an application made to the Court under any written law; or 
c. Proceedings concern solely or primarily the construction of any written law, instrument, document or some question of law and the material facts are not in dispute 

Court can order for OA to be converted into OC if there are disputes of fact in OA supporting affidavit 
O 15 r 7(6)(c)
- Where Court is of the view that there are disputes of facts in the OA’s affidavit, Court may order the OA to be converted into an OC with the necessary directions 
*Originating Claim (there is always a counterparty)
Overview: 
1. Claimant prepares OC with correct form endorsement, annexure
2. Claimant gets the OC issued 
3. Claimant takes note of time period where OC is valid for service (maximum time for service) 
4. Either:
a. Claimant serves OC in Singapore on Df; or 
b. Claimant serves OC out of Singapore on Df 
5. Claimant serves Statement of Claim (SOC) on Df
6. Df serves Notice of Intention to Contest or Not Contest on Claimant
7. Either/or:
a. Df serves Defense on Claimant; and/or
b. Df files and serves counterclaim on Claimant
8. Claimant files and serves Defence to Counterclaim on Df 
9. Parties must not file and serve any Further Pleadings unless Court has granted approval 

Step 1: Claimant prepares OC with correct form endorsement, annexure 
Aka Form, Endorsements and Annexures 
Form
O 6 r 5(1)
- An OC must be in Form 8 (from Appendix A to the Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021)

Endorsements
Option 1: OC endorsed with Statement of Claim in Form 9 
Option 2: If limitation period for cause of action will expire within 14 days after OC is issued => OC can be endorsed with concise description of the claim 
O 6 r 5(2)
- An OC may be endorsed generally with: 
a. A general endorsement with a concise description of the claim; or 
b. A statement of claim in Form 9 (from Appendix A to the Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021)

Statement of claim – concise summary of material facts 
General endorsement – brief summary of the claim with just the cause of action 

O 6 r 5(4)
- Generally, OC must be accompanied with Statement of Claim 
- But if the limitation period for the cause of action will expire within 14 days after the OC is issued then OC can be endorsed generally with a concise description of the claim 
- Court can also allow general endorsement in special cases – this is a case-by-case basis 

Even in a generally endorsed OC, you eventually need to file a SOC. The issue here is just whether the SOC must be attached to the OC. 

Annexures 
For claims for personal injury – Annex medical report and statement of special damages to OC
O 6 r 5(2)
- For claims for personal injuries, claimant must annex a medical report and a statement of special damages claimed to the OC 

NB: for admiralty proceedings, different rules apply (Order 33 Rule 2 ROC 2021)
* An action in rem must be begun by originating claim; and the originating claim must be in Form 48.
* The originating claim by which an Admiralty action in personam is begun must be in Form 8 and the words “admiralty action in personam” must be inserted above the space for the number of the originating claim.

Step 2: Claimant gets the OC issued (same for OA)
OC is deemed issued when Registrar numbers, signs, seals and dates it 
O 6 r 2
- An OC or OA is deemed issued when the Registrar numbers, signs, seals and dates it

Step 3: Claimant takes note of time period where OC is valid for service (maximum time for service) (same for OA)
OC valid for service for 3 months beginning with date of issue
O 6 r 3(1)
- An OC or OA is valid for service for 3 months beginning with the date of its issue 

Procedure to extend validity of OC for service 
Applicant can make application to extend validity of OC for service
O 6 r 3(2)
- An application may be made to extend the validity of the OC or OA for service if it has not been served on all or any of the defendants before or after it expires 

Court may extend validity only twice and by not more than 3 months each time
O 6 r 3(4) 
- Except in a special case, Court may extend validity of OC or OA only twice and by not more than 3 months each time 

Extension period to start the following day after previous deadline 
O 6 r 3(3) 
- Court may order extension of the validity of the OC or OA for service by a period beginning with the day next following that on which the OC or OA would otherwise expire

Principles when Court deciding whether to grant extension 
Lim Hong Kan & Ors v Mohd Sanudin bin Ahmad 
- Court’s discretion to extend the validity of any writ should be exercised only for good reason: 
a. Balance of hardship between parties could be a relevant matter
b. Lower Court decisions on extension should not be interfered with by the appellate court except on special grounds

- Court allowed for extension in this case as:
a. The plaintiff was a menial laborer who had already been ordered to pay costs in a different suit and the plaintiff wanted to save legal costs 
b. The second action would be unnecessary if the plaintiff succeeds in his first appeal and the date of the first appeal is fixed outside of the validity period of the writ of the second action 

The Lircay 
- Good reasons to grant extension: 
a. Where there was an express or implied agreement between parties to defer service of writ 
b. Where delay concerning the application to extend the validity of the writ was induced or contributed by the defendant
c. Difficulty in effective service especially if defendant was evading service 

Step 4A: Claimant serves OC in Singapore on Defendant (within 14 days of OC being issued)
General rule: Personal service is required
O 6 r 4 
- OC and OA must be served personally on each defendant subject to the provisions of any written law and the ROC 

Personal service method
- See below

If personal service not possible, Applicant can apply to Court to request for substituted service
- See below 

Time of service: Reasonable steps must be made to serve OC within 14 days after OC is issued
O 6 r 5(5)
- If OC is to be served in Singapore => Reasonable steps to serve on the defendant must be made as soon as possible and, in any event, within 14 days after the OC is issued 
* Note: If service out of Singapore, then 28 days (O 6 r 5(7))

Special Scenario: If Claimant attends first case conference but has not served OC on Defendant
O 9 r 5 
- If claimant attends first case conference but has not served the claimant’s OC or OA on the defendant, the Court may:
a. Court may dismiss Claimant’s action if Claimant had not taken reasonable steps to effect service expeditiously; OR
b. Court may fix second case conference and order Claimant to:
(i) Serve the originating process within 14 days after date of first case conference; OR 
(ii) Apply for substituted service within that time 

- Court may extend validity of OC if necessary: And extend the validity of the originating process if necessary 

- If Claimant fails to serve OC by second case conference – Court may dismiss Claimant’s action: If claimant fails to serve the originating process by the second case conference, the Court may dismiss the action 

Step 4B: Claimant serves OC out of Singapore on Defendant (within 28 days of OC being issued, must apply to court)
*4B(1): Personal service is required
O 6 r 4 
- OC and OA must be served personally on each defendant subject to the provisions of any written law and the ROC 

Personal service method
- See below

If personal service not possible, Applicant can apply to Court to request for substituted service
- See below 

*4B(2): Time of service – Reasonable steps must made to serve OC within 28 days after OC is issued 
O 6 r 5(7)
- If OC is to be served outside Singapore => Reasonable steps to serve on the defendant must be made as soon as possible and, in any event, within 28 days after the OC is issued 

*4B(3): Exceptions where service out of Singapore does not require court approval  
O 8 r 1(3)
- Court’s approval is not required if service out of Singapore is allowed under a contract between parties 

O 8 r 1(4)
- If Court’s approval has been granted for service of originating process out of Singapore then Court’s approval is not required for service of other court documents 

*4B(4): Claimant has to apply to Court for approval to serve out of jurisdiction 
Claimant has to show that Singapore Court has jurisdiction and is the appropriate Court to hear the action
O 8 r 1(1)
- For claimant to serve originating process or other court document out of Singapore, claimant has to show that the Court has the jurisdiction or is the appropriate Court to hear the action 

Claimant must apply to the Court by summons without notice (Form 2) and supported by affidavit + Affidavit must include O 8 r 1(2) contents
O 8 r 1(2) 
- To obtain the Court’s approval, claimant must apply to the Court by summons without notice and supported by affidavit which must state: 
a. Why the Court has the jurisdiction or is the appropriate court to hear the action
b. In which country or place the defendant is, or probably may be found; and 
c. Whether the validity of the originating process needs to be extended

How to show that the Court is the appropriate court to hear the action? 
Para 63(2) of Supreme Court PD 2021
- To show why Court is the appropriate court to hear the action, claimant must include in the affidavit and show: 
a. Show good arguable cause that there is a sufficient nexus to Singapore
b. Singapore is forum conveniens; and 
c. There is a serious question to be tried on the merits.

For (a): How to show that there is a good arguable case that there is sufficient nexus to Singapore?
Para 63(3) of Supreme Court PD 2021
- To show that there is a good arguable case that there is a sufficient nexus to Singapore, the claimant should rely on the following factors: 
a. Domicile, ordinarily resident, carrying on business, has property in Singapore: Relief is sought against a person who is domiciled, ordinarily resident or carrying on business in Singapore, or who has property in Singapore 
b. Injunction in Singapore: Injunction is sought ordering the defendant to do or refrain from doing anything in Singapore (whether or not damages are also claimed in respect of a failure to do or the doing of that thing) 
c. Another defendant in Singapore: Claim is brought against a person duly served in or outside Singapore and a person outside Singapore is a necessary or proper party to the claim 
d. Contract: Claim is brought to enforce, rescind, dissolve, annul or otherwise affect a contract to recover damages or obtain other relief in respect of the breach of a contract being a contract which:
(i) Was made in Singapore or was made as a result of an essential step being taken in Singapore 
(ii) Was made by or through an agent trading or residing in Singapore on behalf of a principal trading or residing out of Singapore
(iii) Is by its terms, or by implication, governed by the law of Singapore; OR 
(iv) Contains a term to the effect that that Court will have jurisdiction to hear and determine any action in respect of the contract 
e. Breach of contract: Claim is brought in respect of a breach committed in Singapore of a contract made in or outside Singapore and irrespective of the fact, if such be the case, that the breach was preceded or accompanied by a breach committed outside Singapore that rendered impossible the performance of so much of the contract as ought to have been performed in Singapore
f. Tort: Claim:
(i) Is founded on a tort, wherever committed, which is constituted, at least in part, by an act or omission occurring in Singapore; Or
(ii) Is wholly or partly founded on, or is for the recovery of damages in respect of, damage suffered in Singapore caused by a tortious act or omission wherever occurring
g. Immovable property in Singapore: The whole subject matter is immovable property situated in Singapore (with or without rents or profits) or the perpetuation of testimony relating to immovable property so situated;
h. Liability affecting immovable property in Singapore: Claim is brought to construe, rectify, set aside or enforce an act, deed, will, contract, obligation or liability affecting immovable property situated in Singapore; 
i. Debt secured on immovable property in Singapore: Claim is made for a debt secured on immovable property situated in Singapore, or is made to assert, declare or determine proprietary or possessory rights, or rights of security, in or over movable property, or to obtain authority to dispose of movable property, situated in Singapore;
j. Trusts executed according to Singapore law: Claim is brought to execute the trusts of a written instrument, being trusts that ought to be executed according to the law of Singapore and of which the person to be served with the originating process is a trustee, or for any relief or remedy which might be obtained in any such action;
k. Administration of estate of person domiciled in Singapore: Claim is made for the administration of the estate of a person who died domiciled in Singapore or for any relief or remedy which might be obtained in any such action; 
l. Administration action: Claim is brought in an administration action within the meaning of Order 32 of the Rules of Court 2021;
m. Claim is brought to enforce any judgment or arbitral award, or any adjudication determination within the meaning of the Building and Construction Industry Security of Payment Act; 
n. Claim is made under the Corruption, Drug Trafficking and Other Serious Crimes (Confiscation of Benefits) Act, the Terrorism (Suppression of Financing) Act or any other written law; 
o. Restitutionary claim: Claim is a restitutionary one (including a claim for quantum meruit or quantum valebat) or for an account or other relief against the defendant as trustee or fiduciary, and the defendant’s alleged liability arises out of any act done, whether by the defendant or otherwise, in Singapore; 
p. Claim is founded on a cause of action arising in Singapore; 
q. Liability enforceable by proceedings in Singapore: Claim is for a contribution or an indemnity in respect of a liability enforceable by proceedings in Singapore;
r. Submission to Singapore jurisdiction: Claim is in respect of matters in which the defendant has submitted or agreed to submit to the jurisdiction of the Court; 
s. Claim concerns the construction, effect or enforcement of any written law
t. Claim is for a committal order under Order 23 of the Rules of Court 2021.

Duty of full and frank disclosure when drafting affidavit 
Manharlal Trikamdas Mody v Sumikin Bussan International (HK) Ltd 
- Applicant owes a duty of full and frank disclosure to:
a. Place all material facts before Court including facts unfavorable to the applicant’s case so the Court can adjudicate application fairly and justly 
b. Fairly state facts so Court can determine whether case is a proper one for service out of jurisdiction 
c. Include copies of document referred to in the affidavit as exhibits 
- Effect of failure to disclose: Court has discretion to set aside leave of Court to serve out of jurisdiction even if non-disclosure is innocent 

*4B(5): Claimant must identify method of effecting personal service out of jurisdiction 
6 General ways to effect personal service out of Singapore
O 8 r 2
- Service out of jurisdiction may be effected: 
a. According to contractually agreed manner: According to the manner contractually agreed between parties
b. According to Civil Procedure Convention governing service in foreign country: According to the manner provided in a Civil Procedure Convention governing service in the foreign country
c. Through foreign country’s government if government is willing: Through the government of the foreign country if that government is willing to effect service
d. Through foreign judicial authority if they are willing: Through the judicial authority of the foreign country if that authority is willing to effect service
e. Through Singapore consular authority in foreign country: Through a Singapore consular authority in that foreign county
f. According to manner provided by foreign country’s laws: According to the manner provided by law of that foreign country 

Overriding rule: Nothing to be done that is contrary to laws of foreign country 
O 8 r 2(6) 
- Nothing is to be done that is contrary to the laws of the foreign country 

Special Scenario: Where defendant is located in Malaysia or Brunei Darussalam => Claimant can use O 8 r 3 process to serve on Defendant 
O 8 r 3
- Where defendant is located in Malaysia or Brunei Darussalam, service may be effected: 
a. In accordance with Rule 2; or 
b. May be sent by post or otherwise by Registrar to the Magistrate, Registrar or other appropriate officer of any court exercising civil jurisdiction in the area which the person to be served is said to be or to be carrying on business for service on the defendant => The OC is deemed to be duly served if it is returned with an endorsement of service and with an affidavit of such service 

Special Scenario: Where OC is issued by State Courts to be served out of Singapore => Use O 8 r 4 process
O 8 r 4
- If Originating Process is issued by State Courts to be served out of Singapore, it: 
a. Must be sent by Registrar of State Courts to the Registrar of the Supreme Court; and 
b. Must be served in accordance with Rules of Court relating to the service out of Singapore of an originating process issued in the Supreme Court

Special Scenario: Where OC is to be served on a foreign country = Use O 8 r 6 process 
O 8 r 6
- If persons wishes to serve an originating process on a state, he must file in the Registry: 
a. A request for the Ministry of Foreign Affairs to arrange service;
b. A sealed copy of the originating process; and
c. A translation of the originating process in the official language of the State or, if there is more than one official language, in any of those languages which is appropriate for the State to be served, except where the official language or one of the official languages is English.

ALTERNATIVELY: Applicant can apply to Court to request for substituted service out of Singapore 
Janesh s/o Rajkumar v Unknown Person (“CHEFPIERRE”)
- Court has power to allow substituted service out of jurisdiction 

See below for personal service vs substituted service discussion

Singapore-incorporated company with registered office overseas – Leaving document or sending it by registered post to registered office of entity
S387 of Companies Act 
- Document may be served on a company by leaving it at or sending it by registered post to the registered office of the company

Step 5: Claimant serves Statement of Claim (SOC, cf OC) on Defendant
Scenario 1: Where OC is accompanied by SOC => Serve OC and SOC together 
TLDR
- Generally, OC is endorsed by SOC (O 6 r 5(2) => SOC will be served together with the OC 

Scenario 2: Where OC is endorsed with concise description of claim + SOC is filed later on => Serve OC first => then Serve SOC within 14 days after OC has been served 
TLDR
- Even though OC is usually endorsed by SOC => If limitation period for the cause of action will expire within 14 days after the OC is issued, then the OC can be endorsed generally with a concise description of the claim instead of with the SOC (O 6 r 5(4)) 
- => The OC can be served first 
- => The SOC can be served later within 14 days after the OC has been served (O 6 r 5(5))

For service out of jurisdiction: If Court approval was obtained for service of OS out of jurisdiction => Court approval is not required for subsequent service of SOC out of jurisdiction 
O 8 r 1(4)
- If Court’s approval has been granted for service of originating process out of Singapore then Court’s approval is not required for service of other court documents 

Step 6: Defendant serves Notice of Intention to Contest or Not Contest on Claimant
Time to file and serve Notice 
If Defendant served OC in Singapore – Within 14 days after SOC is served on Defendant 
O 6 r 6(1) 
- Defendant who is served an OC in Singapore must file and serve notice of intention to contest or not contest within 14 days after statement of claim is served on the defendant 

If Defendant served OC outside of Singapore – Within 21 days after SOC is served on Defendant 
O 6 r 6(2) 
- Defendant who is served an OC outside Singapore must file and serve notice of intention to contest or not contest within 21 days after statement of claim is served on the defendant 

Form – Form 10 
O 6 r 6(3)
