- Notice of intention to contest or not contest the OC must be in Form 10 of Appendix A to the Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021

Effect of filing the Notice: filing of notice must not be treated as submission to jurisdiction or waiver of improper service of OC 
O 6 r 6(4) 
- Filing and service of notice of intention to contest or not contest is not treated as submission to jurisdiction or waiver of any improper service of OC 

Effect of Defendant NOT filing the Notice within prescribed time 
Claimant can apply for default judgement 
O 6 r 6(5) 
- If defendant fails to file and serve notice of intention to contest within the prescribed time or states in notice that the defendant does not intend to contest all or some of the claims, the claimant may apply for judgement to be given against the defendant 

Claimant must file Application for Default Judgement – Form 11
O 6 r 6(5)
- Claimant must apply for default judgement under Form 11

Claimant must file memorandum of service – Form 12 
O 6 r 6(6)
- Claimant must file a memorandum of service in Form 12 
o Rationale: This is so that the court can consider whether service was done properly and whether sufficient time has passed 

Step 7A: Defendant serves Defense on Claimant 
This is a separate document from the Intention to Contest 

Time to file and serve Defense  
If Defendant served OC in Singapore – Within 21 days after SOC is served on Defendant 
O 6 r 7(1) 
- Defendant who is served an OC in Singapore must file and serve defense to the OC within 21 days after the statement of claim is served on the defendant 

If Defendant served OC outside Singapore – Within 5 weeks days after SOC is served on Defendant 
O 6 r 7(2) 
- Defendant who is served an OC outside of Singapore must file and serve a defense to the OC within 5 weeks after the statement of claim is served on the defendant 

Form – Form 13  
O 6 r 7(3)
- Defense must be in Form 13 

Contents of Defense – Form 13 of Appendix A to the Supreme Court PD 2021

Effect of Defendant NOT filing the Defense within prescribed time 
Claimant can apply for Judgement in Default of Defense  
O 6 r 7(7) 
- If defendant fails to file and serve a defense within the prescribed time, the claimant may apply for judgment in default of defense 
- Claimant must apply for judgement in default of defense in Form 14 

Miscellaneous Issue: Applicable rules if Defendant is only challenging the jurisdiction of the Court
O 6 r 7(4)
- Defendant need not file and serve Defense on merits: If defendant is challenging jurisdiction of the Court then defendant need not file and serve a defense on the merits 
- Defendant needs to file and serve Defense stating grounds which Defendant is challenging jurisdiction of the Court: Defendant only needs to file and serve a defense stating the ground on which the defendant is challenging the jurisdiction of the Court 

Grounds of Challenging Jurisdiction
O 6 r 7(5)
- Challenge to jurisdiction may be for the reason that:
a. Court has no jurisdiction to hear the action; OR 
b. Court should not exercise jurisdiction to hear the action 

Filing of Defense is not treated as submission to jurisdiction 
O 6 r 7(6)
- A Defense filed under O 6 r 7(4) is not treated as a submission to jurisdiction  

Step 7B: Defendant files and serves counterclaim on Claimant 
Defendant must file and serve counterclaim together with the Defense (Both in Form 13)
O 6 r 8 
- If defendant intends to counterclaim against the claimant, defendant must file and serve counterclaim with the defense (Both in Form 13) 
- Defendant must annex medical report and statement of any special damages claimed: If defendant intends to counterclaim for personal injuries, defendant must annex a medical report and a statement of the special damages claimed with the counterclaim 

Step 8: Claimant files and serves Defense to Counterclaim on Defendant
Time to file and serve Defense to Counterclaim 
Within 14 days after Defense and Counterclaim is served on Claimant 
O 6 r 9(1) 
-  If claimant merely wishes to deny assertions in the counterclaim without adding anything material, claimant must file and serve a defense to the counterclaim within 14 days after the defense and counterclaim is served on the claimant 
=> If claimant wishes to add additional material then must file a reply 

Form – Form 13
O 6 r 9(2)
- Claimant’s defense to counterclaim must be in Form 13 

Effect of Claimant not filing the Defense to Counterclaim within prescribed time 
Defendant can apply for Judgement in Default of Defense in respect of the Counterclaim 
O 6 r 9(3)
- If claimant fails to file and serve a defense to a counterclaim within the prescribed time, the defendant may apply for judgement in default of defense in respect of the counterclaim to be given against the claimant 
- Defendant must apply for judgement in default of defense in Form 14 

Step 9: Parties must not file and serve any Further Pleadings unless Court has granted approval 
Context
- Generally: Plaintiff files claim (OC and SOC)=> Defense files defense and counterclaim => Plaintiff files defense to counterclaim then ROC does not contemplate any further pleadings. Need Court’s approval to file further pleadings. 

Parties must seek approval of Court to file Further Pleadings 
O 6 r 10
(1) Parties must seek approval of Court at case conference to file any further pleadings beyond defense and defense to counterclaim, in cases where it is necessary for certain matters to be pleaded 
(2) No further pleadings may be filed unless the Court otherwise orders at the case conference 

Court must not allow Further Pleadings to be filed if they merely deny or repeat assertions without adding anything material 
O 9 r 15
- Court must decide whether it is necessary to file a pleading beyond defense or defense to counterclaim
- Court must not order further pleadings to be filed if they merely deny or repeat assertions in earlier pleadings without adding anything material 

*NOTE: Pleadings – comprise: (1) SOC (by Pf), (2) Defence (by Df), (3) Counterclaim (by Df), (4) Defence to Counterclaim (by Pf). 
- This is important because the claim cannot exceed the bounds of these pleadings 


*Originating Application (simpler than originating claim, may not have a counterparty)
Step 1: Claimant prepares OA in prescribed form and supporting affidavit 
Form 
Option 1: If OA has to be served – Form 15 supported by affidavit
Option 2: If OA need not be served – Form 16 supported by affidavit
- If affects anyone’s rights then OA has to be served 

O 6 r 11(1)
- An OA must be in Form 15 or Form 16 (from Appendix A to the Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021) and must be supported by affidavit 

O 6 r 11(2)
- Form 15 must be used when OA has to be served 

O 6 r 11(3)
- Form 16 must be used when OA need not be served on anyone 

Affidavit: All evidence that is necessary to the claim 
O 6 r 13
- OA must be filed with an affidavit and the affidavit must contain all the evidence that is necessary or material to the claim or to the defense 

Step 2: Claimant gets the OA issued (same as OC, see above)
Step 3: Claimant takes note of time period where OA is valid for service (same as OC, see above)
Step 4A: Claimant serves OA (and affidavit) in Singapore on Defendant
General rule: Personal service is required
O 6 r 4 
- OC and OA must be served personally on each defendant subject to the provisions of any written law and the ROC 

Personal service method
- See below

If personal service not possible, Applicant can apply to Court to request for substituted service
- See below 

Time of service: Reasonable steps must be made to serve OA (and affidavit) within 14 days after OA is issued
O 6 r 11(4)
- If OA is to be served in Singapore => Reasonable steps to serve the OA and affidavit on the defendant must be made as soon as possible and, in any event, within 14 days after the OA is issued 
* Note: If service out of Singapore, then 28 days (O 6 r 11(5))

Special Scenario: If Claimant attends first case conference but has not served OA on Defendant
O 9 r 5 
- If claimant attends first case conference but has not served the claimant’s OC or OA on the defendant, the Court may:
a. Court may dismiss Claimant’s action if Claimant had not taken reasonable steps to effect service: Dismiss the action if it is not satisfied that the claimant has taken reasonable steps to effect service expeditiously; OR
b. Court may fix second case conference and order Claimant to: Fix a second case conference and order the claimant to:
(i) Serve the originating process within 14 days after date of first case conference; OR 
(ii) Apply for substituted service within that time 
- Court may extend validity of OA if necessary: And extend the validity of the originating process if necessary 
- If Claimant fails to serve OA by second case conference – Court may dismiss Claimant’s action: If claimant fails to serve the originating process by the second case conference, the Court may dismiss the action 

Step 4B: Claimant serves OA (and affidavit) out of Singapore on Defendant
4B(1): Personal service is required
O 6 r 4 
- OC and OA must be served personally on each defendant subject to the provisions of any written law and the ROC 

Personal service method
- See below

If personal service not possible, Applicant can apply to Court to request for substituted service
- See below 

4B(2): Take note of time of service – Reasonable steps must made to serve OA within 28 days after OA is issued 
O 6 r 11(5)
- If OA is to be served in Singapore => Reasonable steps to serve the OA and affidavit on the defendant must be made as soon as possible and, in any event, within 28 days after the OA is issued 

4B(3): Exceptions where service out of Singapore does not require court approval  
O 8 r 1(3)
- Court’s approval is not required if service out of Singapore is allowed under a contract between parties 

O 8 r 1(4)
- If Court’s approval has been granted for service of originating process out of Singapore then Court’s approval is not required for service of other court documents 

4B(4): Claimant has to apply to Court for approval to serve out of jurisdiction 
Claimant has to show that Singapore Court has jurisdiction and is the appropriate Court to hear the action
O 8 r 1(1)
- For claimant to serve originating process or other court document out of Singapore, claimant has to show that the Court has the jurisdiction or is the appropriate Court to hear the action (O 8 r 1(1))

Claimant must apply to the Court by summons without notice and supported by affidavit + Affidavit must include O 8 r 1(2) contents
O 8 r 1(2) 
- To obtain the Court’s approval, claimant must apply to the Court by summons without notice and supported by affidavit which must state: 
a. Why the Court has the jurisdiction or is the appropriate court to hear the action
b. In which country or place the defendant is, or probably may be found; and 
c. Whether the validity of the originating process needs to be extended

How to show that the Court is the appropriate court to hear the action? 
Para 63(2) of Supreme Court PD 2021
- To show why Court is the appropriate court to hear the action, claimant must include in the affidavit and show: 
a. Show good arguable cause that there is a sufficient nexus to Singapore;
b. Singapore is forum conveniens; and 
c. There is a serious question to be tried on the merits.

For (a): How to show that there is a good arguable case that there is sufficient nexus to Singapore?
Para 63(3) of Supreme Court PD 2021
- To show that there is a good arguable case that there is a sufficient nexus to Singapore, the claimant should rely on the following factors: 
a. Domicile, ordinarily resident, carrying on business, has property in Singapore: Relief is sought against a person who is domiciled, ordinarily resident or carrying on business in Singapore, or who has property in Singapore 
b. Injunction in Singapore: Injunction is sought ordering the defendant to do or refrain from doing anything in Singapore (whether or not damages are also claimed in respect of a failure to do or the doing of that thing) 
c. Another defendant in Singapore: Claim is brought against a person duly served in or outside Singapore and a person outside Singapore is a necessary or proper party to the claim 
d. Contract: Claim is brought to enforce, rescind, dissolve, annul or otherwise affect a contract to recover damages or obtain other relief in respect of the breach of a contract being a contract which:
(i) Was made in Singapore or was made as a result of an essential step being taken in Singapore 
(ii) Was made by or through an agent trading or residing in Singapore on behalf of a principal trading or residing out of Singapore
(iii) Is by its terms, or by implication, governed by the law of Singapore; OR 
(iv) Contains a term to the effect that that Court will have jurisdiction to hear and determine any action in respect of the contract 
e. Breach of contract: Claim is brought in respect of a breach committed in Singapore of a contract made in or outside Singapore and irrespective of the fact, if such be the case, that the breach was preceded or accompanied by a breach committed outside Singapore that rendered impossible the performance of so much of the contract as ought to have been performed in Singapore
f. Tort: Claim:
(i) Is founded on a tort, wherever committed, which is constituted, at least in part, by an act or omission occurring in Singapore; Or
(ii) Is wholly or partly founded on, or is for the recovery of damages in respect of, damage suffered in Singapore caused by a tortious act or omission wherever occurring
g. Immovable property in Singapore: The whole subject matter is immovable property situated in Singapore (with or without rents or profits) or the perpetuation of testimony relating to immovable property so situated;
h. Liability affecting immovable property in Singapore: Claim is brought to construe, rectify, set aside or enforce an act, deed, will, contract, obligation or liability affecting immovable property situated in Singapore; 
i. Debt secured on immovable property in Singapore: Claim is made for a debt secured on immovable property situated in Singapore, or is made to assert, declare or determine proprietary or possessory rights, or rights of security, in or over movable property, or to obtain authority to dispose of movable property, situated in Singapore;
j. Trusts executed according to Singapore law: Claim is brought to execute the trusts of a written instrument, being trusts that ought to be executed according to the law of Singapore and of which the person to be served with the originating process is a trustee, or for any relief or remedy which might be obtained in any such action;
k. Administration of estate of person domiciled in Singapore: Claim is made for the administration of the estate of a person who died domiciled in Singapore or for any relief or remedy which might be obtained in any such action; 
l. Administration action: Claim is brought in an administration action within the meaning of Order 32 of the Rules of Court 2021;
m. Claim is brought to enforce any judgment or arbitral award, or any adjudication determination within the meaning of the Building and Construction Industry Security of Payment Act; 
n. Claim is made under the Corruption, Drug Trafficking and Other Serious Crimes (Confiscation of Benefits) Act, the Terrorism (Suppression of Financing) Act or any other written law; 
o. Restitutionary claim: Claim is a restitutionary one (including a claim for quantum meruit or quantum valebat) or for an account or other relief against the defendant as trustee or fiduciary, and the defendant’s alleged liability arises out of any act done, whether by the defendant or otherwise, in Singapore; 
p. Claim is founded on a cause of action arising in Singapore; 
q. Liability enforceable by proceedings in Singapore: Claim is for a contribution or an indemnity in respect of a liability enforceable by proceedings in Singapore;
r. Submission to Singapore jurisdiction: Claim is in respect of matters in which the defendant has submitted or agreed to submit to the jurisdiction of the Court; 
s. Claim concerns the construction, effect or enforcement of any written law
t. Claim is for a committal order under Order 23 of the Rules of Court 2021.

Duty of full and frank disclosure when drafting affidavit 
Manharlal Trikamdas Mody v Sumikin Bussan International (HK) Ltd 
- Applicant owes a duty of full and frank disclosure to:
a. Place all material facts before Court including facts unfavorable to the applicant’s case so the Court can adjudicate application fairly and justly 
b. Fairly state facts so Court can determine whether case is a proper one for service out of jurisdiction 
c. Include copies of document referred to in the affidavit as exhibits 
- Effect of failure to disclose: Court has discretion to set aside leave of Court to serve out of jurisdiction even if non-disclosure is innocent 

4B(5): Claimant must identify method of effecting service out of jurisdiction 
6 General ways to effect service out of Singapore
O 8 r 2
- Service out of jurisdiction may be effected: 
a. According to contractually agreed manner: According to the manner contractually agreed between parties
b. According to Civil Procedure Convention governing service in foreign country: According to the manner provided in a Civil Procedure Convention governing service in the foreign country
c. Through foreign country’s government if government is willing: Through the government of the foreign country if that government is willing to effect service
d. Through foreign judicial authority if they are willing: Through the judicial authority of the foreign country if that authority is willing to effect service
e. Through Singapore consular authority in foreign country: Through a Singapore consular authority in that foreign county
f. According to manner provided by foreign country’s laws: According to the manner provided by law of that foreign country 

Overriding rule: Nothing to be done that is contrary to laws of foreign country 
O 8 r 2(6) 
- Nothing is to be done that is contrary to the laws of the foreign country 

Special Scenario: Where defendant is located in Malaysia or Brunei Darussalam => Claimant can use O 8 r 3 process to serve on Defendant 
O 8 r 3
- Where defendant is located in Malaysia or Brunei Darussalam, service may be effected: 
a. In accordance with Rule 2; or 
b. May be sent by post or otherwise by Registrar to the Magistrate, Registrar or other appropriate officer of any court exercising civil jurisdiction in the area which the person to be served is said to be or to be carrying on business for service on the defendant => The OC is deemed to be duly served if it is returned with an endorsement of service and with an affidavit of such service 

Special Scenario: Where OC is issued by State Courts to be served out of Singapore => Use O 8 r 4 process
O 8 r 4
- If Originating Process is issued by State Courts to be served out of Singapore, it: 
a. Must be sent by Registrar of State Courts to the Registrar of the Supreme Court; and 
b. Must be served in accordance with Rules of Court relating to the service out of Singapore of an originating process issued in the Supreme Court

Special Scenario: Where OC is to be served on a foreign country = Use O 8 r 6 process 
O 8 r 6
- If persons wishes to serve an originating process on a state, he must file in the Registry: 
a. A request for the Ministry of Foreign Affairs to arrange service;
b. A sealed copy of the originating process; and
c. A translation of the originating process in the official language of the State or, if there is more than one official language, in any of those languages which is appropriate for the State to be served, except where the official language or one of the official languages is English.

ALTERNATIVELY: Applicant can apply to Court to request for substituted service out of Singapore 
Janesh s/o Rajkumar v Unknown Person (“CHEFPIERRE”)
- Court has power to allow substituted service out of jurisdiction 

See below for personal service vs substituted service discussion

Singapore-incorporated company with registered office overseas – Leaving document or sending it by registered post to registered office of entity
S387 of Companies Act 
- Document may be served on a company by leaving it at or sending it by registered post to the registered office of the company

*Step 5: Defendant serves Defendant’s Affidavit (Reply Affidavit) on Claimant 
Time to file and serve Defendant’s Affidavit / Reply Affidavit  
O 6 r 12(1) 
- Defendant who is served an OA in Singapore must file and serve defendant’s affidavit within 21 days after the OA and affidavit is served on the defendant 

O 6 r 12(2)
- Defendant who is served an OA outside Singapore must file and serve defendant’s affidavit within 5 weeks after the OA and affidavit is served on the defendant 

Contents of Defendant’s Affidavit: All evidence necessary to the Defense (WHERE JURISDICTION IS NOT CHALLENGED)
O 6 r 13 
- Df’s affidavit filed in an OA must contain all evidence that is necessary or material to the defense 

Applicable rules if Defendant is only CHALLENGING THE JURISDICTION OF THE COURT
O 6 r 12(3)
- Defendant need NOT file and serve Defendant’s Affidavit on MERITS: If defendant is challenging jurisdiction of the Court then defendant need not file and serve the defendant’s affidavit on the merits
- But the Defendant’s Affidavit needs to state the grounds which Defendant is challenging jurisdiction of the Court: 

Grounds of Challenging Jurisdiction
O 6 r 12(4)
- Challenge to jurisdiction may be for the reason that:
a. Court has no jurisdiction to hear the action; OR 
b. Court should not exercise jurisdiction to hear the action because it is not the appropriate Court to hear the action 

Filing of Defendant’s Affidavit is not treated as submission to jurisdiction 
O 6 r 12(5)
- A Defendant’s affidavit filed under O 6 r 12(5) is not treated as a submission to jurisdiction (i.e. just because Df filled affidavit does not mean it is agreeing or submitting to the court’s jurisdiction)

*Step 6: Defendant files and serves Counterclaim on Claimant
Defendant must include Counterclaim in Defendant’s Affidavit (cf Defendant’s Defense and Counterclaim which are separate under an originating claim)
O 6 r 14
- If defendant intends to make a counterclaim in the OA against the claimant, defendant must include it in the defendant’s affidavit together with all the evidence that is necessary for the counterclaim

How does the claimant file a defense to the counterclaim? – apply to court under O 6 r 12(6) – court will likely order claimant to file an affidavit that is confined to defending the counterclaim

Step 7: Parties must not file and serve any Further Affidavits except in a special case  
Parties must not file any Further Affidavits except in a special case 
O 6 r 12(6)
- After claimant serve OA + claimant’s affidavit and defendant serve defendant’s affidavit (with the counterclaim), no further affidavits may be filed except in a special case 



*SERVICE (IN GENERAL) 
Process where a party brings a court document for filling to the other party’s notice.

2 different modes of service 
1. Personal service (for originating processes) or Substituted service
2. Ordinary service 

A. Personal service flow
When is personal service required?
Personal service required when expressly required by ROC, Court order, serving party decides to do so voluntarily 
O 7 r 1(1)(a) of ROC
- Personal service is required where:
a. Expressly required by ROC or any written law; or 
b. The Court orders such service; or 
c. The serving party decides to do so voluntarily 
PERSONAL SERVICE IS NORMALLY REQUIRED FOR ORIGINATING PROCESSES (SO WHEN SERVING OC OR OA ON A DEFENDANT)

Who may effect personal service?
O 7 r 2(2)
- Personal service may be effected by: 
a. Process server of the Court 
b. Solicitor 
c. Solicitor’s employee
d. A litigant who is not legally represented or such a person’s employee
e. Any other person that the Registrar may allow in a particular case or generally 

How is personal service to be effected? 
O 7 r 2(1) 
- Personal service is effected
a. Natural person recipient – Leaving document with recipient or recipient’s agent (if recipient is an overseas principal under Rule 4);
b. Entity recipient – Leaving document with entity’s chairman, president, secretary, treasury or other officer;
c. On any person or entity according to the requirements of any written law; or
d. Any manner agreed between server and recipient.
	
Singapore-incorporated company with registered office anywhere – Leaving document or sending it by registered post to registered office of entity
S387 of Companies Act 
- Document may be served on a company by leaving it at or sending it by registered post to the registered office of the company

B. Substituted service flow (Alternative to personal service) 
O 7 r 7 of ROC
Applicant supposed to use personal service => Applicant can apply to Court for order to use substituted service as an alternative when it is impracticable to serve the document personally

Applicant must apply by Form 2 and must show the Court 4 things in the application: 
a. Document is required to be served personally (O 7 r 7(1)(a))
b. Impractical to serve document personally (O 7 r 7(1)(b))
c. 2 reasonable attempts at personal service and affidavit stating why Applicant believes that were reasonable (Para 65 of Supreme Court PD 2021) 
d. Proposed methods of substituted service which will likely bring document to recipient’s notice (Para 65 of Supreme Court PD 2021)

E.g. of methods of substituted service 
Para 65 of Supreme Court PD 2021
1. Substituted service by posting on doors or gates of residential or business premises
2. Substituted service by AR registered post 
- It must contain evidence that the person to be served is resident or can be located at the property 
- It is deemed to be effected when postal service has delivered document or attempted to deliver document 
3. Substituted service by electronic means (email or Internet transmission) 
- It has to be shown that the email account to which the document will be sent belongs to the person to be served and that it is currently active
4. Substituted service by advertisement
- Advertisement in Straits Times if person to be served is literate in English
- Advertisement in one of the main non-English language newspapers if person to be served has unknown language literacy 
- This is last resort and evidence that the person to be served is literate in language of the newspaper must be shown 

How is substituted service to be effected?
Court may order any method of substituted service effective to bring document to notice of recipient
O 7 r 7(2) 
- Court may order any method of substituted service that is effective in bringing document to the notice of the person to be served, including use of electronic means 

Time of substituted service
Substituted service must be effected within 14 days after Court order allowing substituted service
O 7 r 7(3)
- Substituted service is to be effected within 14 days after order of court 

C. Ordinary service (6 methods)
When is ordinary service required? 
- When ROC requires parties to serve a document but does not expressly require personal service 
Summons, affidavits, pleadings, list of documents – can be done by ordinary service

How is ordinary service to be effected? 6 specified modes
O 7 r 3 of ROC
- Ordinary service may be effected: 
a. Leaving or posting document at:
(i) Natural person recipient – Usual or last known address OR Business address of person’s solicitor; or 
(ii) Entity recipient – Entity’s registered or principal office OR its last known business address or address of entity’s solicitor
b. Email to email address provided by recipient: By electronic mail at the electronic mail address provided by the party to be served 
c. Fax if certain conditions: By fax but only if: 
(i) Both the serving party and party to be served act by solicitor; and 
(ii) Solicitor acting for the party to be served indicates to the solicitor acting for the serving party that the first-mentioned solicitor is willing to accept service at a specified fax number and the document is transmitted to that number
d. Any manner agreed between parties: In any manner agreed between parties
e. Any manner directed by Court: In any manner which Court may direct, including use of electronic means
f. Any manner provided under law: In any manner provided under any written law

Time of service and deemed effect 
O 7 r 8 of ROC 
- Service before 5pm – Service deemed effected on same day: When service is effected before 5pm on any particular day, service is deemed to have been effected on that day 
- Service after 5pm – Service deemed effected on following day: When service is effected after 5pm on any particular day, service is deemed to have been effected on the following day 
Service of originating claim or originating application
Personal service is required, subject to written law and ROC (O 6 r 4 of ROC)

There is also a requirement to take reasonable steps to effect service as soon as possible and in any event by no later than: 
(i) 14 days after issuance (for service within jurisdiction), or
(ii) 28 days after issuance (for service out of jurisdiction – outside Singapore)

For originating claim: see Order 6 Rule 5(6) and Rule 5(7) of the ROC 2021.
For originating application: see Order 6 Rule 11(4) and Rule 11(5) of the ROC 2021.

Consequences of failing to serve expeditiously: see Order 9 Rule 5 of the ROC 2021 for the directions that may be made at the first case conference if service is not effected, read with Para 64 of the Supreme Court PD 2021

Note – Service out of jurisdiction is covered under the respective sections on Originating Claim and Originating Application  



Pre-Trial Processes 
MISC. 
Preparations before First Case Conference 
1. Filing Pre-Case Conference Questionnaire 
2. Filing SAPT Checklist 

At First Case Conference 
- Court will:
1. Deal with challenges to jurisdiction 
2. Decide on any arguments on exchange of AEIC before production of documents
3. Give directions for filing of SAPT application and affidavit + filing of Reply and affidavit => Set time for SAPT hearing 
4. Order Production of Documents under Order 11 => Production of Documents done 14 days after First Case Conference 

Parties required to file Pre-Case Conference Questionnaire before case conference
- Parties have to file PCQ before first case conference => Court will refer to matters in PCQ during case conference

Purpose of PCQ
Para 56 of Supreme Court PD 2021 
- Facilitate Court’s discussion with parties at case conference on various issues, timelines and milestones of the case 
- Registrar will discuss the PCQ with the parties at the first case conference 

PCQ – Form B6 of Appendix B of Supreme Court PD
Para 56 of Supreme Court PD 2021 

How to file PCQ
Para 56 of Supreme Court PD 2021 
- Parties must fill up PCQ 
- Parties must exchange PCQ and discuss their agreed positions on matters listed in the PCQ 
- Each party must then file their own PCQ 

PCQ must be filed 1 week before first case conference
Para 56 of Supreme Court PD 2021 
- Each party must file 1 week before first case conference 

Parties required to file SAPT Checklist before case conference
2 weeks before first case conference => Court will direct parties to complete the SAPT Checklist => SAPT Checklist must be filed at least 1 week before first case conference 
Para 56(12) of Supreme Court PD 2021 
- 2 weeks before date of first case conference, Court may direct parties to complete the SAPT Checklist 

SAPT Checklist – Form 8 of Appendix 8 of Supreme Court PD 
Para 56(12) of Supreme Court PD 2021
- SAPT Checklist can be found in Form 8 of Appendix B of Supreme Court PD:
* Intended for parties to indicate their preferred sequence of hearing SAPT matters 

How to file SAPT Checklist 
Para 56(13) of Supreme Court PD 2021 
- Parties must fill SAPT Checklist in the following manner
a. Applicant must complete SAPT checklist and serve it on respondent 
b. Parties must confer on applicant’s proposed sequence to hear SAPT matters 
c. If parties agree on applicant’s proposed sequence to hear SAPT matters then respondent must indicate in the applicant’s SAPT checklist
d. If respondent does not agree, applicant must give reasons for their position and respondent must propose his preferred sequence to hear SAPT matters and must give reasons 

SAPT Checklist must be filed 1 week before first case conference 
Para 56(14) of Supreme Court PD 2021
- Respondent must file the completed SAPT checklist at least 1 week before date of case conference

*A. CASE CONFERENCES
Prior to first case conference, have to do a pre-case conference questionnaire 
- Is quite comprehensive. Rationale is to force parties to consider the case as a whole 

Purpose of case conference 
Allows the court to get a big picture of what the case is about. 
O 9 r 2 of ROC
- At case conference, Court is to: 
* Take control of proceedings and set timelines
* Give directions for proceedings 

When will first case conference be held? 
O 9 r 1(1)
- Case conference is to be held:
a. If Defendant is served in Singapore – 8 Weeks after OC or OA is issued: OR 
b. If Defendant is served outside Singapore – 12 Weeks after OC or OA is issued  

On Court’s own accord or on Parties’ request => Court may hold an earlier or later case conference (cf the guidelines)
O 9 r 1(2)
- Court may hold a case conference earlier or later than the time stated in O 9 r 1(1) at the request of any party or on its own accord 
- CC may be held at any time and as often as the Court thinks appropriate (O 9 r 1(3) ROC)

Matters relating to conduct of first case conference 
Certain communications made in case conference cannot be disclosed to Court during trial (to facilitate frank discussion during CC)
O 9 r 3 
- Subject to law governing admissibility of evidence at trial, any communication made during case conference must not be disclosed to Court during trial if such communication: 
a. Communications stated by any of the parties to be “confidential” or “without prejudice”; OR 
b. Communications marked by Registrar or Judge as “confidential” or “without prejudice” 
=> O 9 r 1(4): As a general rule, Registrar conducts case conference but Registrar may refer matter to Judge 

*Orders made if either party absent at case conference 
If both parties or Claimant absent: Court may dismiss action 
O 9 r 4(1)
- If both parties absent or if only claimant absent: Court may dismiss the action 

If only Defendant absent: Court may grant judgement to Claimant upon proof of service of OA/OC on Defendant 
O 9 r 4(2)
- If only defendant is absent: Court may give judgement for the claimant upon proof of service of OC or OA on the defendant 

If absent party gives valid reasons for absence => Court can set aside dismissal order or default judgement 
O 9 r 4(3)
- Court can set aside or vary dismissal or default judgement if there is proof that there were valid reasons for the absence of the defaulting party 

Parties attend first case conference before serving documents 
Situation where claimant has not served OC/OA but attends first case conference 
O 9 r 5 
- Refer above to Step 4A for consequences of not serving OC/OA before first CC

Situation where defendant has not served defence in OC but attends first case conference
O 9 r 6
If both the claimant and the defendant in an originating claim attend the case conference and no defence is served by the first case conference when the time for filing and serving a defence has expired, the Court may enter judgment against the defendant in default of a defence.
- The Court may set aside or vary such a default judgment.
- The Court may, in a special case, extend time for the defendant to serve the defence after the first case conference, failing which the defendant is deemed not to have served a defence and the claimant is entitled to judgment under paragraph (1).
- This Rule applies, with the necessary modifications, to a claimant’s default to serve a defence to the defendant’s counterclaim.


Challenges to Jurisdiction (in Df’s Defense (in OC) or Df’s Affidavit (in OA))
Court must first deal with objection to jurisdiction at the first case conference 
O 9 r 7(1)
- If both claimant and defendant attend the case conference and:
a. A defense has been filed and served in the case of an OC; or 
b. A defense affidavit has been filed and served in the case of an OA
the Court must first deal with any objection to its jurisdiction 

If Defendant challenges jurisdiction of the Court => Court must make certain directions 
O 9 r 7(2)
- If defendant challenges the jurisdiction of the Court, the Court must: 
a. Direct defendant to file and serve application and supporting affidavit within 14 days after first case conference
b. Direct claimant to file and serve affidavit in reply within 14 days thereafter
=> No further affidavits can then be filed without Court’s approval 
c. Hearing of application will be fixed within 14 days after all affidavits have been filed and served, unless Court otherwise orders

*Court may order exchange of AEICs before exchange of documents (in OC)
If 2 conditions satisfied => Court has discretion to order exchange of Affidavits of Evidence-in-Chief (AEICs) before exchange of documents
O 9 r 8(1)
- The Court may, in any particular case, order parties to file and serve their lists of witness and some or all of the witnesses’ AEICs before any exchange of documents simultaneously (e.g. swap AEICs at the same time) or in any sequence (e.g. Pf files AEIC first then sometime later Df files his AEIC): 
* Condition 1: If application to challenge jurisdiction of Court has been dealt with/there is no challenge to jurisdiction of Court; and 
* Condition 2: After pleadings have been filed and served 
Notes:
* Simultaneously: Ensures that neither party will have any tactical advantage
* Sequentially: Appropriate where it is necessary for a party to first review the perimeters of the opposing party’s evidence before responding

Note: Question 5a of Form B6 of Appendix B to Supreme Court PD PCQ requires parties to indicate whether their case is an appropriate case for AEICs to be filled and served before exchange of documents 

Filing of Documents (first) => AEIC AEIC (first) => Filing of DocumentsIf case is document heavy 

Where there is an inherent lack of balance in parties’ access to documents due to the pleaded causes of action: 
- E.g. in conspiracy claim, Claimant may argue that he is unable to properly present his case before seeing the relevant documents in the hands of the conspirators 
If there are many factual contentions then AEICs are needed to clarify the key issues 
Edge cases 
Oral EIC / Dispensing with requirement of AEIC 
- If witness is uncooperative – can apply for the requirement of the AEIC of this witness to be dispensed with. Instead, can subpoena the witness to compel him to take the stand. 
o Ordinarily, the court will require that a list of questions be sent to the counterparty or even the witness himself, for fairness 

Illiterate witnesses 
- AEIC still required – O 15 r something 
- AEIC read out to witness in the presence of Commissioner for Oaths, then witness agrees and signs 

Document Production (in OC)
Directions for document production will be given by the court at case conference. 

Parties to exchange list and copies of: 
- Documents relied on 
- Known adverse documents 
o Documents that a party knows or ought to know exist, that are adverse to that party’s case 
- Any other documents agreed/ordered 

There is a continuing duty to produce both adverse documents and documents intended to be relied on.



*B. [IN OC] SINGLE APPLICATION PENDING TRIAL (SAPT)
This is an omnibus / umbrella application that is supposed to contain all interlocutory applications including security for costs, further and better particulars, amendment of pleadings, consolidation, judgement on admissions, preliminary questions, etc. 
- With some exceptions, you will need permission to file other applications outside of SAPT 
- Cannot take out any interlocutory application within 14 days of trial unless there is a special case and with the trial judge’s permission 
o No cases explaining what special cases might be, but probably must explain why the application is brought so late and why it is necessary 
- Procedure – 
o Court will instruct parties to take directions for SAPT at the next case conference 
o When directions are given to prepare for SAPT, parties are to prepare the SAPT checklist – para 56 of PD 
* E.g. if a party wishes to file a few applications, the party must look for these applications in the SAPT checklist, and propose a sequence for these applications to be heard. 
* The other party will then insert their comments on the sequence, on the same checklist, in a different color. 
* This is then filed in court and the court will consider the parties’ positions and decide on a hearing order in the RCC on SAPT 
* Where both parties make SAPT, court will decide on both. 

Purpose of SAPT
O 9 r 9(1)
- To allow the Court to consider all matters necessary to bring the proceedings to conclusion in accordance with the Ideals
Timing – when will the court give directions for the filing of a SAPT?
Happens after AEICs or after exchange of documents 
- Depending on whether exchange of documents or AEIC goes first 
- Either: 
(a) after AEICs but before documents are exchanged, if the court orders AEICs before documents (so AEIC-SAPT-docs); or 
(b) after exchange of documents, if the court decides not to order AEICs before documents (so docs-SAPT-AEIC).

What goes into the SAPT?
SAPT to be supported by affidavit or reply affidavit 
O 9 r 9(3)
- Needs to contain all matters necessary for the case to proceed expeditiously

O 9 r 9(4)
- Non-exhaustive list of things the SAPT would include:
a) addition or removal of parties;
b) consolidation of actions;
c) division of issues at trial to be heard separately;
d) security for costs;
e) further and better particulars of pleadings;
f) amendment of pleadings;
g) filing of further pleadings;
h) striking out of part of an action or of the defence;
i) judgment on admission of facts;
j) determination of questions of law or construction of documents;
k) production of documents; --> specific discovery
l) interim relief;
m) expert evidence and assessors;
n) independent witness and interested non?parties; and
o) independent counsel.

- Also includes the “carve out” list (need not necessarily be brought as part of SAPT, and no need prior permission from court to apply) in O 9 r 9(7) (see below for more information):
a) an injunction or a search order which may include an application for any other matter if it is incidental to the injunction or search order;
b) substituted service;
c) service out of Singapore;
d) setting aside service of an originating process;
e) judgment in default of a notice of intention to contest or not contest an originating claim;
f) judgment in default of defence;
g) summary judgment;
h) striking out of the whole of an action or defence;
i) stay of the whole action;
j) stay of enforcement of a judgment or order;
k) an enforcement order;
l) permission to appeal;
m) transfer of proceedings under the State Courts Act;
n) setting aside third party proceedings; or
o) permission to make an application for a committal order

SAPT Checklist
- It is not contemplated that every single component of the single application will be heard inside a single hearing. The court will decide the sequence in which the individual component applications are to be heard and decided

- For the court to decide the order, parties are required to file a SAPT checklist for parties to indicate their preferred sequence of the matters in the SAPT, in accordance with Paragraph 56(12) to 56(16) of the Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 read with Form B8 of Appendix B of the Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021

- Directions to complete the SAPT checklist will come at least 2 weeks before the date of the Registrar’s Case Conference (RCC) where the issue of the filing of the SAPT will be discussed (“Relevant RCC”), or within such other period as the court may direct

- After completing columns A, B and C, the applicant serves the SAPT checklist on the respondent, for them to confirm the applicant’s preferred sequence (either agree or do not agree). If respondent does not agree, it must fill its preferred sequence. Both parties must then provide brief reasons on their positions.

- Once SAPT checklist is fully completed, the respondent party must file it at least 1 week before the Relevant RCC. At the relevant RCC, the court will issue directions for the filing of the SAPT and on the sequence of matters to be heard for the SAPT, having regard to what parties have indicated.

AFTER THE SEQUENCE OF MATTERS HAVE BEEN DETERMINED (SAPT checklist is before and separate from the applications within the SAPT)
- Applying party then has to file its SAPT (supported by affidavit) within 21 days after the Relevant RCC. The opposing party then has 21 days to file and serve an affidavit in reply (O 9 r 9(5) ROC 2021).

 


*Applications that can be made outside SAPT platform, without court’s prior permission – O 9 r 9(7) 
No application may be taken out by any party at any time other than as directed at the case conference or with the Court’s approval, except an application for —
a) an injunction or a search order which may include an application for any other matter if it is incidental to the injunction or search order;
b) substituted service;
c) service out of Singapore;
d) setting aside service of an originating process;
e) judgment in default of a notice of intention to contest or not contest an originating claim;
f) judgment in default of defence;
g) summary judgment;
h) striking out of the whole of an action or defence;
i) stay of the whole action;
j) stay of enforcement of a judgment or order;
k) an enforcement order;
l) permission to appeal;
m) transfer of proceedings under the State Courts Act;
n) setting aside third party proceedings; or
o) permission to make an application for a committal order.

This carve-out list is EXHAUSTIVE – so anything else outside this must be brought in a SAPT

--> for these applications, no need to wait for the SAPT process. It can be filed without obtaining prior approval of the court.


**Seeking approval to file applications outside the SAPT that are NOT in the “carve out” list – O 9 r 9(8) and O 9 r 9(9)
- Parties can seek to bring an application that is NOT within the “carve out” list (SINCE THINGS IN THE CARVE OUT LIST CAN JUST BE BROUGHT WITHOUT COURT PERMISSION):
(a) prematurely (before the SAPT process) or 
(b) late (after the SAPT process), 
by a way of a letter to the court using Form B9 (paragraph 66(6) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021)

- The Court may deal with the request summarily or it may fix a Case Conference to deal with the request.

- However, there is a prohibition on filing such applications LATE, within 14 days before commencement of the trial (and for the duration of the trial ending with the court’s decision on the merits), unless (O 9 r 9(10) ROC):
(a) It is a special case, and
(b) It is with the trial judge’s approval (such approval CANNOT be granted by the registrar, it must be from the trial judge) --> such approval must be obtained by letter via Form B9 setting out the essence of the intended application and reasons why there is a special case (O 9 r 9(11) ROC)

Exceptional circumstances where applications may be made outside of SAPT – 
- If there is a sudden change of facts that will affect the content of the pleadings, then it would save time and costs to amend the pleadings first instead of finishing the document exchange and then applying to amend pleadings under SAPT. Because documents depend on the content of the pleadings. 
- Alternatively where certain documents were only disclosed during SAPT – then need to amend pleadings, but this can only be done outside the SAPT process which is already filed – so can apply to court for permission to amend pleadings outside SAPT
- Upon application for production of specific documents in the SAPT and the production of such documents, you discover that there are other documents referred to. Then can file the application for further discovery after the SAPT. 


Addition and removal of parties 
SAPT matter: Addition and removal of parties
O 9 r 9(4)(a) 
- Application for addition and removal of parties is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT

Court power to add or remove parties 
O 9 r 10(1)
- Court may
* Add or remove claimants or defendants 
* Give permission for defendant to issue a 3rd party notice in accordance with O 10 
* Give directions for originating process to be served on any person who may have an interest in the action 

Person seeking to be added can seek a case conference 
Person seeking to be added can serve letter to Court and all parties to seek a case conference
O 9 r 10(2)
- Any person seeking to be added as a party may attend a case conference if the person is aware of it and may seek a case conference by letter addressed to the Court and copied to all the parties 

Effective date of commencement 
Person added as Defendant => Action will be deemed to commence against that person
O 9 r 10(3)
- Where a person is added as a defendant, action will be deemed to be commenced against that person on the date on which the amendment to the action is made 

Consolidation, joint trials, consecutive trials or stays 
- Consolidation: Combine separate individual actions into one and get a single judgement 
- Joint trials: Hear individual actions in one trial  
- Stays: Staying other trials until current trial has finished

SAPT matter: Consolidation, joint trials, consecutive trials or stays
O 9 r 9(4)(b)
- Application for consolidation, joint trials, consecutive trials or stays is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT
Usually a matter for OC, but sometimes OAs will be consolidated too 

Applicant need satisfy any ground for Court to order consolidation, joint trials, consecutive trials or stays 
O 9 r 11
- Court may order 2 or more actions to be consolidated, order them to be tried together, or to be tried consecutively or for any of them to be stayed pending the determination of another if the Court is of the opinion that: 
a. Ground 1: There is some common question of law in the action; OR  
b. Ground 2: Reliefs claimed in the actions concern or arise out of the same factual situation; OR 
c. Ground 3: It is appropriate to do so 
- Factors that the court considers – whether there are time/cost savings, whether parties would be prejudiced etc. 

Different kinds of consolidation under O 9 r 11
- Formal consolidation 
o Where 2 OCs are filed, there will initially be 2 sets of proceedings 
o After consolidation, there will only be a single set of proceedings. One case file, one case number 
- Informal consolidation – Tried together / one after another 
o Usually when there are similarities, e.g. overlaps in evidence or issues. 
o Same case conference, same trial judge
o Same judge will preside over both cases, which may be heard at the same time or sequentially 
o Rationale – to prevent duplication of work (calling witnesses etc.) and discrepancy between findings 
- Stay pending determination of another action 
o Usually in the case where the first case (which is stayed) is dependent on the outcome or findings that must be made in the second case (determinative proceedings)




*C. SECURITY FOR COSTS 
- Give defendant some assurance that his legal fees will be paid for when he is being asked to fight the trial and he wins (i.e. if the claimant loses)
(if part of OC) SAPT matter: Asking Claimant to put up security for Defendant’s costs 
O 9 r 9(4)(d)
- Application by defendant for claimant to put up security for defendant’s cost of action is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT (if OC)
Type of security ordered 
- Usually – payment into court 
- Bank guarantee on terms that are satisfactory to the other party 
- Solicitors’ undertaking – NOTE – as a solicitor, you should NOT give this undertaking unless and until you have received this amount from the client, otherwise you’re personally liable 

References to “claimant” and “defendant” are to be construed as references to persons in these positions (O 9 r 12(6) ROC)
- i.e. in a counterclaim, a defendant who is pursuing the counterclaim stands in the position of the claimant for the purposes of security of costs, and the claimant stands in the position of the defendant. So the claimant (as the “defendant” now) can apply for security of costs, in relation to the counterclaim, from the defendant (the “claimant” now)

Defendant needs to satisfy *2-stage Test for Court to order Claimant to put up security for Defendant’s legal costs 
1. Defendant needs to satisfy one of the 3 grounds (for jurisdiction)
2. Court must exercise discretion to make the order for security 

Stage 1: Defendant needs to satisfy one of the grounds when applying to Court for Claimant to put up security for Defendant’s legal costs 
O 9 r 12(1)
- Defendant may apply for security for the defendant’s costs of the action if:  
a. Ground 1: Claimant is not ordinarily resident in Singapore (so out of jurisdiction); OR  
o Definition of ordinarily resident: Claimant is ordinarily resident in a place or country if he adopts the place voluntarily AND has settled purpose and regular life in the place, whether of short or of long duration (Tjong Very Sumito v Chan Sing En)
o Person may be ordinarily resident in more than one country if that person had voluntarily adopted more than one place as their residence with settled purpose. 
o If person is ordinarily resident in both Singapore and a foreign country => It would be a strong factor under the second-stage against ordering security for costs
o Person may not be ordinarily resident in any country

b. Ground 2: Claimant is a nominal claimant suing for another’s benefit or is being funded by non-party + Reason to believe Claimant will be unable to pay Defendant’s legal costs; OR 
E.g. company in liquidation (company has not assets) and liquidator is in charge, creditor may find that it is in their interest for the liquidator to commence proceedings against a company debtor – so creditor may fund the litigation (so the company in liquidation will be the claimant, but party who will benefit from outcome of proceedings is the creditor)
E.g. insured person claiming under insurance policy, but insured person already assigned proceeds to someone else. Insured is still the contractual party - but benefits is the assignee

c. Ground 3: Claimant not stated or incorrectly stated address in the OC/OA, or changed address during course of proceedings to evade consequences of litigation. --> must be for purpose of evading, merely changing address is insufficient

O 9 r 12(2)
- Ground 4: If Claimant is a corporation – There is credible testimony and reason to believe that Claimant will be unable to pay Defendant’s legal costs:  S388 of Companies Act applies if claimant is a company – There is credible testimony and reason to believe that the claimant-company will be unable to pay the defendant’s legal costs if defendant is successful 

S388 of Companies Act (ordering a security for costs against a company)
- Where the claimant is a corporation, the Court may require the claimant to give sufficient security for the defendants costs and stay all proceedings until the security is given if: 
* It appears by credible testimony that there is reason to believe that the corporation will be unable to pay the costs of the defendant if successful in the defendant’s defense

Stage 2: Court will consider all the circumstances and decide whether it is just to order the Claimant to provide security for the Defendant’s costs, and if so, the extent of security  
Even if the threshold requirements are met in stage 1 above, the court still has discretion to order SFC or not in stage 2

Tjong Very Sumito v Chan Sing En 
- If Claimant is ordinarily resident in Singapore => Less likely to grant order for security 
- If Claimants do not have fixed assets in Singapore which can satisfy a possible order of costs against claimant => More likely to grant order for security
- Where a plaintiff is ordinarily resident in the jurisdiction, this is a strong factor in favour of the court refusing to exercise its discretion to order security for costs. It is in opposition to the usual rule that other matters being equal, security is normally ordered against plaintiffs resident outside of jurisdiction.

Creative Elegance (M) Sdn Bhd v Puay Kim Seng [1999] 1 SLR(R) 112 at [13]–[25]
- Factors leaning against ordering Claimant to put up security for Defendant’s costs: 
a. If claimant has a bona fide claim with a reasonable prospect of success => Less likely to grant order for security 
b. If order for security will stifle the claimant’s case given its prevailing economic condition => Less likely to grant order for security 
c. If easy to enforce payment of defendant’s costs => Less likely to grant order for security 
Other factors – 
- whether the claimant has a propensity to avoid paying costs in the past
- The strength of the case – though the courts won’t delve into the merits of the case at an interlocutory level, if the claimant’s case appears very weak the court may exercise its discretion to order SFC 

* Once the condition under the respective provision is satisfied, the court’s discretion is invoked, and in exercise of that discretion the court decides whether or not to order security for costs against the plaintiff.
* The court considers all the circumstances and decide whether it is just to order the plaintiff to provide security for costs and the extent of such security.
* Factors:
o Whether Pf has a bona fide, genuine claim and a reasonable prospect of success;
o Whether application for security being used oppressively to try and stifle a genuine claim; 
o Whether Pf’s lack of means was brought by any conduct by the Df; an
o Ease of enforcement of the judgment. 



SAPT matter: Asking non-party to put up security for Defendant’s costs 
O 9 r 9(4)(d)
- Application by defendant for non-party to put up security for defendant’s cost of action is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT

Defendant need satisfy one of the grounds for Court to order non-party to put up security for Defendant’s legal costs 
O 9 r 12(3)
- Defendant may apply for security for the defendant’s costs of the action to be provided by a non-party, if:
a. Ground 1: Non-party has assigned right in action to the Claimant in return for share of money recovered in the action: That non-party has assigned his right in action to the claimant in return for a share of any money or property which the claimant may recover in the action; OR 
b. Ground 2: Non-party has contributed to Claimant’s legal costs in return for share of money recovered in the action: That non-party has contributed or agreed to contribute to the claimant’s costs in return for a share of any money or property which the claimant may recover in the action; OR 
c. Ground 3: Non-party has contributed to Claimant’s legal costs and actively instigated Claimant to maintain action: That non-party has contributed or agreed to contribute to the claimant’s costs and actively instigates or encourages the claimant to maintain the claimant’s actions 

Procedure to apply to Court for non-party to put up security for cost 
Defendant must apply by summons and personally serve on non-party and every party to the proceedings 
O 9 r 12(4)
An application for an order under O 9 r 12(3) must be made by summons => Summons must be personally served on the non-party and ordinarily served on every party to the proceedings 




*D. [IN OC] FURTHER AND BETTER PARTICULARS (FBP)
- Application which can be filed to get further particulars about allegations made in the pleadings (i.e. statement of claim, defense, reply, etc.)
- Only applicable to OCs because it relates to pleadings, which OAs don’t have. 
Preliminary: Request for further and better particulars must be first sent by way of letter and an informal inquiry must be made first => If not Court may make costs orders against applicant 

SAPT matter: Asking opposing party to furnish further and better particulars 
O 9 r 9(4)(e)
- Application for further and better particulars is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT

3 Requirement for Court to order any party to furnish FBP of any matter
Requirement 1: The FBP sought are necessary on the facts of the case 
O 9 r 13
- Court may order a party to serve on any other party particulars of any matter stated in the first-mentioned party’s pleading if the Court is of the opinion that the particulars are necessary on the facts of the case 
- This is for the opposing party to know the case it has to meet 

Requirement 2: The FBP sought relates to material facts and not evidence 
Element Six Technologies Ltd v IIA Technologies Pte Ltd
- Pleadings only need to contain material facts (must be pleaded, if not present then can be struck out) and not evidence => Hence, FBP cannot be used to ask for more evidence
- If a claim doesn’t set out all the material facts, the omission is grounds for striking out 
Differentiating between material facts and evidence
- Material facts: Facts that are necessary for the purpose of formulating a complete cause of action so that the opposing party is given fair notice of the case to be met and may direct his evidence to address the case 
- Evidence: Supporting facts or subordinate facts that are the means of proving the material facts 
- Example – False representation in a tenancy agreement – that URA allows F&B business on the premises 
o Material fact that must be pleaded – the fact that there was a representation from A to B that the premises were usable for F&B 
* If you don’t plead this element that there was a representation, the claim is liable to be struck out 
o Particulars of this representation – if you simply assert that there was a representation, the defendant doesn’t know the case he has to meet. The claimant must furnish more particulars, about the date of the representation, whether it was made verbally or in writing --> THESE SHOULD STILL BE PLEADED SO D KNOWS WHAT C IS REFERRING TO
o What is evidence in this case? 
* A copy of the email, a statement of witnesses who will vouch for the fact stated 

Distinction between FBPs and specific discovery
- FBP relates to facts 
- Discovery relates to evidence – e.g. documents that you believe are in possession of the other party 

Requirement 3: FBP should NOT be granted if applicant already knows what the alleged cause of actions and remedies are sought by the opposing party
Sharikat Logistics Pte Ltd v Ong Boon Chuan
- Order for FBP should NOT be granted for the defendant if it already knows what the alleged cause of action and remedies sought by the claimant 
- Reason: Pleadings only need to state material facts and need not contain all the evidence, order for FBP should only be used for particularizing material facts and should not be used to request for evidence 

Procedure for request for further and better particulars 
If more than 5 categories or sub-categories of requests are sought => Parties must complete summary table (Form B10 of Appendix B to Supreme Court PD 2021)
Para 68 of Supreme Court PD 2021
- Where more than 5 categories of further and better particulars are sought, parties must complete the Summary Table instead of filing written submissions 





*E. [IN OC] AMENDMENT OF PLEADINGS 
- Correct errors, add more points, remove points in the pleadings (e.g. statement of claim, reply)
- For use in OC only, part of SAPT 
SAPT matter: Amendment of pleadings  
O 9 r 9(4)(f)
- Application to amend pleadings is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT
**The threshold for amendment of pleadings is usually not very high (especially if amendments are made at early stage and can be compensated by costs), except in 2 situations:
1. Amendments made after expiry of statutory limitation period (O 9 r 14 ROC, see below)
2. Amendments made less than 14 days before trial – no amendments allowed except in special case (O 9 r 14(3) ROC) --> this is a very high threshold

Preliminary Issues
General prohibition against amendment of pleadings 14 days before commencement of trial => Unless special case 
O 9 r 14(3) 
- Court must not allow any pleading to be amended less than 14 days before the commencement of the trial except in a special case 
Court may consider events occurring AFTER the OC is filed 
O 9 r 14(2) 
- In a special case, the Court may consider events that occurred after the originating claim is filed to be pleaded even though they do not relate back to the date of the filing of the originating claim
o No authorities at the moment on what makes a “special case” but will need very good reasons
*Inferences may be drawn if material facts in the pleadings are amended 
O 9 r 14(6)
- If material facts in the pleadings are amended, the Court may draw the appropriate inferences 
- E.g. material facts are important in pleadings and should be verified – if you amend later, it calls into question the certification of truth given previously. So inferences may be made regarding credibility of the party amending

Method 1 of amending: with written agreement between the parties, without prior court permission
2 requirements for amendment of pleadings without Court approval 
O 9 r 14(5)
- Any pleadings can be amended without court approval if: 
* Requirement 1: It is amended by written agreement between parties; AND 
* Requirement 2: It is amended more than 14 days before commencement of trial 
If any of these requirements are not met (no written agreement or if it is less than 14 days before commencement of trial), must seek court permission (i.e. method 2).

Method 2 of amending: with court’s permission
O 9 r 14(1)-(3)
- Court may allow parties to amend pleadings, but a “special case” needs to be shown
- The court has the power to allow amendment to pleadings at any stage, including cases on appeal
Court’s considerations giving approval to any party to amend pleadings
Sheagar s/o T M Veloo v Belfield International (Hong Kong) Ltd 
1. *Whether amendment sought would allow the real issues in proceedings to be determined, thereby ensuring the ends of substantive justice are met --> MAIN THRESHOLD FOR COURT ALLOWING AMENDMENT OF PLEADING
2. Whether amendment would cause injustice or injury to the opposing party which could not be compensated by an order for costs or otherwise => If so, disallow amendment 
3. Whether procedural fairness to the opposing party is maintained 
a. Delay alone would not prevent amendments but may increase prejudice to opposing party 

*Amendment of pleadings after expiry of statutory limitation period 
Part 1: Court must determine whether the amendment would prejudice the opposing party’s limitation defense 
Management Corporation Strata Title Plan No. 3322 v Mer Vue Developments Pte Ltd 
- Court must consider: Whether the amendment effectively allows the claimant to prosecute a claim which would otherwise have been time-barred if it were brought under a new writ 
--> i.e. even though statutory limitation period has expired, if amendment to pleadings do not prejudice whether the opponent can rely on a time bar as a defence (e.g. clerical errors), then court will just consider if it was just to amend pleadings

--> but if amendment to pleadings prejudices the opponent’s ability to rely on the time bar, then need to look at the specific circumstances under O 9 r 14(4)

Part 2A: If it is determined that the amendment would not prejudice the other party’s limitation defense, then the Court would then consider whether it would be just to allow applicant to amend the pleadings under O 9 r 14(1) (Management Corporation Strata Title Plan No. 3322 v Mer Vue Developments Pte Ltd )
1. If it is determined that amendment would prejudice the other party’s limitation defense then Court will consider whether it can allow amendment under O 9 r 14(4) (previously O 20 r 5(2) – 5(5))
- Example: Cause of action is to be distinguished from a relief claimed (Multistar v Geocon Piling) => If a new relief is sought to be introduced, it would never prejudice opposing party’s limitation defense => Go under O 9 r 14(1) 
- Although Mer Vue was decided under the old RoC 2014, it is likely to continue to apply as authority under RoC 2021.

Part 2: If it is determined that the amendment would prejudice the other party’s limitation defense, then the Court can only allow the amendment of pleadings in any of the 3 situations listed in O 9 r 14(4) 
O 9 r 14(4)
- Where the relevant limitation period has expired, the Court may allow amendment in the following circumstances: 
a. Situation 1: Amendment to correct party’s name – If it was a genuine mistake and not misleading as to the identity of the party in question: even if its alleged effect will be to substitute a new party
b. Situation 2: Amendment to alter capacity in which party sues – If party might have sued in that capacity at the time OC was issued or the counterclaim was made
c. Situation 3: Amendment to add or substitute new cause of action – If new cause of action arises out of substantially the same facts as existing cause of action for which relief has already been claimed in the same action


*F. STRIKING OUT (OC / OA)
- Application to strike out claimant’s pleadings (by defendant) and defendant’s pleadings (by claimant)
- If defendant can strike out all of claimant’s pleadings then can also ask for case to be dismissed 
- If claimant can strike out all of defendant’s pleadings then can also ask for judgement to be entered
- If want to strike out part of pleadings => SAPT matter
- If want strike out entire pleadings => Non-SAPT matter (part of “carve out” list) --> so no need court permission
- Can strike out OA as if pleading (based on the 3 grounds below) – not SAPT as it is not applicable for OA 
- Can also apply to strike out affidavits (certain parts or entire affidavit)

SAPT matter – Application to strike out part of action/defense
O 9 r 9(4)(h)
- Application to strike out part of action/defense is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT

Non-SAPT matter – Application to strike out entire action/defense 
O 9 r 9(7)(h)
- Application to strike out entire action/defense is not a matter to be dealt with under SAPT => Implication: Do not need Court’s approval if application is filed outside SAPT 

Applicant must satisfy one of the 3 grounds for striking out part of/whole action/defense 
O 9 r 16(1) 
- Court may order any or part of any pleading to be struck out or amended on the ground that:
a. Ground 1: Pleading discloses no reasonable cause of action or defense; OR  
b. Ground 2: Pleading is an abuse of process of the Court; OR 
c. Ground 3: It is in the interests of justice to do so 
and may order the action to be stayed or dismissed or judgement to be entered accordingly 

General principles for all Grounds --> GROUNDS FOR STRIKING OUT IS A HIGH THRESHOLD
Principle 1: Power to strike out should only be invoked in plain and obvious cases (Gabriel Peter & Partners (suing as a firm) v Wee Chong Jin)

Principle 2: Where application to strike out involves lengthy arguments => No striking out unless Court has doubts as to soundness of pleading and Court is satisfied that striking out reduces burden of preparing for trial (Gabriel Peter & Partners (suing as a firm) v Wee Chong Jin)
- Where application to strike out involves lengthy arguments, the Court should decline to strike out unless:
a. Court has doubts as to the soundness of the pleading; and 
b. Court is satisfied that striking out will obviate necessity for a trial or reduce the burden of preparing for a trial 
Principle 3: Where Claimant’s SOC is defective only in omitting particulars which Defendant is entitled => Defendant should make application for FBP instead of application to strike out SOC 
- Where claimant’s statement of claim is defective only in not containing particulars to which the defendant is entitled => Defendant should make an application for further and better particulars and not an application to strike out the statement (Gabriel Peter & Partners (suing as a firm) v Wee Chong Jin)

Principles for Ground 1
Principle 1: No evidence is admissible in an application to strike out pleadings under Ground 1 – pleading itself must be so deficient
O 9 r 16(2)
- No evidence is admissible on an application under O 9 r 16(1)(a) 
=> Pleadings itself must be so deficient that it is doomed to fail on the law 
Principle 2: A reasonable cause of action connotes a cause of action which has some chance of success when only the allegations in the pleading are considered (Gabriel Peter & Partners (suing as a firm) v Wee Chong Jin)
- As long as statement of claim discloses some cause of action or raises some question fit to be decided at the trial, the mere fact that the case is weak or is not likely to succeed is not a ground for striking out 
- Read only the pleadings => Determine if the Claimant has some chance of success

Example of defective pleadings at law: 
- Tortious claim without loss being proved 
- Claim is time-barred => Can just strike out 

Principles for Ground 2
Principle 1: Abuse of process includes consideration of public policy and interests of justice 

Principle 2: Examples of abuse of process (Gabriel Peter & Partners (suing as a firm) v Wee Chong Jin)
- Where the judicial process is used as a means of vexation and oppression in the process of litigation 
- Where a claim is brought for an ulterior or collateral purpose
- Where there are no facts to support a claim 
- Where an identical question is litigated over and over again 

Principle 3: 4 categories of abuse of process (Chee Siok Chin v Minister for Home Affairs)
- Proceedings which involve a deception on the Court or are fictitious or constitute a mere sham 
- Proceedings where the process of the Court is not being fairly or honestly used but is employed for some ulterior or improper purpose or in an improper way 
- Proceedings which are manifestly groundless or without foundation or which serve no useful purpose
- Multiple or successive proceedings which cause or are likely to cause improper vexation or oppression 

Principles for Ground 3
Principle 1: The court has inherent jurisdiction to prevent injustice, such as when the claim is plainly or obviously unsustainable (Iskandar bin Rahmat and ors v AG)



*G. SUMMARY JUDGMENT
- Summary process which claimant can use to obtain judgement before trial, in clear cases where C can show that there is not really a defence (by D) to his claims
- Can also be used by the defendant to get a quick judgement on counterclaim brought against C (if C does not really have a defence to the counterclaim)
- Only for OC (outside SAPT, so no need court permission) 
- Not useful for OA because OA is already decided on affidavit 
Non-SAPT matter: Claimant’s application for summary judgement against Defendant 
O 9 r 9(7)(g)
- Application for summary judgement is NOT a matter to be dealt with under SAPT (since it is under the “carve out” list) => Implication: Do not need Court’s approval if application is filed outside SAPT 

Timing
Time of application: Claimant can apply for summary judgement after Defendant files and serves defense 
O 9 r 17(1)
- Claimant may apply for summary judgement against any defendant after the defense had been filed and served in an originating claim --> rationale is to give D an opportunity to set out his defense to C’s claims

OUTER LIMIT OF TIMING: General prohibition against application for summary judgement after expiry of 28 days after date of service of defense (by D)/defense to counterclaim (by C)
O 9 r 17(13)
- Application under O 9 r 17 may not be made after the expiry of 28 days after the date of service of defense (by D) or defense to counterclaim (by C, if D brought a counterclaim)

2-Stage Framework that Court will apply in deciding application for summary judgement 
M2B World Asia Pacific Pte Ltd v Matsumura Akihiko, Ritzland Investment Pte Ltd v Grace Management & Consultancy Services Pte Ltd
Stage 1: Claimant has to show that he has a prima facie case for summary judgement => Then move to Stage 2
O 9 r 17(1)
- Claimant must show (prima facie) that the Defendant has no defense to
a. A claim; OR 
b. A particular part of a claim; OR 
c. A claim or part of a claim, except as to the amount of any damages claimed 

Stage 2: Defendant will have to establish that there is fair or reasonable probability that he has a real or bona fide defense 
- Show triable issues/question 
- Show that a trial should be held for some other reason 
- Summary judgement will be granted if Defendant can only provide mere assertions which are equivocal, lacking in precision, inconsistent with undisputed contemporary documents or is inherently improbable in itself

Other Principles 
Principle 1: Summary judgement should not be granted unless it is very clear that there is no defence. (Ling Yew Kong v Teo Vin Li Richard)
- If defence can be described as more than shadowy but less than probable, Defendant should still be granted leave to defend

Principle 2: Summary judgement should not be granted if the case requires Court to determine points of law or construction that may take hours or even days and the citation of many authorities (Ling Yew Kong v Teo Vin Li Richard)
- However, if Defendant’s only defence is a point of law that the Court can see at once that the point is misconceived or plainly unsustainable => Claimant will be entitled to summary judgement 

Procedure for Claimant to apply for summary judgement 
Claimant must file and serve summons (Form 1 or Form 2) and supporting affidavit for summary judgement 

Claimant’s affidavit must contain all evidence necessary or material to the claim
O 9 r 17(2)
- Claimant’s affidavit must contain all evidence that is necessary or material to the claim – *prima facie case must be established in this affidavit --> if prima facie case is made out, burden shifts to defendant to respond by filing reply affidavit

Defendant must file and serve reply to C’s application within 14 days 
O 9 r 17(3)
- If defendant disputes claimant’s application for summary judgement, defendant must:
