* File and serve defendant’s affidavit on the claimant within 14 days after service of the claimant’s application and affidavit 

Defendant’s affidavit must contain all evidence necessary to the defense 
O 9 r 17(4)
- Defendant’s affidavit must contain all evidence that is necessary or material to the defense – *show that D has a fair probability of a defence or that there are triable issues to be determined at trial

Claimant must file and serve reply to D’s reply affidavit within 14 days --> final responsive affidavit by claimant
O 9 r 17(5)
- If claimant disputes anything in the defendant’s affidavit, claimant must: 
* File and serve claimant’s affidavit on the defendant within 14 days after service of the defense’s affidavit 

No further affidavits may be filed without Court’s approval 
O 9 r 17(6)
- No further affidavits may be filed without Court’s approval 

Court’s powers vis-à-vis application for summary judgement
O 9 r 17(7)
- Court may:
a. Dismiss the application for summary judgement (case proceeds to trial); OR 
--> if C is unable to show prima facie case, costs will be made against C

b. Grant permission to defend to the defendant without any conditions (case proceeds to trial); OR 
--> if C shows prima facie case but D’s reply affidavit also has merit, and the court determines more evidence is needed and case should go to trial, costs would depend on who succeeds or fails at the end

c. Grant summary judgement to the claimant (summary judgement granted, no more trial); OR
--> if C has prima facie case and no triable issues or no reasonable defence by D, then summary judgment likely to be granted

d. Grant permission to defend to the defendant with conditions if the defense or any issue raised therein is of a dubious nature (case proceeds to trial)
--> conditional leave to defend: if defense is shadowy/not strong, but slim chance there may be something then, it will allow D to defend at trial but with conditions on D (e.g. payment into court for some form of security to secure the claim amount and ask D to put his money where his mouth is). If D does not comply with conditions, no more trial, ends there and summary judgement is granted against D
Summary judgment is entered where the claimant has a prima facie case and the defendant is unable to satisfactorily answer 

Special Scenario: Court grants Claimant summary judgement but Defendant has counterclaim 
Court can order stay of enforcement of summary judgement (for C’s benefit) until after trial of Defendant’s counterclaim
O 9 r 17(9)
- Court may grant summary judgement but order a stay of enforcement against any defendant until after the trial of the defendant’s counterclaim 

Special Scenario: Defendant is absent at summary judgement hearing
Court may set aside or vary summary judgement granted against absent Defendant 
O 9 r 17(10)
- Court may set aside or vary any summary judgement granted against a defendant who was absent at the hearing 

Summary judgement where D launches counterclaim and C provides defense to counterclaim
Time of application: Defendant can apply for summary judgement against C after Claimant files and serves defense to counterclaim 
O 9 r 17(11)
- Where defendant to an action commenced by OC has served a counterclaim on claimant and claimant has served defense to the counterclaim, the defendant may apply for summary judgement against the claimant on the counterclaim or to a particular part of the counter claim 

Defendant must satisfy that Claimant has no defense to the counterclaim  
O 9 r 17(11)
- Ground: Claimant has no defense to a claim made in the counterclaim or to a particular part of such a claim 

Same procedure as Claimant application for summary judgement against Defendant
O 9 r 17(12) 
- Rules O 9 r 17(2) to O 9 r 17(10) apply with necessary modifications:
* Procedure same 
Difference between striking out defence vs summary judgment 
- Striking out defence – the focus is on the defence (pleading), whether it is legally and factually sustainable 
- In application for summary judgment, there must first be a prima facie case established for the claimant. Only when there is a prima facie case does the defendant need to file a reply affidavit 




*H. DECISION ON QUESTIONS OF LAW OR CONSTRUCTION OF DOCUMENTS 
- Similar to summary judgement in that it is targeted to reach a quick judgement
- Application to Court for Court to decide question of law or construction of documents WITHOUT A FULL TRIAL

SAPT matter – Application to determine questions of law or construction of documents 
O 9 r 9(4)(j)
- Application to determine questions of law or construction of documents is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT

Court’s power to decide question of law or construction of documents 
Court’s own accord or Party’s application => Court can decide questions of law/construction of documents without trial or hearing facts
O 9 r 19(1)
- Upon a party’s application or on the Court’s accord, the Court may decide any question of law or the construction of any document arising in any action without a trial or hearing on the facts, whether or not such decision will fully determine the action 
--> i.e. this question to be decided need not be the ONLY thing that needs to be determined in the case

United Overseas Bank Ltd v Lippo Marina Collection Pte Ltd
- A free-standing question of law can be summarily determined even though such determination does not dispose of the entire action 
- Requirements in UOB still likely to apply (SO STILL REFER TO THE FACTORS BELOW), but in the context of the court’s discretion (no longer as a threshold requirement)

If the court’s decision would fully determine the matter
If O 9 r 19(2) Requirement + 4 UOB Requirements are satisfied => Court can give judgement or dismiss the entire action 
O 9 r 19(2)
- Court may give judgement or dismiss action or make any order that is appropriate where:
* The Court’s decision in O 9 r 19(1) fully determines (subject only to any appeal) the entire matter or any claim or issue therein

United Overseas Bank Ltd v Lippo Marina Collection Pte Ltd 
- 4 requirements for judgement/dismissal of entire action: --> these were considered threshold conditions under ROC 2014, but no longer in ROC 2021
1. Requirement 1: Defendant has entered appearance in an action; --> I.E. D MUST FILE NOTICE OF INTENTION TO CONTEST
2. Requirement 2: Parties have an opportunity to be heard on the question of law/construction of documents;
3. Requirement 3: Question of law/construction of documents suitable to be determined without full trial of action
a. There are no questions that raise factual issues – a question that raises factual issues is NOT suitable for determination without a full trial  
b. There are no novel issue of law that has considerable public importance 
c. A question that raises complex issues of law may be suitable for determination without a trial BUT if it’s a novel issue of law that has considerable public importance, then a full trial might be more suitable
4. Requirement 4: Determination of question of law/construction of documents fully determines entire cause of action: A determination of question of law/construction of documents will fully determine the entire cause of action, claim or issue 


Judgments on admission
- Another way to get a judgement without a trial => By using an admission from the other party

SAPT matter – Application for judgement on admission of facts  
O 9 r 9(4)(j)
- Application for judgement on admission of facts is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT

Court’s power to grant judgement on admission 
Party’s application orally or in writing => Court can give judgement based on admissions of fact in party’s pleadings or documents 
O 9 r 18(2)
- Court may, on application made orally or in writing, give judgement on admissions of fact made by a party in the party’s pleadings or other documents 
Admissions must be viewed as a whole, be clear and equivocal 
Mycitydeal Ltd (trading as Groupon UK) v Villas International Property Pte Ltd
- Standard of admissions: Admissions must be viewed as a whole (cannot be cherry-picked) and they must be clear and equivocal 

Third Party and similar Proceedings 
- When defendant wants to bring a 3rd party into any OS/OC started by the plaintiff against the defendant 

Objective of 3rd party proceedings 
Chancery Law Corp v Management Corporation Strata Title Plan No. 1024 
- 2 objectives:
1. Enable parties to raise all issues relating to the subject matter of the hearing in one set of proceedings
=> Avoids delay and unnecessary costs resulting from multiple actions
2. Avoid inconsistency between judicial decisions if common issues are litigated in separated proceedings 

Explanation 


If claimant brings a claim against df1, he can join df2 later 
There are situations where df1 has counterclaim against claimant (blue arrow) – df1 is claimant in counterclaim 
Df1 can bring counterclaim against a party who is not party to the original proceedings – this is the 2nd df in counterclaim 

Step 1: Defendant must satisfy any of the 3 grounds to issue a 3rd party notice  
Defendant must satisfy any of 3 grounds to issue a 3rd party notice 
O 10 r 1(1)
- Defendant in any action will have grounds to issue a 3rd party notice if defendant: 
a. Ground 1: Defendant claims indemnity from 3rd party: Claims against a person not already a party to the action any contribution or indemnity; OR 
b. Ground 2: Defendant claims remedy from 3rd party similar to Claimant’s claimed remedy: Claims against such a person any relief or remedy relating to or connected with the original subject matter of the action and substantially the same as some relief or remedy claimed by the claimant (e.g. contractor got sued for something that was subcontractor’s fault) 
c. Ground 3: Defendant requires any question relating to subject matter to be determined between Claimant, Defendant and 3rd party: Requires that any question or issue relating to or connected with the original subject matter of the action should be determined not only as between the claimant and defendant but also as between either or both of them and a person not already a party to the action 

Step 2: Defendant assess if Court’s permission if required to issue 3rd party notice  
Court’s permission NOT required if: (1) Claimant begun action by OC + (2) Defendant issues 3rd party notice before serving defense on Claimant 
O 10 r 1(2)
- Defendant can issue 3rd party notice to 3rd party without Court’s permission only if:
* Claimant begun action by OC; and 
* Defendant issues the 3rd party notice before serving defendant’s defense on claimant 

Court’s permission required if: (1) Claimant begun action by OC + (2) Defendant issues 3rd party notice after serving defense on Claimant 
O 10 r 1(2)
- Defendant cannot issue 3rd party notice to 3rd party without Court’s permission if: 
* Claimant did not begin action by OC; or 
* Defendant issues 3rd party notice after serving defendant’s defense on claimant 

Procedure for Defendant to get Court’s permission 
SAPT matter: Addition and removal of parties => Implication: Application for Court’s permission must be filed as part of SAPT application 
O 9 r 9(4)(a) 
- Application for addition and removal of parties is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT

Court power to add or remove parties 
O 9 r 10(1)
- Court may
* Add or remove claimants or defendants 
* Give permission for defendant to issue a 3rd party notice in accordance with O 10 
* Give directions for originating process to be served on any person who may have an interest in the action 

Defendant makes application by summons without notice in Form 22 => Court may direct summons to be served 
O 10 r 2(1)
- Application for permission to issue a 3rd party notice may be made by summons without notice in Form 22 but Court may direct the summons to be served 

Defendant’s application must be supported by an affidavit with contents in O 10 r 2(2)
O 10 r 2(2)
- Application for permission to issue a 3rd party notice must be supported by an affidavit stating:
a. Nature of claim made by claimant in action 
b. Stage which proceedings in action have reached
c. Nature of the claim made by the applicant or particulars of the question or issue required to be determined (as the case may be) and the facts on which the proposed 3rd party notice is based; and 
d. Name and address of the person against whom the 3rd party notice is to be issued 

Court order granting permission may contain directions as to specified period for issuance of 3rd party notice
O 10 r 3(1)
- Order granting permission to defendant to issue a 3rd party notice may contain directions as to the period within which the 3rd party notice is to be issued 

Step 3: Defendant serves 3rd party notice to 3rd party => For OC matters, 3rd party must serve notice of intention to contest within 14 days of receiving notice
Procedure for defendant to issue a 3rd party notice to 3rd party 
After filing notice of intention to contest (if main OC) => Defendant can issue 3rd party notice in Form 20 or Form 21 to 3rd party => 3rd party notice with contents in O 10 r 1(1) 
O 10 r 1(1)
- After Defendant files a notice of intention to contest => Defendant can issue 3rd party notice in Form 20 or Form 21 to the 3rd party 
* Form 20: Grounds O 10 r 1(1)(a) and O 10 r 1(1)(b)
* Form 21: Ground O 10 r 1(1)(c)
- 3rd party notice must contain: 
* Statement of nature of claim made by claimant against defendant 
* Nature and grounds of claim made by defendant against 3rd party 
* Question or issue required to be determined vis-à-vis claimant, defendant and 3rd party 

Defendant must serve 3rd party notice with a copy of OC/OA and any pleadings served in the action
O 10 r 3(2) 
- Defendant must serve 3rd party notice on 3rd party together with a copy of the OC or OA by which the action was begun and any of the pleadings served in the action 

Method of service: If main action was begun by claimant through OC => Certain rules apply as if 3rd party notice were an OC 
* Defendant must personally serve 3rd party notice on the 3rd party (O 6 r 4)
* 3rd party would need to serve notice of intention to contest or not contest within 14 days of receiving 3rd party notice (O 6 r 6) 
* Rules of service out of Singapore would apply if 3rd party has to be served out of Singapore (O 8)

O 10 r 3(3)
- Where action was begun by claimant through OC, the following provisions apply in relation to a 3rd party notice and to the proceedings begun as if:
a. 3rd party notice were an OC and proceedings begun thereby an action; and 
b. Defendant issuing 3rd party notice were a claimant and the person against whom it is issued a defendant in that action
- Provisions: 
* O 6 r 4: 3rd party notice must be served personally on 3rd party 
* O 6 r 5(6): If 3rd party notice must be served in Singapore, defendant must serve 3rd party notice within 14 days after 3rd party notice was issued
* O 6 r 5(7): If 3rd party notice must be served outside Singapore, defendant must serve 3rd party notice within 28 days after 3rd party notice was issued 
* O 6 r 6: 
(i) O 6 r 6(1): 3rd party being served in Singapore must file and serve notice of intention to contest within 14 days after being served  
(ii) O 6 r 6(2): 3rd party being served outside Singapore must file and serve notice of intention to contest within 21 days after being served 
(iii) O 6 r 6(3): Notice of intention to contest in Form 10 
(iv) O 6 r 6(4): Filing and service of notice of intention to contest is not a submission to jurisdiction or waiver of improper service of 3rd party notice
(v) O 6 r 6(5): If 3rd party fails to file and serve notice of intention to contest, defendant may apply for judgement against 3rd party under Form 11 
(vi) O 6 r 6(6): Defendant must file memorandum of service under Form 12 if he applies for judgement under O 6 r 6(5)
(vii) O 6 r 6(7): Court may direct payment of interest when giving judgment. Interest computed from date of 3rd party notice to date which judgement is given at the rate of 5.33% per year 
* O 6 r 7(4): If 3rd party is challenging jurisdiction of the Court, 3rd party need not file and serve a defense on the merits but must file and serve a defense stating the ground on which defendant is challenging the Court’s jurisdiction 
* O 6 r 7(5): 3rd party may challenge the Court’s jurisdiction that (a) the Court has no jurisdiction to hear the action OR (b) the Court should not exercise jurisdiction to hear the action 
* O 6 r 7(6): Any defense filed by 3rd party under O 6 r 7(4) is not treated as submission to jurisdiction 
* O 7 r 4(1): Court may on a summons without notice, direct service to be effected on an agent or manager of a principal if it is satisfied that (a) Action is against a principal who does not reside within or is absent from Singapore AND (b) Agent or manager has personal control or management within Singapore over the principal’s affairs that specifically relate to the action at time of service AND (c) Authority of agent or manager has not been terminated and they have an ongoing business relationship with principal at time of application 
* O 7 r 4(3): Person serving must send a copy of the order of Court authorizing service under O 7 r 4 and of the relevant document to be served to the principal’s overseas address by prepaid registered post if claimant knows that address within 14 days after service on the agent or manager 
* O 7 r 5: If 3rd party notice claims possession of immovable property, Court may, on summons without notice, authorize service to be effected by placing document on some conspicuous part of immovable property if Court is satisfied that (a) no person appears to be in possession of immovable property AND (b) service cannot be otherwise be effected on any party to be served 
* O 8: Rules for Service out of Singapore 

Method of service: If main action was begun by claimant through OA => Certain rules apply as if 3rd party notice were an OA 
* Defendant must personally serve 3rd party notice on the 3rd party (O 6 r 4)
* Rules of service out of Singapore would apply if 3rd party has to be served out of Singapore (O 8)

O 10 r 3(4)
- Where action was begun by claimant through OA, the following provisions apply in relation to a 3rd party notice and to the proceedings begun as if:
a. 3rd party notice were an OA and proceedings begun thereby an action; and 
b. Defendant issuing 3rd party notice were a claimant and the person against whom it is issued a defendant in that action
- Provisions: 
* O 6 r 4: 3rd party notice must be served personally on 3rd party 
* O 6 r 11(4): If 3rd party notice must be served in Singapore, defendant must serve 3rd party notice within 14 days after 3rd party notice was issued
* O 6 r 11(5): If 3rd party notice must be served outside Singapore, defendant must serve 3rd party notice within 28 days after 3rd party notice was issued 
* O 6 r 12(3): If 3rd party is challenging jurisdiction of the Court, 3rd party need not file and serve a defense on the merits but must file and serve a defense stating the ground on which defendant is challenging the Court’s jurisdiction
* O 6 r 12(4): O 6 r 7(5): 3rd party may challenge the Court’s jurisdiction that (a) the Court has no jurisdiction to hear the action OR (b) the Court should not exercise jurisdiction to hear the action 
* O 6 r 12(5): 3rd party may challenge the Court’s jurisdiction that (a) the Court has no jurisdiction to hear the action OR (b) the Court should not exercise jurisdiction to hear the action
* O 7 r 4: Court may on a summons without notice, direct service to be effected on an agent or manager of a principal if it is satisfied that (a) Action is against a principal who does not reside within or is absent from Singapore AND (b) Agent or manager has personal control or management within Singapore over the principal’s affairs that specifically relate to the action at time of service AND (c) Authority of agent or manager has not been terminated and they have an ongoing business relationship with principal at time of application 
* O 7 r 5: If 3rd party notice claims possession of immovable property, Court may, on summons without notice, authorize service to be effected by placing document on some conspicuous part of immovable property if Court is satisfied that (a) no person appears to be in possession of immovable property AND (b) service cannot be otherwise be effected on any party to be served
* Order 8 service out of Singapore rules apply 

Step 4: Defendant must serve summons on 3rd party and apply to Court for 3rd party directions  
Procedure for Defendant to serve summons on 3rd party and apply to Court for directions 
For main OC – After 3rd party files and serves notice of intention to contest in Form 10 => Then Defendant must serve summons in Form 23 on all other parties to the action => Defendant must also serve on Court to apply for directions 
For main OA – Anytime after Defendant issues 3rd party notice => Defendant must serve summons in Form 23 on all other parties to the action => Defendant must also serve on Court to apply for directions 
O 10 r 4(1)
- Defendant who issued 3rd party notice, must by summons in Form 23, serve on all other parties to the action, to apply to the Court for directions 
- If action was begun by OC, then application to Court for directions must not be made before 3rd party files and serves a notice of intention to contest or not contest in Form 10 

Procedure if Defendant fails to serve summons on 3rd party and fails to apply to Court for directions 
For main OC – If Defendant fails to serve summons on 3rd party => 3rd party must file notice of intention to contest => After 14 days, 3rd party may serve summons in Form 23 on all other parties to the action => 3rd party must also serve on Court to apply for order to set aside the 3rd party notice 
For main OA – If Defendant fails to serve summons on 3rd party => 14 days after 3rd party receives 3rd party notice => 3rd party may serve summons in Form 23 on all other parties to the action => 3rd party must also serve on Court to apply for order to set aside the 3rd party notice 
Failure of defendant to serve summons on 3rd party 
O 10 r 4(2)
- If defendant does not serve summons on 3rd party, 3rd party may: 
a. In an action begun by OC, after 14 days after filing and serving notice of intention to contest => By summons in Form 23 to be served on all other parties to the action, apply to Court for directions for an order to set aside 3rd party notice 
b. In an action begun by OA, after 14 days after serving notice of notice on 3rd party => By summons in Form 23 to be served on all other parties to the action, apply to Court for directions for an order to set aside 3rd party notice

Step 5: Court’s powers when issuing directions
On Defendant’s application for Court directions => Court may make certain orders 
O 10 r 4(3)
- On application for directions under O 10 r 4, Court may: 
a. If liability of 3rd party to defendant is established on hearing => Order judgement to be entered against the 3rd party in favor of defendant 
b. Order any claim, question or issue in 3rd party notice to be tried in such manner as the Court may direct 
c. Dismiss application and terminate proceedings on 3rd party notice and may do so either before or after any judgement has been signed by the claimant against the defendant 

On Defendant’s application for Court directions => Court may make other orders 
O 10 r 4(4)
- On application for directions under O 10 r 4, Court may: 
a. Give 3rd party permission to defend action, either alone or jointly with any defendant, upon such terms as may be just, or to appear at the trial or hearing and to take such part in the trial or hearing as may be just
b. Make orders as appear to the Court proper for having the rights and liabilities of parties most conveniently determined and enforced and as to the extent to which the 3rd party is to be bound by any judgement or decision in the action 



*I. PRODUCTION OF DOCUMENTS 
- Parties in an action commenced by an OC must produce documents relevant to the dispute to each other
- General production (Various kinds) 
o General production – O 11 r 2 
o Specific production – O 11 r 3 
o Pre-action/non-party production – O 11 r 11 

Definition of “document”
S3 of Evidence Act 
- “Document” includes, in addition to a document in writing
a. Any map, plan, graph or drawing
b. Any photograph
c. Any label, marking or other writing which identifies or describes anything of which it forms a part, or to which it is attached by any means whatsoever
d. Any disc, tape, sound-track or other device in which sounds or other data (not being visual images) are embodied so as to be capable (with or without the aid of some other equipment) of being reproduced therefrom
e. Any film (including microfilm), negative, tape, disc or other device in which one or more visual images are embodied so as to be capable (with or without the aid of some other equipment) of being reproduced therefrom
f. Any paper or other material on which there are marks, impressions, figures, letters, symbols or peforations having a meaning for persons qualified to interpret them 

Definition of “electronically stored document” 
Sanae Achar v Sci-Gen Ltd
Definition of electronically stored document
- E.g. word processor documents, spreadsheets, presentation slides and image files
- Sanae: non-exhaustive list, which includes emails, databases, backup copies, sound/video recordings, storage media (e.g. does not include WhatsApp / WeChat messages which are now generally accepted) 

Supreme Court PD No. 3 of 2009 sets out an opt-in framework which parties may adopt to apply for discovery and inspection of electronically stored documents
- Besides the traditional tests of relevancy and necessity, Court will consider the following factors when granting discovery and inspection of electronically stored documents: 
a. Number of electronic documents involved 
b. Nature of case and complexity of issues
c. Value of claim and financial position of each party
d. Ease and expense of retrieval of any electronically stored document including: 
(i) Accessibility, location and likelihood of locating document 
(ii) Costs of recovering and giving discovery and inspection of document 
(iii) Likelihood that document will be materially altered in the course of recovery, giving discovery and inspection 
e. Significant of electronically stored document to the issues in dispute 
f. Relevance and materiality of electronically stored document to the issues in dispute 

Cases of Court allowing discovery and inspection of electronically stored documents 
- Emails: Trek Technology (Singapore) Pte Ltd v FE Global Electronics Pte Ltd 
- Databases: Sumitomo Corporation v Credit Lyonnais Rouse Ltd
- Backup copies: Record TV Pte Ltd v MediaCorp TV Singapore Pte Ltd
- Sound recordings in MP3 files: Sony Music Entertainment (Australia) Limited v University of Tasmania 
- Video recordings: Record TV Pte Ltd v MediaCorp TV Singapore Pte Ltd
- Storage media (CD Rom and tapes) and recording devices (hard disk): Megastar Entertainment Pte Ltd v Odex Pte Ltd 

General Provisions on Court’s power in relation to production of documents 
Order 11 disclosure provisions do not affect any agreement of parties or Court’s power to allow discovery in the interests of justice  - i.e. O 11 only sets out the BASIC REQUIREMENTS of the parties’ obligation to produce and exchange documents, so it can be a higher requirement (via agreement or court order)
O 11 r 1(1)
- Order 11 does not affect: 
a. Any agreement that the parties or any set of parties may make to broaden the scope of such obligations; OR 
b. Court’s power to allow a broader scope of discovery where it is in the interests of justice to do so 
*Court must have regard to O 11 r 1(2) principles when exercising powers under Order 11
O 11 r 1(2)
- In exercising power under Order 11, Court must bear in mind the following principles:
a. Claimant must sue and proceed on strength of his case and not weakness of defendant’s case; and
b. Party who sues or is sued does not give up privacy and confidentiality in any document or communication.

Court’s power to broaden scope of discovery in the interests of justice
O 11 r 1(3)
- Court may allow a broader scope of discovery where Court determines that it is in the interests of justice to do so 
O 11 r 1(4)
- It will be in the interest of justice to allow a broader scope of discovery where it could aid in disposing proceedings fairly
O 11 r 1(5)
- Where there is an application for a broader scope of discovery, the 2 principles in O 11 r 1(2) does not apply and Court must have regard only to Ideals and to O 11 r 1(4) 

Production of documents (Exercised during First Case Conference)
*1st stage: Initial Orders for production (old “general discovery”) – takes place before SAPT
Part 1: After Court makes Initial Order during case conference => Parties have 14 days to exchange a list of and copy of documents in their possession or control  
Where AEICs are ordered before document production – typically no need for any general production. But there may still be specific production after the AEICs are exchanged (as part of SAPT)!

Where AEICs are ordered before document production, typically no need for general production. 
- This is because AEICs will already contain a list of documents that the party wishes to rely on 
- There may be situations where parties agree to have general production despite the AEIC 
- Specific production occurs after AEICs are exchanged 

O 11 r 2(1)
- Court can make an Initial Order for production of documents at a case conference 
- Parties must within 14 days after the date of the case conference, exchange a list of and a copy of the documents in their possession or control which fall within the scope of disclosure 
O 11 r 2(3)
- A copy of any document may be in paper form or, if in an electronic form, in a common electronic format that the other party can use 
Part 2: Scope of Documents which must be disclosed
O 11 r 2(1)
- All documents in the possession or control of parties if they fall within one or more of the following categories: 
a. Documents that party will be relying on
b. Known adverse documents: documents which will be harmful to your case
c. Any other documents coming within the scope of any broader discovery agreed by parties or ordered by Court
Definition of “known adverse documents” 
O 11 r 2(2)
- “Known adverse documents” includes documents which a party ought reasonably to know are adverse to the party’s case 

Part 3: Parties need not exchange common documents
O 11 r 2(4)
- To comply with O 11 r 2(1), it is NOT necessary for parties to exchange documents common to them that are in their possession or control to avoid duplication and to save costs 

Part 4: Consequences of non-compliance 
O 11 r 2(5)
- Parties may not rely on any document that was not exchanged or produced under Order 11 --> unless you get permission of the court

Adverse costs orders for failure to comply 
O 21 r 4(d)
- That party has failed to comply with any order of court, any relevant pre?action protocol or any practice direction.

Other consequences under O 11 r 7
O 11 r 7
- If any party fails to comply with any order made by the Court under Order 11, the Court may:
a. Order that action be dismissed or defense be struck out and judgement entered: Order that the action be dismissed or that the defense be struck out and judgement be entered accordingly 
b. Draw any adverse inference: Draw an adverse inference or make any such order as the Court deems fit
c. Punish party for contempt of Court => Unless party show that he was not notified: Punish that party for contempt of Court if the order has been served on the party’s solicitor => But it is open to that party to show that that party was not notified or did not know about the order 
d. Order that party may not rely on document: Order that that party may not rely on any document that is within the scope of the order unless the Court approves

*2nd stage: Order for production of requested documents (old “specific discovery”)
SAPT matter: Production of Documents  
O 9 r 9(4)(k)
- Application for production of documents is matter to be dealt with under SAPT
Not limited to OC, but not commonly used in an OA situation 
NOTE BEFORE MAKING SAPT APPLICATION FOR SPECIFIC DISCOVERY – YOU SHOULD FIRST WRITE TO THE OTHER SIDE WITH A LETTER AND REQUEST THE DOCUMENTS AND EXPLAIN WHY – only if the other side ignores or rejects then consider filing application for specific discovery

Part 1: Requesting party can make request to Court => Requesting party must properly identify requested documents and show materiality of requested documents => Court may make orders for production of requested documents 
O 11 r 3(1)
- Court may order any party to produce the original or a copy of a specific document or class of documents in the party’s possession or control, if the requesting party:
a. Properly identifies the requested documents; and
b. Shows that the requested documents are material to the issues in the case 

Part 2: Procedure for requesting party to request
If more than 5 categories of documents are sought => Parties must complete summary table (Form B10 of Appendix B to Supreme Court PD 2021)
Para 68 of Supreme Court PD 2021
- Where more than 5 categories of particulars or documents are sought and the application is contested, parties must complete the Summary Table instead of filing written submissions 

Form B10 of Appendix B to Supreme Court PD 2021 


Part 3: If requested documents are NOT in party’s possession and control => requested party must file affidavit stating O 11 r 3(2) contents  
O 11 r 3(2)
- If requested documents are not in party’s (the party against whom the order is made) possession or control => Court can order party to file affidavit stating:
* The fact that requested documents are not in party’s possession or control; and
* Whether that party had such possession or control previously; and
* If so, when that party had parted with possession or control of requested documents 

Special Scenario: If AEICs are ordered to be exchanged before exchange of documents, the Court cannot order production of requested documents until parties have complied with Court’s order to exchange AEICs
O 11 r 3(3)
- If Court has ordered parties to exchange AEICs before exchanging documents => Court cannot order production of requested documents until parties have complied with Court’s order to exchange AEICs
- Normally where AEICs are ordered to be exchanged before exchange of documents, there will NOT be a second stage of discovery

Order for production of any document (against a party or non-party) [this is post-action, cf pre-action under O 11 r 11]
Court on its own accord and at any time=> Order any party or non-party to produce a copy of any document in party/non-party’s possession or control
O 11 r 4
- Court may of its own accord and at any time, order any party or non-party to produce a copy of any document that is in the person’s possession or control 
- Subject to O 11 r 5, r 8 and r 9
* O 11 r 5: No order for production of certain documents
* O 11 r 8: Privileged documents
* O 11 r 9: Confidential documents 

*General restrictions on order for production of certain documents – both for general and specific production
No production of “train of inquiry” documents unless special case
O 11 r 5(1)
- Court must not order production of any document that is not material in itself but merely leads a party on a train of inquiry to other documents 
- Default is no, except in a special case 

No production of private correspondences unless special case
O 11 r 5(2)
- Court must not order production of any document that is part of a party’s private or internal correspondence whether in paper form or in an electronic format (e.g. electronic mail, short message service, instant messaging service) 
- Except: --> 2 situations where private correspondences CAN be produced
a. It is a special case; OR 
b. Such correspondences are known adverse documents (I.E. EVEN IF DOCS ARE INTERNAL/CONFIDENTIAL, IF THEY ARE KNOWN BY THE POSSESSOR TO BE ADVERSE TO THEM, IT MUST BE DISCLOSED, e.g. private correspondence contains an admission) 

No production of documents subject to privilege or where contrary to public interest 
O 11 r 5(3)
- Court must not order production of any document which is: 
* Subject to privilege (e.g. legal professional privilege); OR 
* Where its production would be contrary to public interest 

GENERAL RULE: No order for production of privileged documents unless privileged party consents or Court approves 
O 11 r 8(1)
- Document which was at any time subject to privilege must not be relied on unless the party entitled to the privilege consents or the Court approves 
O 11 r 8(2)
- A privileged document does not lose its privilege or confidentiality even if it was disclosed or taken inadvertently or unlawfully by anyone 
GENERAL RULE: Order of production of confidential documents can be allowed, if ordered by Court
O 11 r 9(1)
- A party who is required by any order made by the Court under this Order to produce documents may not withhold or object to the production of any document on the ground that the document is confidential.

O 11 r 9(2)
- A confidential document does not lose its confidentiality even if it was disclosed or taken inadvertently or unlawfully by anyone.

Pre-action/non-party production of documents 
Why? 
- To identify possible parties to proceedings 
- To trace party’s property 
- Any other lawful purpose 

Covered more comprehensively above – see here 

O 11 r 11(1) – The Court may order the production of documents and information before the commencement of proceedings or against a nonparty to identify possible parties to any proceedings, to enable a party to trace the party’s property or for any other lawful purpose, in the interests of justice.

Non-party is entitled to reasonable costs
O 11 r 11(3)
- A non-party is entitled to all reasonable costs arising out of such an application 
- “Non-party” includes the respondent to a pre-action application, who is not a party yet 

Miscellaneous Issues
*Continuing duty to produce 
When Court orders production of document => Document only comes into party’s possession or control during proceedings => Party must produce the document within 14 days 
O 11 r 6
- Where Court makes an order under O 11 r 2, O 11 r 3 or O 11 r 4, the party required to produce documents will remain under a duty to produce any documents within 14 days after that document comes into the party’s possession or control at any times in the course of proceedings 
* O 11 r 2: Initial Order for production 
* O 11 r 3: Order for production of requested documents
* O 11 r 4: Order for production of any document (party or non-party) 

*Consequences of non-compliance with order for production 
Court may make certain orders
O 11 r 7
- If any party fails to comply with any order made by the Court under Order 11, the Court may:
a. Order that action be dismissed or defense be struck out and judgement entered; --> this is very serious so requires a serious breach
b. Draw any adverse inference or make any such order as deemed fit;
c. Punish party for contempt of Court if order has been served on his solicitor => Unless party show that he was not notified or did not know about the order;
d. Order that party may not rely on document unless the Court approves.
Practical matter – how do you know if the other party is not disclosing certain documents? 
- Documents normally leave trails – they are referred to in other correspondence etc. 
- Or documents that are required to be kept under certain laws, e.g. Companies Act 
- Then you can go and complain to court for non-disclosure, or you can ask court to draw adverse inference 

Adverse costs orders for failure to comply 
O 21 r 4(d)
- That party has failed to comply with any order of court, any relevant pre?action protocol or any practice direction.

Confidentiality NOT a defense against disclosure order
O 11 r 9(1)
- A party who was required by any Court order to produce documents may not withhold or object to the production of any document on the ground that the document is confidential 
- Note – this applies to all production of documents under O 11 
- See also O 11 r 8 (above) which is another relevant rule on confidentiality 

*Restriction on use of documents produced in other proceedings 
Document produced under Order 11 must NOT be relied on in other proceedings => Unless party who produced document consents or Court otherwise approves
O 11 r 10(1)
- Any document produced under Order 11 or by compulsion of law in Court proceedings must not be relied on in other proceedings by other parties or non-parties 
- Except:
* Party who produced document consents
* Court otherwise approves 

O 11 r 10(2): APPLYING FOR EXPRESS COURT ORDER TO PROHIBIT USE OF A DOCUMENT
- Party who use or produced any document in a case may apply to the Court to prohibit the use of such documents for any purpose other than for that case 

Inspection of originals upon request 
Any party can request to inspect original of any document produced => Opposing party must arrange mutually convenient time and place for investigation  
O 11 r 12(1)
- If a party requests to inspect the original of any document produced, the party who produced the document must arrange a mutually convenient time and place for the inspection to take place 

Inspection within 14 days after request 
O 11 r 12(2)
- Inspection must take place within 14 days after request unless parties otherwise agree


Witnesses 
*A. FACT WITNESSES AND AEICS 
*General rule: Trial must be decided on basis of AEICs (affidavits of evidence-in-chief), cross-ex, re-ex, oral or written subs 
O 15 r 16(1)
- General rule: The trial in an OC, assessment of damages or value and taking of accounts must be decided on the basis of: 
* The witnesses’ AEICs;
* Cross-examination;
* Re-examination; and 
* Oral or written submissions.

- The AEIC is the general method for adducing a witness’ testimony at trial. The scheme of adducing evidence-in-chief by way of AEICs is designed to achieve a fair and expeditious disposal of proceedings, to save costs and to eliminate any element of surprise 
--> witnesses submit written AEICs and they would then be subject to cross-examination to test their statements under oath

*Exception: Dispensation with written AEIC to use oral EIC only 
O 15 r 16(2)
- In a special case, Court may allow a witness’s EIC to be given orally instead of by AEIC.
- E.g. When an order to attend court is to compel a witness to testify at trial.
- E.g. there is a last minute witness before trial and there is no time to get their written AEIC, so need to compel them to give oral EIC at trial instead
Application for Court’s leave to dispense with AEIC are heard inter partes => Opposing party can object to application for such leave
Basil Anthony Herman v Premier Security Cooperative Ltd 
- Objecting party must raise all objections in the dispensation application rather than to delay and mount an attack later on 
- If objecting party does not object, then he has to live with the decision later on 
- Reason: Judicial policy against trial by ambush which would severely disrupt a party’s ability to plan its case 

*AEIC can only be used if witness attends court for cross-examination 
Filing and service of AEIC does not automatically mean it can be used
O 15 r 16(3) 
- AEIC may not be used if the maker does not attend Court for cross-examination 
- Unless the parties otherwise agree  


Contents of AEIC 
AEIC must contain all material facts 
Material facts cannot be departed from during trial unless new facts occur after AEIC made
O 15 r 16(4)
- AEIC must contain all material facts 
- The material facts in the AEIC may not be departed from or supplemented by new facts in oral evidence unless new facts occurred after the date of making the AEIC

AEIC must contain only evidence that is admissible in law 
O 15 r 16(5)
- AEIC must contain only evidence that is admissible in law 

CES v International Air Transport Association 
- Solicitors should ensure that proper bounds of evidentiary affidavits are not transgressed 

AEIC must not contain submissions on conclusions of fact or legal arguments 
CES v International Air Transport Association 
- AEICs are to place facts and disputed facts before the Court and are not for submissions on conclusions of fact or legal arguments (which are for written or oral submissions) 
- AEICs should not have arguments inside 

AEIC must only contain relevant facts 
O 15 r 25(1)
- AEIC must contain only relevant facts 

AEIC must not contain vulgarities and offensive words
O 15 r 25(2)
- AEIC must not contain 
a. Vulgar or insulting words unless those words are in issue in the action; OR 
b. Anything that is intended to offend or to belittle any person or entity 

AEIC and all documents filed and used in Court must be in English 
O 15 r 18
- AEIC must be in English language 

O 3 r 7(1)
- All documents filed or used in Court must be in English language

Translation: Accompanied by translation certified by Court Interpreter or Verified by affidavit of qualified translator
O 3 r 7(2)
- A document which is not in English Language must be accompanied by a translation in the English Language certified by a court interpreter or verified by an affidavit of a person qualified to translate the document 

AEIC must be signed and affirmed before Commissioner of Oaths
O 15 r 18
- AEIC must be signed and affirmed before a commissioner of oaths 

Form of AEIC
AEIC – Form 31 
O 15 r 19(1)
- AEIC must be in Form 31 with text set out in consecutively numbered paragraphs 

Formatting of AEIC 
O 15 r 19(2)
- In cases of AEICs in an OC, the maker of the AEIC must include at the top left hand portion of the first page of the AEIC a color photograph of the maker in the space indicated in Form 31, which:
a. Measures 35mm wide and 45mm high;
b. Is taken in the last 12 months; and
c. Shows the full face facing forward with eyes open and with no headwear except what is worn in accordance with religious or racial customs 

Additional formalities requirements for AEIC 
Paras 78 – 80 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Instructions for formatting AEIC
- Instructions for non-documentary exhibits in AEIC (CD-ROM, samples of merchandize) 
- Instructions for documentary exhibits in AEIC 

Effect of non-compliance with formalities 
AEIC may be rejected and party in default may be subject to costs order
Para 83 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021
- AEICs which do not comply with Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 and Rules may be rejected by the Court and made the subject of an order for costs

Court can order exchange of AEICs simultaneously or sequentially 
O 9 r 8(1)
- Court may order parties to file and serve their lists of witnesses and AEICs of all or some of the witnesses simultaneously or in any sequence 

Notes:
- Simultaneously: Ensures that neither party will have any tactical advantage
- Sequentially: Appropriate where it is necessary for a party to first review the perimeters of the opposing party’s evidence before responding 

Parties can raise objection to contents of opposing party’s AEIC
Objecting parties required to give notice to object at least 28 days before first hearing date 
O 15 r 16(6)
- If party intends to object to contents of AEIC on ground of admissibility or other reason, party must by filing and serving form specified in the practice directions, give notice to the party who is relying on those AEICs of at least 28 days before the first date of hearing 

Form of notice to object – Form 13 and contents in Para 84 of SCPD
Para 84 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Objecting party must file and serve notice in Form 13 of Appendix B of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Form 13 must state: 
* All objections to contents of the AEIC that will be raised at the hearing of the cause or matter
* All the grounds of the objections 

Time of adjudication: At trial 
Para 84 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Adjudication on material objected to in AEIC should only be sought at the trial or hearing of the cause or matter for which AEICs were filed and not before 
- If adjudication is sought prior to the trial or hearing => Application for adjudication will be adjourned to be dealt with at trial or hearing of the cause or matter => Applicant may be ordered to pay costs of adjournment 


*B. EXPERT WITNESSES AND AEICS
- Expert Witnesses’ evidence => Admitted into Court through AEIC + Examination 

*General rule: Trial must be decided on basis of AEICs, cross-ex, re-ex, oral or written subs 
O 15 r 16(1)
- General rule: The trial in an OC, assessment of damages or value and taking of accounts must be decided on the basis of: 
* The witnesses’ AEICs
* Cross-examination
* Re-examination and 
* Oral or written submissions 

*Step 1: Whether evidence is considered expert evidence under O 12
O 12 r 1(1)
- Expert is a person with scientific, technical or other specialized knowledge based on training, study or experience 

Sanum Investments Limited v ST Group Co Ltd – Alleged foreign law expert not considered expert as he did not have enough experience
- Court found that a foreign law expert put forward by a party did not qualify as an expert under the Rules of Court
- The foreign law expert was called in to provide his opinion on Lao law which is the governing law of the agreement but: 
a. Foreign law expert did not formally practice as a lawyer in Laos and was only a party representative who files petitions, submits evidence, lodges appeals and argues cases 
b. His area of practice was not clear 
c. It was not known how many cases he was involved in 
d. He only began practicing recently (5 years)

*Step 2: Party intending to use expert evidence must inform Court during case conference and make SAPT application to apply for Court’s approval 
Parties intending to use expert evidence must inform Court during case conference
O 9 r 21(1)
- Parties are to inform Court during case conference if they intend to rely on expert evidence
SAPT matter: Use of expert evidence 
O 9 r 9(4)(m) 
- Application to use expert evidence is a matter to be dealt with under SAPT

*Step 3: In the SAPT application – Party seeking to use expert evidence must ask Court for approval by satisfying 3 requirements 
Expert evidence CANNOT be used in Court unless there is Court approval (O 12 r 2(1) ROC)

3 requirements for Court approval 
O 12 r 2(2)
- Parties must consider: 
* Requirement 1: Whether expert evidence will contribute materially to the determination of any issue that relates to scientific, technical or other specialized knowledge 
* Requirement 2: Whether such issue can be resolved by an agreed statement of fact or by submissions based on mutually agreed materials – i.e. issue cannot be resolved by some form of agreement between the parties or based on mutually agreed material, then can use expert evidence
o E.g. of issue being resolved by an agreed statement of fact/mutually agreed materials: foreign statute clarifying application of foreign law (so foreign law expert no longer required)
O 12 r 2(3)
- Court must not approve the use of expert evidence unless it will contribute materially to the determination of any issue in the case and the issue cannot be resolved in the manner stated in O 12 r 2(2)
O 12 r 2(4) --> this is a continuing power of the court – even after court has originally approved, it can later disallow
- Requirement 3: Court may disallow the use of or reject any expert evidence if it is of the opinion that: 
* The expert lacks the requisite specialized knowledge in the issues referred to him or her; or 
* That he or she lacks impartiality.

*Number of Experts which can be appointed by Parties
General rule: Parties should agree on one common expert 
O 12 r 3(1)
- As far as possible, parties must agree on one common expert. This is for efficiency concerns.

Parties can have separate experts but each only one expert for any issue => Unless special case and with Court’s approval 
O 12 r 3(2)
- Except in a special case and with the Court’s approval, a party may not rely on expert evidence from more than one expert for any issue 
Selecting an expert
- Each party will nominate its expert in a certain template. The other party has the opportunity to argue about the other party’s choice of expert in this template 
- The court retains the power to determine whether the nominated expert has the necessary expertise before bringing the expert 

Court can appoint its own expert to add or replace parties’ experts
O 12 r 3(3)
- In a special case, the Court may appoint a court expert in addition to or in place of the parties’ common expert or all the experts 
- Ensures that Court will still get the assistance needed when parties’ expert evidence are contradictory or unhelpful 

Where there is more than one expert => Court may order that experts testify as a panel 
O 12 r 7(1)
- In a case where there is more than one expert, the Court may order that all or some of the experts testify as a panel 

Court may order experts to opine on each other’s views 
O 12 r 7(4)
- Where experts testify as a panel, Court may order that they give their views on the issues referred to them and comment on one another’s views 

Court may order cross-examination and re-examination of experts in panel
O 12 r 7(5)
- Court may order cross-examination and re-examination of all or some of the experts in the panel in any sequence as the Court thinks appropriate, whether before or after the experts have testified as a panel 

Court must give appropriate directions relating to experts 
O 12 r 3(4)
- The Court must give all appropriate directions relating to the appointment of experts, including the method of questioning in Court and the remuneration to be paid to them
- Usually, these issues are discussed at case conference. 




Expert must file Expert Report with his expert evidence
Parties must agree on list of issues and facts for experts to rely on in preparing Expert Report 
O 12 r 4(1) 
- Parties must agree on list of issues to be referred for expert evidence and the common set of agreed or assumed facts that the experts are to rely on 
=> O 12 r 4(4): As far as possible, issues must be expressed in the form of questions which can be answered with “yes” or “no” 

If parties cannot agree => Court will decide on list 
O 12 r 4(3)
- If there is no agreement as mentioned in O 12 r 4(1), the Court must decide the list of issues and the common set of agreed or assumed facts 

List of issues and facts must be approved by Court => Expert Report confined to the list 
O 12 r 4(2)
- List of issues and common set of agreed or assumed facts must be approved by Court and unless Court otherwise orders, expert evidence must be confined to the list and set 

Expert Report 
Form of Expert Report
Report signed by expert + Report exhibited in affidavit
O 12 r 5(1)
- Expert evidence must be given in a report signed by the expert and exhibited in an affidavit made by the expert 

Contents of Expert Report 
Contents in O 12 r 5(2)
O 12 r 5(2) 
- Expert’s report must include: 
a. Expert’s qualifications: The expert’s qualifications showing that he or she has the requisite specialised knowledge on the issues referred to him or her;
b. Expert understanding of his duty to the Court: The expert’s statement that he or she understands his or her duty is to assist the Court in the matters within his or her expertise and on the issues referred to him or her and that such duty to the Court overrides any obligation to the person from whom he or she receives instructions or by whom he or she is paid;
c. Issues and common facts relied on: The issues referred to the expert and the common set of agreed or assumed facts that he or she relied on;
d. List of materials relied on: A list of the materials that the expert relied on and including only extracts of the materials which are necessary to understand the report;
e. Qualifications of test subjects: Where the materials include tests, experiments or the collection or analysis of data, the names and qualifications of the persons who did the tests, experiments or the collection or analysis of data and whether they did so under the expert’s supervision or guidance;
f. Summary of different opinions and reasons for expert’s opinion: Where there is a range of opinion on the matters dealt with in the report —
(i) A summary of the range of opinion; and
(ii) The reasons for the expert’s opinion;
g. Statement of belief of correctness: A statement of belief of correctness of the expert’s opinion;
h. Conclusions on issues and reasons: The conclusions reached on the issues referred to the expert and the reasons to support the conclusions.

Expert’s Duties to the Court 
Paramount duty to Court to assist in matters within his expertise 
O 12 r 1(2)
- An expert has the duty to assist the Court in the matters within his expertise and on the issues referred to him 

Expert’s duty to Court overrides any obligation to parties 
O 12 r 1(3)
- The expert’s duty to the Court overrides any obligation to the person from whom the expert receives instructions or by whom the expert is paid 

Principle 1: Solicitors have a duty to bring expert’s attention to their duty to the Court 
Pacific Recreation Pte Ltd v S Y Technology Inc and another appeal 
- Solicitors have a duty to remind the expert that: 
a. Expert’s duty is to the court
b. Expert evidence should be the independent product of the expert and be uninfluenced as to the form or content by the exigencies of litigation 
c. Expert should not conceal any adverse opinions which have come to his knowledge 
d. Expert should not express any opinion on issues outside his area of competence 
e. If expert can only come to a provisional or qualified opinion, it must be stated clearly in the expert report along with an explanation of the reasons for the uncertainty 

Principle 2: Guidelines for expert witness giving expert evidence on foreign law 
Pacific Recreation Pte Ltd v S Y Technology Inc and another appeal 
- Expert must: 
1. Inform the Court of the relevant contents of the foreign law and identify statutes or other legislation and explain the foreign Court’s approach to their construction 
2. Identify judgements or other authorities, explaining what status they have as sources of foreign law 
3. Where there is no authority directly in point, assist the Court in making a finding as to what the foreign Court’s ruling would be if the issue was to arise for decision there 
- For construction of foreign document: Expert’s role is to prove the rules of construction of foreign law. The Court will interpret the document in accordance with those rules 

Different role of expert witness and counsel 
CES v International Air Transport Association 
- Experts should place contents of foreign law before the Court and give an opinion as to the effect of the law 
- However, experts should not give opinions on the conclusions which the Court ought to draw, such conclusions should be the subject of counsel’s submissions 

Principle 3: Court should not blindly accept uncontradicted expert evidence => Court must still examine premises of uncontradicted expert evidence 
Poh Soon Kiat v Desert Palace Inc 
- Rule of prudence: 
a. Court must not blindly accept expert evidence on any matter merely because that evidence was not contradicted 
b. However, Court is also not entitled to substitute his own views for those of an uncontradicted expert’s evidence 
c. Court should weigh the uncontradicted evidence and assess if it flies in the face of proven extrinsic facts relevant to the matter 
d. Court should also examine the premises of the expert’s evidence and his reasoning process 

Principle 4: Experts must promptly disclose the existence of any significant relationship with any interested party 
HSBC Institutional Trust Services (Singapore) Ltd v Toshin Development Singapore Pte Ltd 
- Experts must promptly disclose the existence of any significant relationship with any interested party 
a. Includes situation where expert has been approached by one of the directly-interested parties to give a professional opinion on another matter
- Non-compliance: Failure by an expert to voluntarily disclose in a timely manner may give rise to serious concerns of apparent or actual bias 

Independent witnesses, interested non-parties and independent counsel (Amicus Curae)
- Court invite independent witness to give evidence 
- Court invite interested non-parties to give evidence 
- Court invite independent counsel to give evidence 

Evidence from Independent Witnesses 
Court may invite independent witness to give evidence 
Give evidence orally or by affidavit 
O 9 r 22(1)
- Court may order, on its own accord, a person not named as a witness for any party to give evidence orally or by way of affidavit as an independent witness 

Court may direct cross-examination of independent witness
O 9 r 22(2)
- Court may give directions for cross examination of an independent witness 

Evidence from non-parties 
Court may invite interested non-parties to assist in issues 
Assist by giving views in writing on specific issues 
O 9 r 22(3) 
- Court may invite any natural person or entity who has an interest or is able to assist in the issue to give his or its views in writing on specific issues

Interested person is not subject to cross-examination and need not attend hearing
O 9 r 22(4)
- Interested person or entity is not subject to cross-examination and need not attend hearing 

Reasonable expenses of non-party paid by either party
O 9 r 22(5)
- Court may order one or more of the parties to pay for reasonable expenses incurred by an independent witness or an interested person or entity 

Evidence from Independent Counsel (amicus curae)
Court may invite independent counsel to assist on specific issues of law 
O 9 r 23(1)
- Court may on its own accord appoint one or more independent counsel to assist the Court in any matter on specific issues of law 

Independent counsel: Solicitor, academic teaching law, person who has special legal knowledge 
O 9 r 23(2)
- Independent counsel may be: 
a. A solicitor; 
b. An academic involved in the teaching of law at present or in the past; OR 
c. A person who has special knowledge or experience in any area of law

Court must give directions to independent counsel on issues of law, filing and service of written submissions, attendance in Court 
O 9 r 23(3)
- Court must give directions to independent counsel on: 
a. Specific issues of law to be addressed by independent counsel;
b. Filing and service of written submissions by independent counsel and the parties; and
c. Independent counsel’s attendance in Court to make oral submissions 



*C. ORDER TO ATTEND COURT/PRODUCE DOCUMENTS – SUBPOENAS 
- TLDR: Party can request for an order to compel any person who is in a position to give relevant evidence to come forward and give oral evidence or produce documents 

Step 1: Party must request for Registrar to issue an “Order to Attend Court” or “Order to Produce Documents”   
Party must file Order to Attend Court/Produce Documents in Form 29 when requesting Registrar to issue it
O 15 r 4(1)
- Parties may request Registrar to issue an order to attend Court or an order to produce documents in Form 29 to any witness, stating whether the witness is to give oral evidence or to produce documents or both

Para 76 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021
- Party must file order to attend court in the manner and form set out in Form 29 of Appendix A of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021

Contents of Order to Attend Court/Produce Documents in O 15 r 4(2)
O 15 r 4(2)
- Order to attend Court or Order to produce documents must state:
* The requesting party,
* Whether the witness is to give oral evidence or to produce documents or both.

Step 2: If approved, Registrar will seal the Order to Attend Court => Order to Attend Court will be considered issued when sealed
Order to attend Court issued when sealed 
Para 76 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021
- An order to attend Court is issued when it is sealed by an officer of the Registry 

Step 3: Requesting party must serve Order to Attend Court/Produce Documents on the person 
Requesting Party must personally serve the order at least 28 days before hearing
O 15 r 4(3)
- Order to attend Court or an Order to produce documents must be served by the requesting party on the witness by personal service in Singapore at least 28 days before the hearing 

Service out of jurisdiction is NOT allowed: Cannot subpoena a witness who is out of jurisdiction
- O 15 r 4(3) expressly provides that Order to Attend Court/Produce Documents can only be personally served on a witness in Singapore 
- Service out of jurisdiction is not allowed => Implication is that you cannot subpoena a witness who is out of jurisdiction 

Step 4: Any person/Registrar’s own accord can apply to Registrar to revoke the Order to Attend Court/Produce Documents --> this is when the Registrar can consider the relevance, necessity and materiality of documents
Any person/Registrar’s own accord make application to Registrar => Registrar can revoke Order
O 15 r 4(4)
- Upon application by any person or the Registrar’s own motion => Registrar may revoke an Order to attend Court or an Order to produce documents or both 
- Can the witness/non-party himself apply to revoke the order? Case Lao Holdings suggests that the equivalent rule under the old ROC would permit the witness himself to make an application to set aside the subpoena. The rule has not changed from the old ROC, so this same principle should apply. 

Principle applying to all Orders to Attend Court/Produce Documents: Subpoena should not be used frivolously or to embarrass the party being subpoenaed 
Ong Jane Rebecca v Lim Lie Hoa 
- Subpoena should not be used frivolously, in a scandalous manner or issued without basis or reason so as to embarrass the party being subpoenaed 

Principle applying to Order to Attend Court (subpoena to testify): should be granted if witnesses were disinterested and in a position to give relevant and crucial oral and documentary evidence 
Basil Herman Anthony v Premier Security Cooperative Ltd 
- Outcome of the case turned on key events which witnesses were in a position to give oral and documentary evidence
- Witnesses were disinterested parties and were more likely to give credible evidence 

Principles applying to Order to Produce Documents (subpoena for documents) 
Principle 1: Orders to Produce Documents must satisfy 2 requirements
The Lao People’s Democratic Republic v Sanum Investments Ltd 
- Requirement 1: What is sought in a subpoena to produce documents must be relevant, material and necessary for the fair disposal of the matter 
- Requirement 2: Subpoena to produce documents would be an improper use of the procedure if it amounts to an order for discovery (i.e. it cannot amount to an order for discovery, it must have sufficient precision in identifying particular documents, rather than being too generic)

Principle 2: Subpoena for production of documents would be set aside if for clearly irrelevant documents or a collateral purpose 
BVU v BVX 
- Held: Documents sought were unnecessary for the determination of the case and the subpoena was a backdoor to reopen an arbitrated dispute and would amount to an abuse of process 

The Lao People’s Democratic Republic v Sanum Investments Ltd 
- Non-party was compelled by subpoena to produce documents in aid of arbitration 
- Issues: 
a. Whether subpoena lacked sufficient precision in identifying the particular documents required to be produced i.e. whether it would amount to an attempt to obtain discovery against a 3rd party
b. Whether documents sought were sufficiently relevant and material to the arbitration and whether they were necessary for the disposal of the matter
- Held: The documents sought were highly relevant and material to the issues in the arbitration => Subpoena was sufficiently precise to enable the party being subpoenaed to know what was required of him

Step 5: Dissatisfied party can appeal against Registrar’s decision on the subpoena
Dissatisfied party can appeal against Registrar’s decision => Appeal to GDHC or District Judge 
O 15 r 4(5)
- Any party who is dissatisfied with any decision of the Registrar may apply to a Judge sitting in the General Division or a District Judge for a review of the Registrar’s decision 

Dissatisfied party must make appeal application by summons supported by affidavit within 14 days after Registrar’s decision 
Appeal process 
O 15 r 4(6)
- Appellant must make application by summons supported by an affidavit within 14 days after the Registrar’s initial decision 

Order to Attend Court/Produce Documents will have effect until conclusion of appeal hearing
O 15 r 4(7)
- Order to attend Court or Order to produce documents continues to have effect until conclusion of hearing 

Step 6: Instructions for person subject to Order to Attend Court/Produce Documents 
Person subject to Order entitled to claim reasonable compensation for time and expense from requesting party 
O 15 r 4(9) 
- A witness who complies with an Order to attend Court or an Order to produce documents is entitled to claim reasonable compensation for his time and expenses in complying with the order from the requesting party upon request 

Non-compliance with Order: Liable for contempt of Court 
- Liable for contempt of Court

Special instructions for Order to produce documents
Implied undertaking by requesting party in Riddick v Thames Board Mills Ltd applies to documents obtained 
- Requesting party is under an implied undertaking to not use documents produced for any purpose other than for pursuing the action 
- Requesting party cannot use documents produced for any collateral or ulterior purpose 
Riddick v Thames Board Mills Ltd
- Parties will be taken to have given an implied undertaking to the Court to not use documents produced under an Order to produce documents for a collateral or ulterior purpose
- Non-compliance: Amounts to a breach of undertaking given to the Court and can result in liability for contempt of Court. Breach can be restrained by injunction too. 
Reason:
- Riddick undertaking balances between the interests of: 
a. Full and complete disclosure in the interests of justice 
b. Protection against intrusion of privacy 

Person subject to Order to Produce Documents need NOT attend Court personally if documents produced are in accordance with order
O 15 r 4(8)
- A witness served with an Order to produce documents need not attend court personally if he ensures that all the documents required are produced in accordance with the order to produce documents
- 

Order for Pre-Trial Examination 
- If party needs to record evidence of any witness in or out of Singapore before a trial => Can apply for a pre-trial examination for the Court to examine the witness prior to trial 

Court’s power to order Pre-trial examination 
Party establish that it is necessary in interests of justice to record witness’s evidence before trial => Party may apply to Court for order for pre-trial examination of any witness (in Singapore or outside)
O 9 r 24(1)
- Where it is necessary in the interests of justice to record the evidence of any witness in or out of Singapore before a trial, a party may apply to the Court to make an order for pre-trial examination 

Rules governing Pre-trial examination 
Pre-trial examination rules same as normal trial rules
O 9 r 24(6)
- A pre?trial examination in Singapore must be before a Judge or the Registrar and must be conducted according to the rules governing trials

Rules for Order for pre-trial examination same as rules for Order to Attend Court/Produce Documents
O 9 r 24(7)
- Where an order for pre?trial examination in Singapore has been made, the attendance of the witness before the examiner in Singapore and the production by the witness of any document at the examination may be enforced by an order to attend court and an order to produce documents in like manner as the attendance of a witness and the production by a witness of a document at a trial may be enforced.

Pre-trial examination outside Singapore must be conducted by Singapore-Court appointed examiner
- A pre?trial examination outside Singapore must be conducted by the examiner appointed by the Court and in the manner directed by the Court and the examiner must not do anything that is contrary to the law of that place

Procedure to apply for Pre-trial examination
Applicant must file affidavit showing contents in O 9 r 24(2)
O 9 r 24(2)
- Party who applies for an order for pre-trial examination must file an affidavit showing: 
a. the witness’ evidence is necessary for the party’s case;
b. the other parties do not agree that the evidence of that witness be given in an affidavit without cross?examination; and
c. the witness will not be able or willing to attend the trial or to give evidence by live video or live television link, or the witness’ age or health makes it likely that he or she will pass away before or become incapable of testifying at the trial.

Additional procedure for Pre-trial examination outside Singapore
Additional affidavit contents in O 9 r 24(3)
O 9 r 24(3)
- For pre-trial examination conducted outside of Singapore, the affidavit must also state: 
a. the place where the pre?trial examination is to be conducted;
b. that the law of that place allows the pre?trial examination to be conducted in that place;
c. the particulars and remuneration of the examiner who will be conducting the pre?trial examination; and
d. the rules that will apply to the pre?trial examination.

Letter of requested required by foreign jurisdiction
O 9 r 24(4): If letter of request is required by foreign jurisdiction 
- If the law of the place for the pre?trial examination outside of Singapore requires the issue of a letter of request to the relevant authorities for the evidence of the person in that jurisdiction to be taken, the party who applies for the order may include a request for the issue of a letter of request and such application can only be made in the General Division even if the proceedings are commenced in the State Courts.

O 9 r 24(5)
- For the purposes of paragraph (4), the party obtaining the order must prepare and file in the Registrar:
a. The letter of request which must be —
(i) In a case where the jurisdiction in which the evidence is to be taken is a jurisdiction to which the Hague Evidence Convention applies — in the current version of the applicable Recommended Model Form; or
(ii) In any other case — in Form 17, with such variations and in compliance with such requirements as may be required by the jurisdiction in which the evidence is to be taken or by the order; and
b. An undertaking in Form 18 signed by the party or the party’s solicitor to be responsible personally for all expenses incurred by an issuing authority or a transmitting authority in respect of the letter of request and, on receiving due notification of the amount of those expenses, to pay that amount to the issuing authority or transmitting authority and to produce a receipt for the payment to the proper officer of the Registry.



*D. ATTENDANCE OF WITNESSES AT TRIAL 
- For Witness’s AEIC to be received as evidence => Witness needs to attend trial 

*General rule: If witness does not attend trial, his AEIC cannot be received in evidence 
AEIC maker must attend trial for cross-examination so that his AEIC to be received as evidence => Unless parties otherwise agree
O 15 r 16(3) 
- AEIC may not be used if the maker does not attend Court for cross-examination 
- Unless the parties otherwise agree  

Note: Filing and service of AEIC does not automatically mean that AEIC can be used => Must attend court for cross-examination then can admit AEIC as evidence

*What if witness is confined in prison? 
Requesting party must use Order to Attend Court and provide Registrar issuing Order with details of the prison
O 15 r 4(10) 
- If witness is a person confined in prison and requesting party requests Order to attend Court to be served on witness => Requesting party must include in his letter (the request being made to the Registrar to issue a Form 29 order):
* The name of the prison the witness is confined in 
* The reasons for requiring the witness to attend Court 
* An undertaking to pay upon request the costs to be incurred by the prison in complying with the order to attend Court 

Registrar will issue Order to Attend Court with details 
O 15 r 4(11)
- Order to attend Court addressed to a person confined in prison must include the following words: 
“This order to attend court is sufficient authority as an order under section 38 of the Prisons Act for the Superintendent to produce the named person in court at the time and place stated. 

The requesting party undertakes to pay upon requests the costs to be incurred by the prison in complying with the order to attend court.” 

Requesting party must serve Order to Attend Court on Superintendent of prison at least 14 days before hearing  
O 15 r 4(12)
- The order to attend Court in O 15 r 4(11) may be served on the Superintendent of the prison by ordinary service and must be served at least 14 days before the hearing 

S38 of Prisons Act 
- Whenever presence of person confined in prison is required in any civil or criminal court => Court may issue written order to Superintendent requiring production of the person before court at a time and place named => Superintendent must cause the person named to be brought up and must provide for the person’s safe custody at the time and place named in the order 

*What if witness wants to attend trial by live video or television link evidence? 
Step 1: Requesting party must trigger Court’s discretion by satisfying any ground under s62A(1) EA:
S62A(1) of Evidence Act
- A person may, with permission of the Court, give evidence through a live video or live television link in any proceedings other than proceedings in a criminal matter or proceedings for contempt of court (i.e. can only be used for civil proceedings) if: 
a. Ground 1: Witness below 18 years’ old; OR
b. Ground 2: It was expressly agreed between parties; OR 
c. Ground 3: Witness is outside Singapore; OR 
d. Ground 4: It is in interests of justice. 

Step 2: Requesting Party must satisfy Court that the circumstances warrant the Court to grant permission
