If Ground 3: Witness outside Singapore => Court will have regard to circumstances under s62A(2)
S62A(2) of Evidence Act 
- In considering whether to grant permission for a witness outside Singapore to give evidence by live or live television link under s62A, the Court is to have regard to all the circumstances of the case including the following: 
a. Reasons why witness unable to give evidence in Singapore; 
b. Administrative and technical facilities and arrangements for witness to give evidence overseas; 
c. Any unfair prejudice to any party to the proceedings; 
d. Any other matter prescribed by rules made under s62A(9) 

If any Ground => Court will have regard to s62A(5) – Fairness to parties
S62A(5) of Evidence Act
- Fairness to parties: The Court is not to make an order under s62A or include a particular provision in such an order, if to do so would be inconsistent with the Court’s duty to ensure that the proceedings are conducted fairly to the parties to the proceedings

Principle 1: Court would consider a few other factors under case law 
- Court would consider factors such as: (Sonica Industries Ltd) 
a. Reasons why the witness cannot give evidence in Singapore
b. Whether evidence of witnesses were material to the issue at hand 
c. Efforts taken to secure witness’s presence in Singapore 

Principle 2: Applicants should not blindly cite the COVID-19 pandemic as a reason why a witness is unable to testify physically at trial (Wang Xiaopu v Koh Mui Lee)
- COVID-19 situation has stabilized and close attention must be paid to the precise facts explaining exactly why the witness is unable to travel, mere generalizations about travel disruptions brought on by COVID-19 are insufficient 
- Risk of contracting COVID-19 is irrelevant unless it was shown that the witness had health-related issues that put him at risk of serious complications if he contracted COVID-19 

Principle 3: Unwillingness of witness to travel does not preclude grant of permission => Court may still grant permission if other circumstances exist 
- Even though s62A(2) seems to suggest that the unwillingness of witness to travel is not a relevant reason to grant permission to give evidence by live video or live television link => Recent cases seem to suggest that the Court is willing to still grant permission to give evidence by live video or television link if:
a. Party has no control of the witness (Anil Singh Gurm) 
b. Witness’s evidence is material (Anil Singh Gurm) 
c. There will be unfair prejudice if permission is not granted and no unfair prejudice if permission is granted (Sonica Industries Ltd) 
Examples of case law:
Permission granted: Even though reason is based on witness’s unwillingness => There will be no unfair prejudice is permission is granted and there will be unfair prejudice if permission is not granted 
Sonica Industries Ltd v Fu Yu Manufacturing Ltd 
- Pf sought leave for 2 witnesses to give evidence by live video
- Held: Leave granted in respect of 1 witness 
1. Permission granted for 1st witness: 
a. There was no way Pf could compel the 1st witness to travel to Singapore to give evidence and Court was satisfied that Pf had made the necessary attempts to secure his presence but without any success 
b. 1st witness’s evidence is material and relevant and there would be prejudice to the Pf if permission was not granted 
c. Df would not be prejudiced if permission was granted
(i) Pf identified the particular facts and issues which could be proved by 1st witness
(ii) Statement of 1st witness’s evidence had been furnished to the Dfs and Dfs would not be taken by surprise
(iii) Dfs can cross-examine the 1st witness through live video
(iv) 1st witness’s evidence is not very complicated or technical  
2. Permission not granted for 2nd witness:
a. 2nd witness’s evidence was peripheral to the main issue and no prejudice if permission not granted
Peter Rogers May v Pinder Lillian Gek Lian
- If sufficient reason is given why the actual physical presence of foreign witnesses cannot be effected, a court should lean in favour of permitting video-linked evidence in lieu of the normal rule of physical testimony. Sufficient reason ought to be a relatively low threshold to overcome and should be assessed with a liberal and pragmatic latitude. 
- If a witness is not normally resident within a jurisdiction, that may itself afford a sufficient reason with a view to minimising costs. 
- On the other hand, if for instance the evidence of an important foreign witness cannot be voluntarily obtained by video link, this could tip the balance in favour of hearing the matter in the foreign jurisdiction where the witness resides so the witness can be compelled to give evidence there. Even then, the importance of that witness personally giving evidence as a factor may not be critical if deposition taking is available. 
- The relative gravity of the witness personally giving evidence must invariably be weighed and measured against the nature and relevance of the proposed evidence

Permission granted: Witness subject to travel restriction + Difficulties obtaining permission even though steps were taken to do so + Poor health 
Permission NOT granted: Witness looking for convenience 
Bachmeer Capital Limited v Ong Chi Ching 
- Applicant sought leave for 2 witnesses to give evidence by live video 
- Held: Leave granted in respect of 1 witness 
1. Permission granted for 1st witness
a. 1st witness was subject to a travel restriction. There were difficulties obtaining permission from relevant Chinese authorities even though steps were taken to do so.  
b. 1st witness was in poor health 
2. Permission not granted for 2nd witness
a. 2nd witness was able to attend trial physical but was simply hoping to have a more convenient means of testifying 
Anil Singh Gurm v J S Yeh & Co 
- Applicant sought leave to give evidence by video link testimony 
- Held: Leave granted 
1. Generally s62A only covers situations where a witness is unable to physically give evidence due to circumstances outside their control => Currently, witness was unwilling to enter Singapore for fear of arrest => However, this did not preclude the Court from granting permission 

2. Court will consider whether the party has control over the witness => If party has no control over the witness and has made all reasonable attempts to secure the witness’s attendance => Court is more likely to grant permission 

3. Witness can give material evidence on key factual issues => Denial of permission would mean no evidence would be adduced => Appellant would be prejudiced if he was deprived of opportunity to call witness but Respondent would not be prejudiced if witness gives evidence by video link => Court is more likely to grant permission 

- Note Policy Considerations: 
1. Party’s right to access to justice and his entitlement to put forward the best possible case and relevant evidence
2. Court should not facilitate a person’s attempt to evade justice 

- Paragraph 46(HC): “a witness cannot raise a valid reason for not attending in person, and instead for giving evidence by video link, his professed desire to avoid the normal processes of the law.”
Permission NOT granted: witnesses unwilling + no steps were taken by the applicant for special permission from ICA
Sahara Energy International Pte Ltd v Chu Said Thong 
- Applicant sought leave for 2 witnesses to give evidence by video-link
- Held: Permission not granted 
1. The 2 witnesses were unwilling but not unable to come to Singapore to give evidence => Even though the applicant cited COVID-19 travel related restrictions, the applicant did not even try to apply for special permission from the Singapore authorities for the 2 witnesses to enter Singapore 
2. Court made the following orders: 
a. Applicant will apply to ICA for permission for 2 witnesses to come to Singapore and if they need to be quarantined, they will serve their 14 days’ quarantine which should be completed before commencement of trial 
b. Applicant will be given liberty to apply for live video evidence in the event the 2 witnesses are still in quarantine when the trial commences 
c. Unless Applicant takes the above steps, the applicant’s claim will be dismissed with costs ordered against the applicant 
Permission NOT granted: court cautioned against blindly citing the pandemic as a reason as to why a witness is unable to testify physically at trial
Wang Xiaopu v Koh Mui Lee 
- Applicant sought leave for Wang to give evidence by video-link
- Held: Permission not granted 
1. Wang cited COVID-19 travel disruptions => However, COVID-19 situation has stabilized and mere generalizations about travel disruptions brought on by COVID-19 pandemic are insufficient => Need to pay attention to the precise facts explaining exactly why the witness is unable to travel
2. Wang cited substantial risk of contracting COVID-19 => However, it is irrelevant unless it was shown that Wang had health-related issues that put her at risk of serious complications if she contracted COVID-19 
3. Even though safety issues are important, Court will balance safety issues with the need to ensure justice and equity of the case 
4. Court will not allow parties to manipulate prevailing situation to their advantage and compromise a fair hearing 

Step 3: Court can grant permission for witness to give evidence by live video or television link subject to certain orders 
S62A(3) of Evidence Act 
- Court may, in granting permission under s62A(1), make an order on all or any of the following matters:
a. Person to be with witness (accompanying them) 
b. Person to NOT be with witness while he is giving evidence
c. Persons in courtroom who must be seen and/or heard by witness
d. Persons in courtroom who must NOT be seen/heard by witness
e. Witness must be seen/heard by persons in courtroom
f. Stages of proceedings during which a specified part of the order is to have effect
g. Method of operating live video or live television link system including compliance with such minimum technical standards as may be determined by the Chief Justice 
h. Any other order the Court considers necessary in the interests of justice 

Step 4: Court can revoke, suspend or vary order granted under certain circumstances 
S62A(4) of Evidence Act 
- Court may revoke, suspend or vary an order under s62A if:
a. Live video stops working and unreasonable delay before it works or becomes available; OR
b. Necessary for fairness to parties; OR
c. Necessary for witness to do something physically in Court: so that the witness can identify a person or a thing or so that the witness can participate in or view a demonstration or an experiment; OR 
d. Part of proceeding is being heard outside a courtroom; OR 
e. Material change in circumstances; OR 
f. Any conditions prescribed by rules made s62A(9) for the purposes of s62A are met 

*Witness located overseas requires letter of request to relevant authorities for evidence to be given overseas 
Requesting party can apply using Form 30 for issue of letter of request 
O 15 r 5(1)
- Where application is made for permission for witness outside Singapore to give evidence by live video and the laws of the jurisdiction where the witness is located requires the issue of a letter of request to the relevant authorities of that jurisdiction for such evidence to be given => Application may be made for an order in Form 30 for issue of the letter of request 

Application made by Originating Summons and supported by Affidavit 
O 15 r 5(3)
- Application must be made by OS and supported by an affidavit setting out basis for application and enclosing a copy of each document the applicant intends to file in Registry under O 15 r 5(4)

Application must follow O 15 r 5(5), (6), (7) and (8)
O 15 r 5(4)
- Application for letter of request must follow O 15 r 5(5), (6), (7) and (8)
(5)  The party obtaining the order must prepare the letter of request and file it in the Registry, and the letter must be:
a. In a case where the jurisdiction in which the evidence is to be given is a jurisdiction to which the Hague Evidence Convention applies — in the current version of the applicable Recommended Model Form; or
b. In any other case — in Form 17, with such variations as may be required by the jurisdiction in which the evidence is to be given or by the order.
(6)  A letter of request filed under paragraph (5), or a document attached to the letter, must be accompanied by a translation of the letter or document in a language specified by the jurisdiction in which the evidence is to be given, unless that jurisdiction accepts the letter or document in English.

(7)  Every translation filed under paragraph (6) must be certified by the person making it to be a correct translation; and the certificate must contain a statement of that person’s full name, address and qualifications for making the translation.

(8)  The party obtaining the order must, when the party files in the Registry the documents mentioned in paragraphs (5), (6) and (7), also file in the Registry an undertaking in Form 18 signed by the party or the party’s solicitor to be responsible personally for all expenses incurred by an issuing authority or a transmitting authority in respect of the letter of request and, on receiving due notification of the amount of those expenses, to pay that amount to the issuing authority or transmitting authority and to produce a receipt for the payment to the proper officer of the Registry.

*Exclusion of witness from courtroom prior to giving evidence 
Non-party witness must remain outside Courtroom until he is called to give evidence --> to avoid contamination of his evidence
O 15 r 4(13)
- Witness who is not a party and who has not given his evidence must remain outside the courtroom until he is called into Court
O 15 r 4(14)
- Witness who has given his evidence may remain in or leave the courtroom 

*E. ABSENCE OF PARTY AT TRIAL 
General rule: All parties must attend trial in person (either party or party’s solicitor) 
O 15 r 3(1)
- All parties must attend the hearing of any matter in person (if they are not legally represented) or by a solicitor (if they are legally represented) 
- Appearance may be in person or by counsel (Lam Hwa Engineering & Trading v Yang Qiang)

Consequences of both sides’ failure to attend hearing: Dismissal of claim and any counterclaim
O 15 r 8(3)
- If none of the parties attends Court when the trial begins, the Court may dismiss the claim and any counterclaim 

Consequences of one party’s failure to attend hearing (e.g. claimant’s failure): Dismiss party’s application or make any appropriate order against the party
O 15 r 3(2)
- If a party fails to attend hearing, Court may dismiss the party’s application, action or appeal or make any other appropriate order against the party 

Consequences of one side’s failure to attend hearing (e.g. defendant’s failure): Proceed with trial, give judgement against absentee, dismiss claim of absentee or make any appropriate order
O 15 r 8(4)
- If one of the parties does not attend Court when the trial begins, the Court may proceed with the trial or give judgement against or dismiss the claim of the absent party or make any other appropriate order

Court can dispense with parties’ attendance , except in certain cases (IN THESE 2 CASES IT MUST BE IN PERSON): 
O 15 r 3(3)
- Court may dispense with the attendance of the parties or their solicitors and decide any matter after reading the documents filed without the need for oral arguments except: 
a. Where oral evidence is given at any part of the proceedings (including any part of a trial of an action), unless all the parties consent  (to dispense attendance)
b. Where the hearing of the matter is required under written law or an order of the Court to be advertised or published in any newspaper or the Gazette 




Examination of witness 

Rules relating to EIC, Cross-Examination and Re-Examination
Definitions of “EIC”, “Cross-examination”, “Re-examination”
S139 of EA
- Examination-in-chief: Examination of a witness by the party who called him 
- Cross-examination: Examination of a witness by the adverse party  
- Re-examination: Where a witness has been cross-examined by adverse party and is then examined by the party who called him

Order of EIC, cross-examination and re-examination 
S140(1) of EA
- Witnesses are to be first examined-in-chief => Adverse party cross-examines => Party calling them re-examines 

Examination-in-chief
EIC cannot be on matters outside of AEIC unless it is a new matter after AEIC made 
O 15 r 16(4)
- Witness may not give a EIC on matters not contained in his AEIC 
- Unless it is a new matter which occurred after the date of making of the AEIC

Cross-examination
Cross-examination must relate to relevant facts 
S140(2) of EA
- Examination and Cross-examination must relate to relevant facts

Cross-examination need not be confined to facts testified in EIC
S140(2) of EA
- Cross-examination need not be confined to the facts to which the witness testified on his EIC

Re-examination 
Re-examination is for explaining matters referred to in cross-examination 
S140(3)
- Re-examination must be directed to the explanation of matter referred to in cross-examination 

Court can permit introduction of new matter in re-examination => Adverse party will have right to cross-examine the new matter
S140(3)
- If Court permits a new matter to be introduced in re-examination => The adverse party may further cross-examine upon the matter 

Miscellaneous Issue: Leading questions in EIC, re-examination and cross-examination 
Definition of leading questions
S143 of Evidence Act
- Any question suggesting the answer which the person putting it wishes or expects to receive or suggesting disputed facts as to which the witness is to testify 

If leading questions are asked in EIC/Re-examination => Opposing party can object => Witness need not answer unless Court allows for the leading question  
S144(1) of Evidence Act
- Leading questions must not be asked in an EIC or in a re-examination if the adverse party objects except with the permission of the Court 

Court shall allow leading questions (1) Which are introductory or undisputed or (2) Which have been already sufficiently proved
S144(2) of Evidence Act 
Court shall permit leading questions as to matters:
* which are introductory or undisputed or 
* which have in its opinion been already sufficiently proved

Leading questions can be asked in Cross-examination subject to 2 qualifications 
S145(1) of Evidence Act 
- Leading questions may be asked in cross-examination subject to the following qualifications: 
a. Qualification 1: Question must not be put into the mouth of the witness the very words which he is to echo back again; and 
b. Qualification 2: Question must not assume that facts have been proved which have not been proved, or that particular answers have been given contrary to the fact 

Court can exercise discretion to prohibit leading questions 
S145(2) of Evidence Act
- Court in its discretion may prohibit leading questions from being put to a witness who shows a strong interest or bias in favor of the cross-examining party 

Miscellaneous Issue: Preparing witnesses for examination 
Ernest Ferdinand Perez De La Sala v Compania De Navegacion Palomar SA 
- Key principle: Witness’s evidence must be his own independent testimony 
1. Solicitor preparing witness must not allow other persons, including the solicitor, to supplant or supplement the witness’s testimony 
2. Preparation should not be too lengthy or repetitive as over time, oblique comments, non-verbal cues and the general shape of questioning might influence the witness to adopt answers which he did not believe to be the truth (preparation exercise itself carries an inherent danger)
3. Preparation should not be done in groups as that would exacerbate the risk that witnesses might change their testimony to bring it in line with what they believed the best answer to be 

Note:
- Lawyer prepared a script containing certain questions and answers for the witness and it was prepared in a group setting => It casts serious doubt on whether the witness’s evidence is his own 
- If witness made a mistake in practicing his answer => Lawyers would point out discrepancies and lead the witness in answering the question “correctly => Serious doubt as to the witness’s independence 
- However, there is nothing wrong with: 
a. Having practice runs 
b. Lawyer informing witness that his answer contradicts his AEIC and to ask the witness to consider what the true answer is 
c. Lawyer informing the witness of the questions which the opposing counsel might ask regarding the possible inconsistency 

Order of proceedings at trial 
Default Order of Proceedings => Unless Court gives directions 
O 15 r 8(5)
- Court must have control over the order of proceedings and may give the appropriate directions before or during the trial and subject to such directions, the following order of proceedings set out in O 15 r 8(6) to O 15 r 8(13) is to apply

Preliminary: Opening statements and AEIC need not be read out
O 15 r 8(6)
- The opening statements and AEICs need not be read out in Court 

Step 1: Claimant testifies then claimant’s witness testifies 
O 15 r 8(7)
- The claimant must begin and testify => Claimant’s witness testifies 

Step 2: Defendant and other parties cross-examine claimant and claimant’s witness
O 15 r 8(8)
- Defendant and any other parties may cross-examine claimant and the claimant’s witnesses 

Step 3: Re-examination of claimant and claimant’s witness 

Step 4: Defendant decide if it wants to submit “No Case to Answer” 
Defendant must show that the evidence in claimant’s case had not made out a case requiring the Defendant to make a Defense 
O 15 r 8(11)
- At conclusion of claimant’s case, defendant may make a submission of “No Case to Answer” in that 
* The evidence in the claimant’s case has not made out a case requiring the defendant to make the defendant’s defense 

Effect of making submission of “No Case to Answer”
O 15 r 8(11)
- Submission of “No Case to Answer” is made on the basis that the following apply in relation to the defendant’s submission: 
a. Defendant will not give any evidence: Defendant will not be giving evidence by himself or through the defendant’s witnesses even if the Court rules against the defendant 
b. Defendant will not pursue counterclaim on same facts: Defendant does not have a counterclaim arising out of substantially the same facts as the claimants case or, if the defendant has such a counterclaim, the defendant withdraws it
c. Where there are multiple defendants: If there is more than one defendant and not all the defendants make the submission of “No case to answer” and the Court decides not to rule immediately on the submission of “No case to answer”, the defendant who makes the submission cannot rely on or make any submissions on the evidence given by any other party and cannot cross?examine any party or witness who gives evidence after the submission was made;
d. Defendant can rely on defendant’s expert evidence if already given: The defendant who makes the submission of “No case to answer” may rely on the evidence of the defendant’s expert and any other expert if those experts have already given evidence as a panel of experts during the claimant’s case;
e. The defendant who makes the submission of “No case to answer” and is unsuccessful may make submissions on the costs of the action.

Step 5: If defendant does not want to make submission of “No Case to Answer” => Defendant testifies then defendant’s witness testifies 
O 15 r 8(9)
- When the claimant and the claimant’s witnesses have completed giving their evidence, subject to paragraph (11), the defendant must begin and testify before the defendant’s witness

Step 6: Claimant and other parties cross-examine defendant and defendant’s witness
O 15 r 8(10)
- Claimant and any other parties may cross-examine defendant and defendant’s witness 

Step 7: Re-examination of defendant and defendant’s witness

Step 8: Court can give decision immediately after hearing or at a later date 
O 15 r 8(13)
- Court may give its decision immediately after the hearing or at a later date 

O 15 r 18(12)
- At the conclusion of evidence for all parties, Court must hear the submissions of all parties in the order that the Court considers appropriate 

Miscellaneous Issue: Where party wants certain portions of submissions/examination/cross-examination/re-examination to be conducted by different counsel in the same case 
Paras 96(2) – 96(3) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021
- If party wants certain portions of submissions/examination/cross-examination/re-examination of witness to be conducted by different counsel in the same case, oral application should be made to Court no later than commencement of trial or hearing
- Application needs to state:
a. The issues on which each counsel will be making submissions; 
b. The witnesses to be examined, cross-examined or re-examined by each counsel, or the portions of their evidence for which each counsel will conduct the examination, cross-examination or re-examination.
- If permission is granted, counsel must ensure that each is confined to respective issues and portions of evidence and there is no overlap in issues or examination done 

Miscellaneous Issue: Junior assisting counsel is required to deliver the oral opening statement 
Para 96(5)(a) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021
- Except where lead counsel is a junior counsel, the junior assisting counsel must deliver the oral opening statement unless the Court otherwise orders 


*Pre-trial interim relief 
INTRO
Source of Court’s power for Interim Reliefs (founded in statute)– in order to preserve the status quo, protect interim rights and ensure the case can be fairly adjudicated at the trial
- S4(10) of Civil Law Act
- S18(2) and Para 5 of First Schedule of SCJA 
- S31 and s32 of SCA
Mareva Injunctions 
- Order to individuals and entities not to dispose or dissipate their assets. The assets are not taken away from the individuals and entities 
Search Order
- Search order to enter into premises and seize articles for the purpose of legal proceedings – helpful in intellectual property disputes
Detention and Preservation of property 
- Order to detain and take into custody property e.g. detention of painting that is the subject matter of dispute 
Taking of Samples 
- Order to take samples for the purposes of conducting experiments or examination 

*A. INTERLOCUTORY/INTERIM INJUNCTION 
Application for Interim Injunction will be heard by Registrar
Para 72 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Applications for interim junctions will be heard by a Registrar
Difference between interim injunction and freestanding injunction 
Tay Long Kee Impex Pte Ltd v Tan Beng Huwah 
- Interim injunction: Injunction made in the meantime and until something is done e.g. the final disposal of the matter
- Interlocutory injunction: Injunction made at the interlocutory stage of the suit, prior to the final disposal of the suit 
- Interim injunction is an interlocutory injunction 
Sulzer Pumps Spain SA v Hyflux Membrane Manufacturing (S) Pte Ltd 
- Freestanding injunction is not an interlocutory injunction
- Freestanding injunction: Where the sole and entire purpose of the originating process is to obtain the injunction
- Freestanding injunctions are the main and substantive relief sought but *interlocutory injunctions are ancillary relief sought separate to another substantive claim 
NB: Interim injunctions may be of two kinds, prohibitory and mandatory. Interim injunctions are prohibitory if they forbid the commission or continuance of an act. They are mandatory if that they compel the defendant to do some positive act to repair an omission or restore the status quo by undoing some act.
*Step 1: When can party apply for interlocutory injunction?

Party can apply for interlocutory injunction regardless whether it was mentioned in main originating process (OC/OA)
O 13 r 1(1)
- Party may apply for an injunction whether or not a claim for such relief was included in the party’s originating process, counterclaim or 3rd party notice 
Party can apply for interlocutory injunction before originating process (OC/OA) is issued 
O 13 r 1(2)
- In an urgent case, the claimant may apply for an injunction before the originating process is issued 
*Need for timely application
- The Court normally requires the Applicant to apply for the interlocutory injunction promptly after becoming aware of the facts in relation to which it seeks protection
- A failure on the part of the Applicant to move with real expeditious would undercut the argument that the application is so urgent that it has to be dealt with by way of a without notice application

*Step 2: Applicant must make ex partes (one-sided, without notice) application for interlocutory injunction   

Applicant may make ex partes application for interlocutory injunction by:
1. OA without notice (if you need to file interlocutory injunction urgently before main OC/OA is filed), or 
2. Summons without notice (if main OA/OC has already been filed), 
Both being supported by affidavit.

O 13 r 1(3)
- Where application is urgent and OC has commenced, a party can apply for an interlocutory injunction with OA without notice or summons without notice, supported by affidavit.
Supporting affidavit with contents in O 13 r 1(3) and Para 73 of SCPD
O 13 r 1(3)
- Application must be supported by an affidavit: 
* Explaining urgency 
* Explaining why defendant should not be informed about the application 
* Explaining merits of the application 
Para 73 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Affidavit in support of OA: Applicant must prepare affidavit in support of the OA must state:
a. Reasons the ex partes application is taken without notice and whether applicant believes there is a risk of dissipation of assets, destruction of evidence or any other prejudicial conduct 
b. Urgency of application including whether there is any particular event that may trigger the dissipation of assets, destruction of evidence or any other prejudicial conduct 
c. Factual basis for the application, including the basis of any belief that there will be dissipation of assets, destruction of evidence or any other prejudicial conduct. Whether there have been any past incidents of the same and whether there is any evidence of dishonesty or bad faith of the opponent 
d. Factual basis for any reasonable defenses that may be relied on by the opponent 
e. Whether applicant is aware of any issues relating to jurisdiction, forum non conveniens or service out of Singapore and if so, whether any related application has been or will be made
f. An undertaking to pay for losses that may be caused to the opponent by granting of the orders and stating what assets are available to meet that undertaking and to whom the assets belong
g. Any other material facts which the Court should be aware of 

Skeletal arguments and Notes on hearing with contents in Para 73 of SCPD
Para 73 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Skeletal arguments: Applicant must prepare and file skeletal arguments on points to be raised 
- Note on hearing: Applicant must prepare a note of the hearing setting out salient points and arguments to be served together with Court documents on any opponent not present 
Procedure when urgent application is made outside of Court’s office hours 
Para 85 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021  
- Office hours: Monday to Fridays (excluding public holidays) from 9am – 12.30pm, 2.30pm – 5pm 
- Applicant must contact Registrar on duty through phone number 
- Applicant must prepare all papers required for the application and if the papers have not been filed in Court, applicant must give an undertaking to Registrar to file the documents in Court by such time as the Registrar may direct and, in any event, no later than the next working day 
- Applicant must ensure all applicable notice requirements prescribed are complied with 
- Registrar will only allow for matter to be heard outside office hours if it is so urgent that it cannot be heard the next working day 
- Hearing may take place in Registrar’s chambers in Supreme Court or at any place directed by the Judge or Registrar 
- Registrar may direct counsel to send application and supporting documents by email and he has discretion to deal with application by email or to hear oral arguments from counsel 

*Step 3: Applicant must serve Notice of ex partes application to other concerned parties prior to the hearing unless the Exception applies
Para 71 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Even if party makes an ex partes application interlocutory injunction, party must still give notice of the ex partes application to the other concerned parties prior to the hearing 
Form of notice 
a. Notice may be given by way of letter or email or in cases of extreme urgency, orally by telephone 
b. Notice must inform the other parties of the date, time and place fixed for the hearing and the nature of the relief sought 
c. A copy of the originating process, summons without notice or OA without notice and supporting affidavits should be given to other party in draft form 

Notice must be given a minimum of 2 hours before hearing – Except in cases of extreme urgency
- Except in cases of extreme urgency or with Court’s permission, notice must be given a minimum of 2 hours’ before the hearing 

If any concerned parties are not present at hearing – Applicant must inform Court of certain matters
- At the hearing, if some or all of the other parties are not present or represented, applicant’s lawyers should inform Court of:
a. Attempts made to notify the other parties or their solicitors of the making of the application 
b. Documents given to the other parties or their solicitors and when they were given 
c. Whether the other parties or their solicitors consent to the application being heard without their presence

*EXCEPTION to notice of application (para 71(3) PD): If giving notice would defeat purpose of ex partes application 
- Directions set out here need not be followed if giving of notice to the other parties would or might defeat the purpose of the application without notice:
a. E.g. if defendant would act swiftly to render the injunction application moot
b. Reasons for not following directions should be clearly set out and substantiated in the affidavit supporting the summons / OA without notice 

*Step 4: Applicant must satisfy duty to disclose all material facts in ex partes application 
Applicant duty to disclose to the Court: (1) All material facts that applicant knows or reasonably ought to know, and (2) Any matter that may adversely affect merits of applicant’s injunction --> DUTY OF FULL AND FRANK DISCLOSURE
O 13 r 1(5)
- Party applying for injunction has a duty to disclose to the Court: 
* All material facts that the party knows or reasonably ought to know
* Including any matter that may affect the merits of the party’s case adversely 

Material facts: Facts which enable the Court to make an informed decision 
Tay Long Kee Impex Pte Ltd v Tan Beng Huwah 
- Material facts: Facts which enable the Court to make an informed decision 
a. Matters which Court should consider in making its decision 
b. Defenses which might be reasonably raised by the defendant 
c. If there were unsuccessful prior proceedings and the reasons for dismissal then 
d. Material facts are limits to what the applicant could reasonably be expected to know or to have found out through proper inquiries at the point of application 

*Consequences for breach of duty: Setting aside of injunction 
- Breach of duty to disclose to provide full and frank disclosure is an independent ground for setting aside an injunction 

Reason for Duty to disclose
The “Vasiliy Golovin”
- Duty of disclosure in ex partes application: Applicant must disclose to the court all matters within his knowledge which might be material even if they are prejudicial to the applicant’s claim 
- Manner of disclosure: not open for Applicant to say that it has fulfilled the duty simply because the relevant facts can be distilled from the voluminous documents exhibited to the affidavit. Material facts should be fairly stated in the text of the affidavit itself
- Reason
a. Injunctions can cause irreparable damage to parties concerned
b. In an ex partes application, Court does not have the benefit of countervailing arguments (from the opposing party) and may not be appropriately sensitized to the real merits of the application and the potentially hazardous ramifications 
c. Applicant has a duty to ensure that the Court is given a balanced view of the matter

*Step 5: Applicant must satisfy 2-stage test for Court to grant interlocutory injunction 
American Cyanamid Co v Ethicon Ltd 
- 2-stage test:
1. Whether there is a serious question to be tried, and
2. the “balance of convenience” being is in favor of granting the injunction sought 

Stage 1: Whether there is a serious question to be tried
- Applicant needs to show that he has a cause of action that is not frivolous or vexatious, with prospects of success which in substance and reality exist
- The prospect of success is only to be investigated to a limited extent and the threshold is low – the Court is not required to evaluate the chances of success in the action on a balance of probabilities

Stage 2: Whether the balance of convenience is in favor of granting the injunction sought
- Court needs to assess the extent to which each party is capable of being compensated in damages in the event that the opposing party wins at trial 
a. If applicant (for injunction) can be compensated by damages + defendant is in position to pay such damages => Injunction against defendant unlikely granted 
b. If applicant (for injunction) cannot be adequately compensated by damages + if applicant has provided an undertaking to compensate any of defendant’s damages => Injunction against defendant likely granted
- Court should take the course which appears to carry the lower risk of injustice if that course should ultimately turn out to have been the wrong one at trial, in the sense of granting relief to a party who fails to establish its rights at trial (i.e. helping out losing party), or failing to grant relief to a party who succeeds at trial (i.e. not helping out successful party)

*Application to interim mandatory injunctions vs prohibitory injunctions
Chuan Hong Petrol Station v Shell Singapore
GENERAL RULE: the court will grant an interim injunction to preserve the status quo pending trial, and “should take whichever course appears to carry the lower risk of injustice if it should turn out to have been wrong at trial in the sense of granting relief to a party who fails to establish his rights at the trial, or of failing to grant relief to a party who succeeds at the trial”. 
--> i.e. the court will try to grant relief to a losing party or fail to help an eventually successful party

EXCEPTION 1: However, this general rule does NOT apply where an interim mandatory injunction is sought (compelling the defendant to do a positive act).
- Such an injunction is “a very exceptional discretionary remedy” and there is thus “a much higher threshold to be met in order to persuade the court to grant such an injunction as compared to a prohibitive injunction” (NCC International AB v Alliance Concrete Singapore Pte Ltd)
- Such an injunction should only be granted if there are “special circumstances” (NCC International at [75]), in a “clear case” or if there is a “high degree of assurance” that it will appear at trial to have been rightly granted, and this is a higher standard than is required for a prohibitory injunction (Chuan Hong at [85]-[86])
- This is because “a high degree of assurance” is a guideline which reflects the fundamental principle of lower risk of injustice (Chuan Hong Petrol Station; followed in Projector SA v Marubeni International Petroleum (S) Pte Ltd [2005] 2 SLR(R) 144)

--> Need for caution arises from the recognition that an interim mandatory injunction is a more invasive remedy and, as such, may lead to irreversible prejudice to a defendant. A court must therefore be sure, in accordance with the fundamental principle described above, that the grant of the interim mandatory injunction is indeed the course of action which carries the lower risk of injustice – in other words, that the prejudice to the applicant from not granting the interim mandatory injunction outweighs that to the defendant from granting it. This will not be an easy hurdle to surmount

EXCEPTION 2: The general rule also does NOT apply to interim prohibitory injunctions where a defendant is about to breach, or has already breached, a negative covenant in a contract, and the interim prohibitory injunction sought is to restrain a prospective breach or a further breach. --> I.E. LOWER STANDARD COMPARED TO NORMAL PROHIBITORY INJUNCTIONS SINCE D SHOULD NORMALLY OBSERVE THE CONTRACT IT ENTERED INTO
- An interim prohibitory injunction WILL readily be granted to restrain a prospective breach or a further breach
- The court in such a case is NOT concerned whether there is a serious question to be tried and whether the balance of convenience is in favour of granting such an interim injunction. That damages would be an adequate remedy is not generally a relevant consideration
- As the remedy is an equitable one, it is in principle discretionary, and it may be refused on the ground that it would cause particular hardship as to be oppressive to the defendant. But the burden caused by having to observe the contract does NOT qualify as hardship
- An injunction will only be refused if a defendant shows hardship over and above that which results from having to observe the contract

Reasoning:
- Gee: There is a strong policy for enforcing bargains and this goes to the extent of enforcing a negative covenant. There is a strong presumption that the covenant will be enforced by injunction, albeit the matter remains discretionary.
- Bean, Parry and Burns: Any argument that granting an injunction to uphold a contractual bargain would cause hardship should be “treated with some scepticism, since the defendant has undertaken the obligation voluntarily”. 


*Step 6: Court might require Applicant to provide an undertaking to compensate any of defendant’s damages if interlocutory injunction is granted 
- Whether applicant’s undertaking can compensate defendant’s damages is a factor under Stage 2 of American Cyanamid too
Court can request applicant provide undertaking before granting injunction 
2022 White Book at [13/1/28]
- Forms 24 and 25 enables applicant to provide an undertaking => Court can decide whether to enforce an undertaking if the defendant incurs losses as a result of the interlocutory injunction

Undertaking as a condition of interlocutory injunction
2022 White Book at [13/1/29]
- Undertaking ought to be a condition of every interlocutory injunction 

Extent of undertaking – Undertaking usually given to all defendants in the case including defendants against whom no injunction is made => However, not defendants who have joined after interlocutory injunction has been discharged
2022 White Book at [13/1/30]
- Undertaking in damages given by the claimant extends to all defendants in the case including defendant against whom no injunction is made and defendants jointed after it is granted
- However, a defendant who joined after an interlocutory injunction granted against another defendant has been discharged is not entitled to the benefit of the undertaking 
=> Reason: It is pointless at time of grant of injunction to predict which defendants will be affected if injunction turns out to have been granted improperly since damages will be payable only where damage is actually suffered 

Court can request that applicant fortify the undertaking by giving security 
2022 White Book at [13/1/33]
- Court may impose a condition that the claimant’s undertaking should be fortified by his giving security:
* By the bond of an insurance company or 
* By payment into court or 
* By some other means e.g. payment to the applicant’s solicitors or to the solicitors for each party jointly to be held pending further order 
- Defendant can apply for Injunction applicant to fortify his undertaking if he can show that: 
a. The Court has made an intelligent estimate of the likely amount of loss which might result to the defendant by reason of the injunction 
b. There is a sufficient level of risk of loss and the undertaking will be insufficient 
c. The contemplated loss would be caused by the grant of the injunction 
- Defendant should apply for security at the time when injunction is granted, after an inter partes hearing and the undertaking is given 

If defendant wins at trial => Defendant can apply to enforce C’s undertaking => Court will consider 2 questions
2022 White Book at [13/1/34] – [13/1/37]
- Undertaking is given to the Court => If it is held at trial that the interlocutory injunction ought not to have been granted in the first instance => Defendant can apply to Court for the undertaking to be enforced 
- Court will ask two things: 
1. Should the Court order that the undertaking be enforced?
2. Has defendant suffered any damage by reason of the granting of the interlocutory injunction? 

Question 1: Should the Court order that the undertaking be enforced?
- Court should exercise discretion in accordance with equitable principles and consider: 
a. Whether interlocutory injunction order was wrongly asked for by the applicant 
(i) For Mareva injunction: Depends on whether risk of dissipation existed at time of grant of injunction and its continuance 
(ii) For ordinary interlocutory injunction: Depends on whether a final injunction is granted at trial 
b. Whether special circumstances existed in the case

Question 2: Has defendant suffered any damage by reason of the granting of the interlocutory injunction? 
- Court will have to decide causation and quantum of damages 
- Measure of damages: Loss which is the natural consequence of the interlocutory injunction 


*B. MAREVA INJUNCTION 
- Injunction or freezing order to prohibit the disposal of assets by a defendant to defeat a potential judgement. It cover assets locally in Singapore or worldwide where assets are wholly or partly located abroad

Application for Mareva Injunction will be heard by Judge
Para 72 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Applications for Mareva injunctions will be heard by a Judge 
- Applicant should not inform any 3rd party of proceedings until after the return date 

Can Mareva injunction be granted to in aid of foreign proceedings where there are no substantive proceedings in Singapore? 
Bi Xiaoqiong v China Medical Technologies
- No. 
- A *Mareva Injunction is merely an ancillary relief that cannot be “free-standing” and this means that it must be grounded on a reasonable accrued cause of action in Singapore before it is granted. 

Difference between Mareva injunction and proprietary injunction 
Ernest Ferdinand Perez De La Sala v Compania De Navegacion Palomar SA
- Proprietary injunction: Injunction which fastens on a specific asset in which the applicant asserts a proprietary interest. The defendant is prevented from dealing with the specific asset and any of its traceable proceeds
- Mareva injunction: Injunction granted in support of a substantive claim for personal relief and it does not latch onto any specific asset of the defendant. The defendant is prevented from disposing of his own assets (from a pool) beyond a certain value such that he would be able to defeat a possible judgement that may be rendered against him --> therefore no objection in principle to a defendant being allowed to use assets subject to a Mareva injunction for living expenses, as long as it does not conflict with the policy underlying the Mareva jurisdiction

Step 1: When can party apply for Mareva injunction? (same as interlocutory application above)

*Step 2: Applicant must make ex partes application for Mareva injunction
Applicant may make ex partes application for Mareva injunction by OA or summons, supported by affidavit, with/without notice
O 13 r 1(3)
- Same as interlocutory injunctions generally: made by OA or summons, supported by affidavit, with or without notice (in cases of urgency)
- Such applications which are draconian, will be heard by a Judge and not a registrar (Para 72(1) SCPD 2021)

Local Mareva injunction – Ex partes application must be in Form 24
O 13 r 1(6): Local Mareva injunction 
- A local injunction prohibiting the disposal of assets in Singapore must be in Form 24

International Mareva injunction – Ex partes application must be in Form 25
O 13 r 1(7): International Mareva injunction 
- A worldwide injunction prohibiting the disposal of assets worldwide must be in Form 25

If Applicant departs from Forms used => Applicant must justify departure in the supporting affidavit 
Para 72 of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Forms 24 and 25 of Appendix A of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 should be used
- If applicant departs from the forms used, must justify in his supporting affidavit 

Step 3: Applicant must serve Notice of ex partes application to other concerned parties prior to the hearing unless Exception (same as interlocutory application above)

Step 4: Applicant must satisfy duty to disclose all material facts in ex partes application (same as interlocutory application above)

*Step 5: Applicant must satisfy 4 limbs + Convince Court that it is just and equitable for Court to grant domestic Mareva injunction 
2022 White Book at [13/1/60]
- For a domestic Mareva injunction, the claimant must show 
1. There is a valid cause of action [referring to the main action] over which the Court has jurisdiction; AND
2. There is a good arguable case on the merits of the claimant’s claim; AND
3. The defendant has assets within the jurisdiction; AND
4. There is a real risk that the defendants will dissipate his assets to frustrate the enforcement of an anticipated judgement of the Court 
- Court will then consider whether it would be just and convenient to grant a Mareva injunction 

Limb 1: There is a valid cause of action over which the Court has jurisdiction
2022 White Book at [13/1/61] 
- Claimant must show that he has a substantive claim over which Singapore Court has jurisdiction 
- Reason: A Mareva injunction is an ancillary remedy collateral to the main action and cannot stand on its own 

Limb 2: There is a good arguable case on the merits of the claimant’s claim
2022 White Book at [13/1/63] 
- A good arguable case is one which is more than barely capable of serious agreement but not necessarily one which the judge considers would have a better than a 50% chance of success 

Limb 3: The defendant has assets within the jurisdiction
2022 White Book at [13/1/64] – [13/1/65] 
- Applicant for Mareva injunction must give grounds for believing that the defendant has assets within the jurisdiction and identify what assets are available 
Applicant can use Ancillary Order (an “ancillary asset disclosure order”) to require Defendant to disclose all his assets in an affidavit to the Applicant
Ancillary Order requiring defendant to disclose all his assets in his affidavit to the applicant (Sea Trucks Offshore Ltd v Roomans Jacobus Johannes) 
- Note – this order is usually applied for in the main application for Mareva injunction – no point making a separate application. 
- Assets which must be disclosed:
a. Assets legally owned by defendant 
b. Assets beneficially held by defendant 
- Assets which need not be disclosed: 
a. Assets legally owned by defendant but held on trust for 3rd parties 
b. Assets of a company entirely owned by defendant
==> However: Defendant’s shareholding in the company are still caught by the injunction and defendant would still be restricted from procuring the company to make disposals other than in the ordinary course of business because that could lead to diminution of his share value
- Disclosure obligations would not go further than what is required for Applicant to enforce Mareva injunction  

Special Scenario: Where Defendant disputes ownership of an asset (e.g. 3P ownership)
1. If the assets appear to belong to a 3rd party then they should NOT be included in the scope of the Mareva injunction without evidence that they are the defendant’s 
2. The mere assertion of the defendant that a 3rd party owns the assets need not be accepted without inquiry and the same principle applies to claims by a 3rd party to intervene to vary the injunction to exclude the assets 
3. Court must do its best to do what is just and convenient between all concerned
4. In a proper case, the Court may direct an issue to be tried either before or after the main action as to the ownership of the assets 

Limb 4: There is a real risk that the defendants will dissipate his assets to frustrate the enforcement of an anticipated judgement of the Court 
2022 White Book at [13/1/66] 
- Applicant must show solid evidence that there is a risk that assets will be dissipated 
- Examples of such evidence:
a. Direct evidence that the defendant has previously acted in a way which shows that his probity is not to be relied on 
b. If the defendant is a company, its incorporation, corporate structure and assets so as to raise an inference that the defendant is not to be relied on 
c. The fact that inquiries about the characteristics of the defendant have led to nothing 
- Court can consider the nature of assets sought to be injuncted and the ease with which such assets can be dissipated 
The true ground for a Mareva injunction is the real risk of dissipation of assets by the party sought to be restrained (Bouvier, Yves Charles Edgar v Accent Delight International Ltd [2015] 5 SLR 558). This involves whether there was sufficient evidence that the subject of the injunction would, or at least were likely to, dissipate their assets so as to defeat or frustrate the realisation of a judgment that the applicants might eventually obtain against them (Heng Holdings SEA (Pte) Ltd v Tomongo Shipping Co. Ltd [1997] 2 SLR(R) 669). 

Allegation of dishonesty alone is insufficient => Need to establish a real risk of dissipation
Bouvier, Yves Charles Edgar v Accent Delight International Ltd 
- An allegation of dishonesty does not by itself obviate the need to establish a real risk of dissipation => The allegation of dishonesty has to be of a nature or characteristic that sufficiently bears upon the risk of dissipation 

Effect of Mareva injunction on defendant’s bank account 
2022 White Book at [13/1/64]
- If defendant’s assets consist of a bank account which contains a mixture of defendant’s money and 3rd party’s money => Court has jurisdiction to restrain defendant from removing or dealing with any of the money in the account 
- If defendant’s money is under control of a 3rd party bank account, the 3rd party bank and the defendant will both be enjoined and restrained from removing or dealing with the money in the bank account 

*C. COSTS ORDER FOR INJUNCTIONS 
Costs of injunction depends on discretion of judge 
Chiarapurk Jack v Haw Par Bros International Pte Ltd 
- Question of costs depends on discretion of the judge hearing the application and the judge can order costs to be in the cause 
Common order – Costs to be in the cause
- Losing party will be made to pay costs to winning party 

*D. SEARCH ORDER – ANTON PILLER ORDER 
Applicable where there is a grave danger that the defendant would dispose of or destroy incriminating evidence in its possession. This complements the Court’s powers to grant an order for the detention and preservation of property and taking of samples (see below).
- The order allows the claimant or its representative to enter the defendant’s premises to enter and search for, inspect and seize material so they may be preserved until the trial
- Such orders involves a serious interference with the defendant’s privacy and can seriously disrupt operations in the case of business premises
- Due to the draconian nature of such orders, they are remedies granted only when it is essential or necessary in the interests of justice and the requirements are stringent – Court will generally insist upon a clear showing of fraud, dishonesty or imminent removal or destruction of property/evidence

Step 1: When can party apply for search order? (same as interlocutory application above)

*Step 2: Applicant must make ex partes application for search order (same as interlocutory application above)
Applicant may make ex partes application for search order by OA without notice or summons without notice, supported by affidavit
O 13 r 1(3)
- Party’s application for interlocutory injunction may be by OA without notice or summons without notice, supported by affidavit stating urgency, etc. (refer above)

Search order – Ex partes application must be in Form 26
O 13 r 1(8)
- A search order must be in Form 26 of Appendix A of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021
- If applicant departs from the form used, must justify in his supporting affidavit (Para 72(2) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021)

Step 3: Applicant must satisfy duty to disclose all material facts in ex partes application (same as interlocutory application above)

*Step 4: Applicant must satisfy 4 limbs + Convince Court that the search order is proportionate for Court to grant search order  
2022 White Book at [13/1/87]
Asian Corporate Services (SEA) Pte Ltd v Eastwest Management Ltd (Singapore Branch)
- 4 limbs for making of a search order 
1. Limb 1: Applicant must have strong prima facie main case: There must be an extremely strong prima facie case of a civil cause of action. 
a. A scrutiny of the merits of the claimant’s main case is an essential preliminary to the grant of a search order 
2. Limb 2: Damage to be avoided by applicant must be very serious: Damage, potential or actual, to the claimant to be avoided by the grant of a search order must be very serious 
a. If search order is sought to forestall the destruction of evidence, the evidence in question must be of major and critical importance 
3. Limb 3: Clear evidence that defendant has incriminating evidence: There must be clear evidence that the defendant has in his possession incriminating documents or items 
4. Limb 4: Real possibility that defendant will destroy evidence: There must be a real possibility that the defendant may destroy such material before an application inter partes can be made 

Proportionality – The harm likely to be caused by the execution of the search order to the defendant and his business affairs must not be excessive or out of proportion to the legitimate object of the search order 
Bengawan Solo Pte Ltd v Season Confectionary Pte Ltd
- Principle of proportionality is particularly relevant where the search order results in the seizure of trading stock or the perusal by the claimant of the defendant’s confidential commercial documents 
- Held: 
1. Court discharged the search order and commented that if any search order is really required, a limited search order for the claimants to enter a couple of outlets to take a few samples of the infringing mooncakes for preservation should have been considered  
Computerland Corp v Yew Seng Computers Pte Ltd 
- Search order has a draconian nature and it should only be granted in exceptional circumstances
- Court has to balance the claimant’s right to recover his property or to preserve evidence against the violation of the privacy of a defendant who has no opportunity to put his side of the case 

*Step 5: Court might require Applicant to provide an undertaking to compensate any of defendant’s damages if search order is granted 
Court can require applicant/applicant’s solicitor to provide undertaking
2022 White Book at [13/1/90] 
- Applicant, the applicant’s solicitors and the supervising solicitor might be required to give an undertaking 
- Undertaking can be: 
* Payment into Court 
* By provision of a bond by an insurance company which has a place of business within Singapore
* By a written guarantee from a bank which as a place of business within Singapore 
* Payment to the applicant’s solicitor to be held by the solicitor as an officer of the court pending further order  
Undertaking: Applicant give undertaking to compensate Defendant for any loss caused by the search order/execution of search order
- The undertaking is that the applicant should comply with any order the Court may make if the Court later finds that the search order or carrying out of the search order has caused loss to the defendant and decides that the defendant should be compensated for that loss 
- Court must be satisfied that the applicant is good for sums which may be due upon his undertaking as to damages 

*Step 6: Procedure for carrying out search under Search Order
Search must be carried out in supervising solicitor’s presence and under his supervision
Para 72(5) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Form 26 must be served by a supervising solicitor and affidavit should include the identity and experienced of proposed supervising solicitor
- Search must be carried out in supervising solicitor’s presence and under his supervision 
- Supervising solicitor: An experienced solicitor who is not a member or employee of the firm acting for the applicant and who has some familiarity with the operation of search orders 

*E. ORDER FOR DETENTION AND PRESERVATION OF PROPERTY AND THE TAKING OF SAMPLES 
Order for Detention and Preservation of property
- Used for the interim preservation of property and evidence
- Procedure: summons supported by affidavit
- Need not be made on application of any party – such orders can be made by the Court on its own accord

Order must be against a property which is the subject matter of action/gives rise to issues in the action
O 13 r 2(1)
- Court may order the detention, custody or preservation of any property which is the subject matter of or may give rise to issues in an action 
==> Property must be the subject matter of the issues in an action e.g. a machine which is alleged to constitute an infringement of a patent 

Ancillary Order: Allow applicant to inspect opposing party’s property
O 13 r 2(2)
- Court may order the inspection of any such property in the possession or control of a party

Ancillary Order: Authorize applicant to enter place to detain and preserve property 
O 13 r 2(3)
- Court may authorize any person to enter upon any immovable property in the possession or control of any party to effect any order made under O 13 r 2(1) and 2(2)

Special Scenario: If the property is a right to a specific fund 
O 13 r 2(4)
- Where there is a dispute as to the right of any party to a specific fund, Court may order the fund to be paid into Court or otherwise secured 

Order for Taking of Samples of property
Order must be against a property which is the subject matter of action/gives rise to issues in action 
O 13 r 3(1)
- If any property is the subject matter of or may give rise to issues in an action, the Court may order 
a. Any sample of the property to be taken; and 
b. Any experiment or examination to be conducted on the sample 
==> Property must be the subject matter of the issues in an action 

Ancillary Order: Authorize applicant to enter place to take samples 
O 13 r 3(2)
- Court may authorize any person to enter upon any immovable property in the possession or control of any party to effect any order made under O 13 r 3(1)

UCMI Ltd v Tokio Marine & Fire Insurance Co (Singapore) Pte Ltd 
- Applicant needs to demonstrate a danger of destruction to existing evidence
- Issue relates to the authenticity of a document signed by Mr Lim
- Applicant sought an order to compel Mr Lim to: 
a. Make and provide handwriting samples
b. Provide similar documents previously made in the course of business which had a sample of Mr Lim’s handwriting 
- Held: Court rejected both orders 
1. Order (a) rejected because an order “compelling Mr Lim to replicate his handwriting” is not an order for taking of samples. The handwriting samples were not even in existence and could not fall under the definition of property  
2. Order (b) rejected because there was no evidence to suggest that the documents were in danger of being destroyed 

Order for Taking of Samples not applicable to choses in action (e.g. bank money in bank account)
Sang Cheol Woo v Charles Choi Spackman 
- Orders only apply to property and does not apply to choses in action => Implication is that orders cannot be over money in a bank account which is a chose in action i.e. right to money in a bank account => Applicant who wants an order to apply to money in a bank account has to try and use O 13 r 2(4) (under an order for detention and preservation, rather than taking of samples)


Post-judgment recourse 
*A. FURTHER ARGUMENTS (TO THE SAME COURT)
1. State Courts
2. GDHC
3. ADHC/CA 
4. Reliance on further evidence in further arguments 

*Further Arguments in State Courts 
Procedure for party to request to make Further Arguments in State Courts
Para 81 of State Courts Practice Directions 2021 
(1) Party must file Request for Further Arguments through Electronic Filing Service: All requests for further arguments shall be made by way of a “Request for Further Arguments” and filed through the Electronic Filing Service.
(2) Contents of Request: The party filing the Request must, either in the Request or a document attached thereto:
a. state the party making the request;
b. identify the Judge or Registrar who heard the matter in question;
c. specify when the order concerned was made;
d. state the provision of law under which the request is made;
e. set out the proposed further arguments briefly, together with any supporting authorities; and
f. include a copy of each of the authorities cited.
(3) Party must furnish a copy of Request to all concerned parties: A copy of the Request must be furnished to all parties concerned.
(4) Request must be addressed to Registrar: All Requests must be addressed to the Registrar.

*Further Arguments in GDHC (only for decisions made during trial e.g. interlocutory applications, but not for hearing of a full trial)
Further Arguments only for decisions during trial (e.g. interlocutory application), but not after trial
S29B(1) of Supreme Court Judicature Act 
- Further Arguments are not allowed after hearing of a full trial: S29B statutory framework applies to a decision by a judge in the original or appellate civil jurisdiction after any hearing other than a trial of an action 
o No further arguments after a trial
o But further arguments can be made after a decision is made in an application
- Further Arguments may be allowed in any other type of hearing

TG Master v Tung Kee Development
- The High Court does NOT have the jurisdiction to hear further arguments after a trial
- Parliament intended to curtail the HC’s inherent jurisdiction to hear further arguments after a trial, by excluding the hearing of further arguments after a trial from its conferral of statutory jurisdiction to hear further arguments in other matters
- The possibility of further arguments being heard was only ever intended to apply for interlocutory applications and not a full trial. The underlying concerns behind the need to retain the right to request for further arguments were borne out of: 
(a) the short amount of preparation time that counsels have to present their arguments to a judge in chambers for interlocutory applications, and 
(b) the correspondingly short amount of deliberation time a judge has before making an interlocutory order quickly, and sometimes, even on the spot for urgent applications

Time to Request for Further Arguments in GDHC – 
before any notice of appeal is filed AND before earlier of:
(1) time at which judgement is extracted OR 
(2) 15th day after date of judgement 
S29B(2) SCJA
- GDHC Judge who made the decision may hear further arguments in respect of the decision if parties/GDHC Judge requests for further arguments: 
* Before any notice of appeal is filed against the decision; AND 
* Before the earlier of the following: 
a. Time at which judgement or order relating to the decision is extracted; OR 
b. 15th day after the date on which the decision is made 

GDHC Judge powers after hearing Further Arguments – may affirm, vary or set aside decision
S29B(3) SCJA
- After hearing further arguments, the Judge may affirm, vary or set aside the decision

Effect of Request for Further Arguments on Appeals --> I.E. RFFA BLOCKS ANY NOTICES OF APPEAL FROM BEING FILED
S29B(4) SCJA
- If a request for further arguments has been made under subsection s29B(2): 
a. Notice of Appeal cannot be filed against the decision until:
(i) Judge affirms, varies or set asides decision after hearing further arguments; or
(ii) Judge certifies no Further Arguments are required; and

b. The time for filing a notice of appeal in respect of the decision begins on the date the Judge: 
a. Affirms, varies or sets aside the decision after hearing further arguments; or
b. Certifies, or is deemed to have certified, that no further arguments are required

Procedure for party to request to make Further Arguments in GDHC
O 18 r 28 of ROC 
(1) Party must make request by letter to Registrar of Supreme Court => Must serve letter on all parties: A request to the Judge sitting in the General Division for further arguments from the parties after he or she has given his or her decision on an application in the exercise of original or appellate jurisdiction, must be made by letter to the Registrar of the Supreme Court and served on all parties to the application.
(2) Time of Request: s29B(2) of SCJA: The request in paragraph (1) must be made within the time provided in section 29B(2) of the Supreme Court of Judicature Act. --> i.e. before notice of appeal is filed + before judgement is extracted or 15 days after date of decision (whichever earlier)
(3) Contents of Request: The request must set out the proposed arguments briefly and include a copy of any authority cited.
(4) Registrar of Supreme Court will approval or reject the Request within 14 days after receiving the Request: The Registrar of the Supreme Court must inform the requesting party within 14 days after receiving the request whether the Judge requires further arguments
(5) Silence is rejection: If the Registrar of the Supreme Court does not inform the requesting party as mentioned in paragraph (4), it is deemed that the Judge does not require further arguments.

Para 112 of Supreme Courts Practice Directions 2021 
(1) Party must file Request for Further Arguments through Electronic Filing Service: All requests for further arguments shall be made by way of a “Request for Further Arguments” and filed through the Electronic Filing Service.
(2) Contents of Request: The party filing the Request must, either in the Request or a document attached thereto:
a. state the party making the request;
b. identify the Judge or Registrar who heard the matter in question;
c. specify when the order concerned was made;
d. state the provision of law under which the request is made;
e. set out the proposed further arguments briefly, together with any supporting authorities; and
f. include a copy of each of the authorities cited.
(3) Party must furnish a copy of Request to all concerned parties: A copy of the Request must be furnished to all parties concerned.
(4) Request must be addressed to Registrar: All Requests must be addressed to the Registrar.

*Further Arguments in ADHC/CA 
No Further Arguments in ADHC/CA after:
(1) ADHC/CA has heard appeal and reserved its decision; OR 
(2) ADHC/CA has given its decision in the appeal (commenced by OA)

O 18 r 38 of ROC 
- Unless the appellate Court otherwise directs, there are to be no further arguments from the parties after:
* The appellate Court has heard the appeal and reserved its decision or 
* After the appellate Court has given its decision in the appeal

No Further Arguments in ADHC/CA after: 
(1) ADHC/CA has heard appeal and reserved its decision; OR 
(2) ADHC/CA has given its decision in the appeal (commenced by OC)

O 19 r 34 of ROC
- Unless the appellate Court otherwise directs, there are to be no further arguments from the parties after:
* the appellate Court has heard the appeal and reserved its decision or 
* after the appellate Court has given its decision in the appeal

*Reliance on Further Evidence in Further Arguments 
Further evidence only allowed if it supports new Further Arguments made
ARW v Comptroller of Income Tax 
- Further evidence in support of new arguments made (not raised previously) in the course of Further Arguments may be allowed
- Further evidence to strengthen or support previously raised arguments are not allowed 
 
*B. APPEALS (TO A HIGHER LEVEL COURT)
Basics 
Step 1: Is there a right to appeal? 
Step 2: If so, is permission to appeal required? And from which Court should permission be obtained?
Step 3: Is it an Order 18 or Order 19 case? 
Step 4: Which Order 18 case/Order 19 case? 
Step 5: General provisions which apply to both Order 18 case and Order 19 case
a. Timeline for appealing and applying for permission to appeal 
b. Appeal is not a stay of enforcement (no automatic stay of enforcement while appeal is pending)
c. Expedited appeal 
Step 6: Respective flow will apply 

FOCUS of the course is on:
(a) Allocation of appeals from GDHC to ADHC and CA
(b) Requirement for permission for leave to appeal against a decision of the GDHC

Structure of Order 18 (streamlined process): 
Division 1
General (all tiers)Division 2
r 15 – 16 
Appeal within State Courts: Registrar to District Judge

Division 3
r 17 – 23 
Appeals from District Judge/Magistrate to GDHC 
Division 4 
r 24 – 25 
Appeal within Supreme Court: Registrar to GDHC
 Division 5 
r 26 – 41
Appeal within Supreme Court: GDHC to ADHC/CA
Types of appeals:
* Appeals from applications in an action (and further appeals from there)
* Appeals from other decisions of the Registrar (including on assessment, accounts and OAs)
* Appeals from DJ/Mag decision on OAs
* Appeals from summonses within O 18 appeals or O 19 appeals

Appeals under Order 18 are for more straightforward issues, like appeals on applications filed in an action, e.g. interlocutory matters, document production, striking out, etc.. Not much documentation has to be filled, so timelines are also shorter.

Structure of Order 19 (more detailed documentation): 
Division 1
General (all tiers of appeal under O 19)Division 2
r 13 – 23 
Appeals from DJ/Mag in State Courts to GDHC Division 3
r 24 – 40 
Appeals from GDHC to ADHC/CA 

Further Appeals from ADHC to CA
Division 4 
r 41 
Appeals from Disciplinary Tribunal to 3 Judges of the GDHC (under Medical Registration Act)Types of appeals:
* Appeals from judgements of DJ/Mag in OCs, assessments, accounts, committal
* Appeals from judgements of the GDHC (excluding Registrar)
* Appeals from judgements of the ADHC
* Appeals from tribunals to the ADHC or CA pursuant to written law
* Appeals from a Medical Registration Act Disciplinary Tribunal to 3 Judges of GDHC

Appeals under Order 19 are for more complex matters after a more protracted process, the documentation required is more extensive and timeline for appeals are also longer.

Appeals – general issues
Filing of an appeal does not operate as a stay of enforcement of the lower Court’s order / judgement, unless the lower or appellate Court orders otherwise
Default position: lower Court’s position or judgement continues to have full effect unless the lower or appellate Court orders otherwise. That means you can enforce the lower Court’s judgement while the appeal is pending unless you can depart from this position.

Order 18: General rule that an appeal does not operate as a stay of enforcement 
O 18 r 6 of ROC
(1) Except so far as the lower Court or the appellate Court may otherwise direct, an appeal does not operate as a stay of enforcement or of proceedings under the decision of the lower Court.
(2) Except so far as the appellate Court may otherwise direct, no intermediate act or proceeding is to be invalidated by an appeal.
(3) On an appeal, interest for such time as enforcement has been delayed by the appeal is to be allowed unless the lower Court or the appellate Court otherwise orders.

Order 19: General rule that an appeal does not operate as a stay of enforcement 
O 19 r 6 of ROC
(1) Except so far as the lower Court or the appellate Court may otherwise direct, an appeal does not operate as a stay of enforcement or of proceedings under the decision of the lower Court
(2) Except so far as the appellate Court may otherwise direct, no intermediate act or proceeding is to be invalidated by an appeal
(3) On an appeal, interest for such time as enforcement has been delayed by the appeal is to be allowed unless the lower Court or the appellate Court otherwise orders.

Some notes on this 
- If enforcement is not stayed before appeal, and the appeal is successful, then the successful appellant is entitled to return of sums paid under the original judgment. Court order can be granted to compel this (I think this is not in syllabus). 
- Practically, this means that if you think the opposing party is not likely to abide by an appeal decision, then you should apply for stay of enforcement pending appeal 

Expedited appeal
Expedited appeal if urgent or special reason 
O 18 r 11(1) of ROC
- If the appeal is urgent or there is a special reason, the lower Court or the appellate Court may order an expedited appeal upon any party’s application or on its own accord.

O 19 r 9(1) of ROC
- If the appeal is urgent or there is a special reason, the lower Court or the appellate Court may order an expedited appeal upon any party’s application or on its own accord.

Dispense with compliance of any provisions for Expedited appeal 
O 18 r 11(2) of RPC
- In an expedited appeal, the lower Court or the appellate Court may dispense with compliance with any provision of these Rules or practice directions or modify them for the purposes of the appeal.

O 19 r 9(2) of RPC
- In an expedited appeal, the lower Court or the appellate Court may dispense with compliance with any provision of these Rules or practice directions or modify them for the purposes of the appeal.

Flow for all types of Appeals
- Regardless of tier of the Court 
- Regardless of whether matter begins by OC or OA

**Step 1: Is there a right to appeal? 
No right to appeal against GDHC decisions in 2 situations – if satisfied, inquiry ends here, cannot even seek permission to appeal
S29 of Supreme Court of Judicature Act
