(2) The Court hearing such application may order any issue between the judgment creditor and the judgment debtor to be tried in any manner in which an issue in an action may be ordered to be tried.
(3) Grounds for Court to set aside registration: Where the Court hearing an application to set aside the registration of a judgment registered under the first Act is satisfied that the judgment falls within any of the cases in which a judgment may not be ordered to be registered under section 3(2) of that Act, or that it is not just or convenient that the judgment should be enforced in Singapore, or that there is some other sufficient reason for setting aside the registration, the Court may order the registration of the judgment to be set aside on such terms as the Court thinks fit.

Step 5: Issuance of enforcement order by Court 
O 60 r 10
(1) Enforcement order issued after period to set aside registration expired: An enforcement order must not be issued to enforce a judgment registered under the first Act or second Act until after the expiration of the period which, in accordance with Rule 5(2), is specified in the order for registration as the period within which an application may be made to set aside the registration or (if that period has been extended by the Court) until after the expiration of that period as so extended.
(2) Enforcement order issued after application to set aside determined: If an application is made to set aside the registration of a judgment, an enforcement order to enforce the judgment must not be issued until after such application is finally determined.
(3) Any party wishing to apply for an enforcement order to enforce a judgment registered under the first Act or second Act must produce to the Court an affidavit of service of the notice of registration of the judgment and any order made by the Court in relation to the judgment.

Arbitration awards under Arbitration Act
O 34 r 14 
(1) Make application without notice supported by affidavit which includes: An application under section 37 or 46 for permission to enforce an award may be made without notice and must be supported by an affidavit:
a. Exhibiting the arbitration agreement or any record of the content of the arbitration agreement and the original award or, in either case, a copy of the arbitration agreement or record and a copy of the award;
b. Stating the name and the usual or last known place of residence or business of the applicant (called in this Rule the creditor) and the person against whom it is sought to enforce the award (called in this Rule the debtor), respectively; and
c. As the case may require, stating either that the award has not been complied with or the extent to which it has not been complied with at the date of the application.
(2) Judgement creditor serve order to enforce arbitral award on judgement debtor An order granting permission must be drawn up by or on behalf of the creditor and must be served on the debtor:
a. By delivering a copy to the debtor personally;
b. By sending a copy to the debtor at the debtor’s usual or last known place of residence or business; or
c. In such other manner as the Court may direct.
(3) Service of the order out of Singapore is permissible without permission, and Order 8, Rules 2, 3, 7 and 8 apply in relation to such an order.
(4) Judgement debtor apply to set aside order within 14 days after service of order: Within 14 days after service of the order or, if the order is to be served out of Singapore, within such other period as the Court may fix, the debtor may apply to set aside the order and the award must not be enforced until after the expiration of that period or, if the debtor applies within that period to set aside the order, until after the application is finally disposed of.
(5) The copy of the order served on the debtor must state the effect of paragraph (4).
(6) In relation to a body corporate, this Rule has effect as if for any reference to the place of residence or business of the creditor or the debtor there were substituted a reference to the registered or principal address of the body corporate.
(7) Nothing in paragraph (6) affects any written law which provides for the manner in which a document may be served on a body corporate.

Arbitration awards under International Arbitration Act 
O 48 r 6 
(1) Local award – Make application without notice supported by affidavit which includes: An application for permission to enforce an award may be made without notice and must be supported by an affidavit:
a. Exhibiting the arbitration agreement or any record of the content of the arbitration agreement and the duly authenticated original award or, in either case, a duly certified copy of the arbitration agreement or record and a duly certified copy of the award and, where the award, agreement or record is in a language other than English, a translation of it in the English language, duly certified in English as a correct translation by a sworn translator or by an official or by a diplomatic or consular agent of the country in which the award was made;
b. Stating the name and the usual or last known place of residence or business of the applicant (called in this Rule the creditor) and the person against whom it is sought to enforce the award (called in this Rule the debtor) respectively; and
c. As the case may require, stating either that the award has not been complied with or the extent to which it has not been complied with at the date of the application.
(2) Foreign award – Make application without notice supported by affidavit which includes: An application for permission to enforce a foreign award may be made without notice and must be supported by an affidavit:
a. Exhibiting the arbitration agreement and the duly authenticated original award or, in either case, a duly certified copy of the arbitration agreement or award and, where the award or agreement is in a language other than English, a translation of it in the English language, duly certified in English as a correct translation by a sworn translator or by an official or by a diplomatic or consular agent of the country in which the award was made
b. Stating the name and the usual or last known place of residence or business of the applicant (called in this Rule the creditor) and the person against whom it is sought to enforce the award (called in this Rule the debtor) respectively; and
c. As the case may require, stating either that the award has not been complied with or the extent to which it has not been complied with at the date of the application
(3) Judgement creditor serve order to enforce arbitral award on judgement debtor: An order giving permission must be drawn up by or on behalf of the creditor and must be served on the debtor by delivering a copy to the debtor personally or by sending a copy to the debtor at the debtor’s usual or last known place of residence or business or in such other manner as the Court may direct.
(4) Service of the order out of Singapore is permissible without permission, and Order 8, Rules 2, 3, 7 and 8 apply in relation to such an order.
(5) Judgement debtor apply to set aside order within 14 days after service of order: Within 14 days after service of the order or, if the order is to be served out of Singapore, within such other period as the Court may fix, the debtor may apply to set aside the order, and the award must not be enforced until after the expiration of that period or, if the debtor applies within that period to set aside the order, until after the application is finally disposed of.
(6) The copy of that order served on the debtor must state the effect of paragraph (5).
(7) In relation to a body corporate, this Rule has effect as if for any reference to the place of residence or business of the creditor or the debtor there were substituted a reference to the registered or principal address of the body corporate; so, however, that nothing in this Rule affects any enactment which provides for the manner in which a document may be served on a body corporate.


*D. COSTS
As a general rule, as part of the court’s discretion on costs, the court will order that COSTS SHOULD FOLLOW THE EVENT (i.e. winning party gets paid its costs), unless there are circumstances justifying departure from this general rule.
E.g. despite having won the outcome, the winning party acted very unreasonably during proceedings – so the court may not grant winning party its costs, or to grant costs with a large discount

*What is considered a cost in contentious legal business? 
Definition of “costs” and “contentious business” 
O 21 r 1(2)
- Contentious business: Same definition as per s2(1) of Legal Profession Act 
- Costs: Includes fees, charges, disbursements, expenses and remuneration 

S2(1) of LPA
- Contentious business: Business done in or for the purposes of proceedings begun before a court of justice or before an arbitrator
- Wide definition: not just during court proceeding but includes actions in pre-action period, so includes letter of demand and drafting of statement of claim

Examples of costs for contentious business
Legis Point LLC v Tay Choon Ai
- The following costs considered as costs for contentious business => Will be taken into consideration when Court orders P&P costs:
a. Costs incurred in preparation of writ of summons and statement of claim 
b. Costs incurred as part of preparation for without prejudice negotiations prior to filing of writ 
(i) Review of documents and drafting of correspondence
(ii) Attendance in settlement negotiations with opposing party
(iii) Even though the negotiations were without prejudice, the very existence of negotiations can still be raised to the Court in determination of P&P costs 
c. Costs incurred in respect of mediation after writ is filed 
(i) There were existing court proceedings here as writ was filed and the mediation work to settle could be described as incidental to court proceedings 

*Difference between S&C costs and P&P costs
S&C costs: Costs that are payable by a party to his own solicitor in relation to work done by the solicitor in respect of a contentious or non-contentious matter
- Usually based on whatever is agreed in the Letter of Engagement between party and his solicitor 
- Payable regardless of outcome of case 

P&P costs: Costs payable by “losing party” to “winning party” after Court has given judgement 
- Party paying P&P costs depends on outcome of case 

Interaction between S&C costs and P&P costs 
1. Party’s obligation to pay S&C costs is independent of whether party can recover P&P costs
a. Agreement to tag S&C costs payable to whether P&P costs is recoverable is illegal => Indirectly makes S&C costs subject to whether solicitor wins the case 
b. Agreement to limit quantum of S&C costs payable to quantum of P&P costs awarded to either party is legal (Legis Point LLC) --> i.e. capping S&C costs at amount of P&P costs eventually awarded to either party is OK, because the S&C cost would be payable anyways even if P&P costs is awarded or not 

2. Quantum of P&P costs recovered by winning party cannot be more than the quantum of S&C costs payable by the winning party to his solicitor (s112(2) LPA) 
a. Reason: P&P costs are supposed to be compensatory to compensate the winning party’s legal costs and they are not supposed to give winning party a windfall 

3. Quantum of P&P costs recovered by winning party usually represent a portion of S&C costs payable by winning party to his solicitor (Maryani Sadeli v Arjun Permanand Samtani) 
a. Reason for P&P costs to not be equal to S&C costs: The cost regime is not just concerned with compensation but is also interested in enhancing access to justice for all and thus the P&P costs payable by losing party should not be so high that it would be overly prohibitive on risk-averse claimants who have meritorious claims 

*Entitlement to P&P costs 
3 sources of a party’s entitlement to costs from another party 
1. Legislation 
2. Contract 
3. Judgement or Court order (see below)

Legislation 
- Certain legislative provisions give a party the right to demand for P&P costs from another party upon the occurrence of certain defined events without the need for any extra Court order 

Recovery of money payable to SAL 
S25 of Singapore Academy of Law Act
- The Senate may recover any subscription or other moneys due and payable to the Academy and any interest thereon as a debt due to the Academy and the Academy is entitled to the payment of its full costs as between solicitor and client.

Costs of making amendments without permission => Paid by party making amendment
O 21 r 3(3)
- The costs of and occasioned by any amendment made without permission in the originating claim or any pleadings must be borne by the party making the amendment unless the Court otherwise orders.

Costs of applications for time extension => Paid by party making application
O 21 r 3(4)
- The costs of and occasioned by any application to extend the time fixed by these Rules or the Court, for serving or filing any document or doing any other act must be borne by the party making the application, unless the Court otherwise orders.

Costs of trustee, PR, mortgagee who act on behalf of a party in proceedings => Paid by trust fund, PR, mortgaged property
O 21 r 3(5)
- Where a person is or has been a party to any proceedings in the capacity of trustee, personal representative or mortgagee, that person is, unless the Court otherwise orders, entitled to the costs of those proceedings, insofar as they are not recovered from or paid by any other person, out of the fund held by the trustee or personal representative or out of the mortgaged property, as the case may be.

Where Claimant discontinues action or claim against Defendant without permission => Claimant has to pay Defendant’s cost of action 
O 21 r 9(1)
- Where:
a. A claimant by notice in writing and without permission either wholly discontinues the claimant’s action against any defendant or withdraws any particular claim made by the claimant in an action against any defendant; or
b. An action is deemed discontinued,
the defendant is, unless the Court otherwise orders, entitled to the defendant’s costs of the action or claim incurred to the time of discontinuance, which may be assessed if not agreed between the parties, and the defendant may sign judgment for the defendant’s assessed costs after 48 hours after assessment.

Contract 
Examples of party’s contractual entitlement to costs 
- Contract providing for legal costs to be paid by one party to another in the event of a dispute 
- Contract providing basis for how legal costs should be computed in the event of a dispute  
- Contract providing for no cost-shifting i.e. if matter goes to Court, each party will bear its own cost 

Principle 1: Party enforcing costs agreement must clearly and properly plead it as a cause of action 
Telemedia Pacific Group Ltd v Credit Agricole (Suisse) SA (Yeh Mao-Yuan, third party) 
- Claim for costs under court’s ordinary statutory discretion need not be pleaded because costs issue will naturally arise in all cases at the end of the hearing 
- But if party wants to enforce its contractual rights directly under a costs agreement, such a cause of action would have to be clearly and properly pleaded 

Abani Trading Pte Ltd v BNP Paribas 
- Clause 11.4 provides for costs on an indemnity basis:
“The Borrower shall fully indemnify and keep indemnified the Bank (at its head office and any and all branches of the Bank), its agents, sub-agents, Affiliates and every director, officer, employee or agent of any of the foregoing against any and all losses, damages, reasonable costs and expenses (including but not limited to legal costs on a full indemnity basis), charges, actions, suits, proceedings, orders, warrants, injunctions, claims or demands which may be brought against any of them or which any of them may suffer or incur in connection with or arising from (i) the provision of any Facilities or the Borrower’s account(s) held with the Bank or its Affiliates, or (ii) any transaction referable to, involving or relating to the Borrower or the Facilities or the Borrower’s account(s) held with the Bank or its Affiliates ... save where the same arises directly from their respective gross negligence, wilful misconduct or fraud. ..”
- Issue: Whether a claim for costs on an indemnity basis has to be specifically pleaded? 
- Held: Undecided but it is prudent to do so for contractual provisions 
1. Where claim for indemnity cost is premised entirely on a contractual provision governing both parties => It would be prudent for party who intends to rely on the contractual provision to specifically plead it => Avoids any argument that the other party was taken by surprised or prejudiced because of lack of notice 
2. Include the contractual provision in pleadings to allow other party to make informed decision whether there is a need to adduce evidence to address enforceability of the contractual provision 

Principle 2: Legal uncertainty as to the ways for party to enforce costs agreement 
Telemedia Pacific Group Ltd v Credit Agricole (Suisse) SA (Yeh Mao-Yuan, third party) 
- Currently 2 ways for party to enforce costs agreement: 
1. Sue directly under contractual agreement on the basis that the agreement ousts the Court’s statutory discretion to award costs 
2. Rely on the Court’s discretion but to assert that the costs agreement is a relevant factor in determining the basis of the award 
- Legal issues which arise from the 2 ways: 
a. Inequality in the 2 ways of enforcement: In Abani Trading, where Court’s statutory discretion to award costs is invoked, Court may disregard contractual agreement between the parties if it would be manifestly unjust to uphold the agreement => It is unclear whether Court can also invoke the same discretion when asked to directly enforce a contractual agreement on costs between two commercial parties. Especially when Courts ordinarily cannot disregard contractual rights purely on grounds of hardship or unfairness 

Principle 3: Indemnity clauses requiring a party to fully indemnify another party regardless of whether indemnified party wins suit is unfair and would require clear and express language to be effective  
Telemedia Pacific Group Ltd v Credit Agricole (Suisse) SA (Yeh Mao-Yuan, third party) 
- A clause that requires the borrower to indemnify the bank against legal costs arising out of a borrower’s successful suit against the bank => It is a clause that grants full indemnity to the bank against any litigation by the borrower even if the bank was truly guilty of wrongdoing and this is uncommercial, unfair and unreasonable => Far clearer and mor express language would have to be used to achieve that effect 
*By Judgment or Court order 
If not from legislation or contract, a party is only entitled to recover costs of proceedings from any other party pursuant to a Court order 
O 21 r 3(1) 
- Except where expressly provided for by legislation or contract, no party is entitled to recover any costs of or incidental to any proceedings from any other party to the proceedings except under an order of the Court 

Costs are at Court’s discretion and can be sought at any stage of proceedings
O 21 r 2(1) 
- Subject to any written law, costs are in the discretion of the Court and the Court has the power to determine all issues relating to the costs of or incidental to all proceedings in the Supreme Court or the State Courts at any stage of the proceedings or after the conclusion of the proceedings.

In practice: Court will make costs orders at the end of each interlocutory stage and at the end of proceedings 
Note: 
- In practice: After each interlocutory stage, Court will ask parties to make submissions on cost orders for that particular interlocutory stage => Court will then make costs orders for that interlocutory stage 
- Parties can also request for Court to make costs orders at end of proceedings 
Costs orders are consequential orders and has to be sought within the same or related proceedings (Chin Yoke Choong Bobby v Hong Lam Marine). 
- Thus, costs payable by any party to any other party in any matter must be fixed by the Court which heard the matter, unless the Court thinks fit to direct an assessment of the costs (O 21 r 2(3) ROC).

In exercising its power to fix or assess costs, the Court must have regard to all relevant circumstances, including:
O 21 r 2(2) 
- In exercising its power to fix or assess costs, the Court must have regard to all relevant circumstances, including:
a. Efforts to reach amicable resolution: Efforts made by the parties at amicable resolution;
b. Complexity of case and novelty of questions: The complexity of the case and the difficulty or novelty of the questions involved;
c. Skill, specialized knowledge, time labor expended by solicitor: The skill, specialised knowledge and responsibility required of, and the time and labour expended by, the solicitor
d. Urgency and importance of action to parties: The urgency and importance of the action to the parties;
e. Number of solicitors involved: The number of solicitors involved in the case for each party;
f. Parties’ conduct: The conduct of the parties;
g. Proportionality: The principle of proportionality; and
h. Stage which proceedings conclude: The stage at which the proceedings were concluded

*Step 1: Court’s starting point when determining party’s percentage entitlement to costs is the principle that – Costs follows the event 
Costs follows the event: Unsuccessful party will have to pay the legal costs of the successful party 
O 21 r 3(2) 
- The Court must, subject to this Order, order the costs of any proceedings in favour of a successful party, except when it appears to the Court that in the circumstances of the case some other order should be made as to the whole or any part of the costs
Exceptions – 
- Ex parte applications – applicant always pays unless contested 
- 3p exceptions – e.g. application for 3p interrogatories – applicant pays 3p’s costs 
- Amendment of pleadings – usually the amending party pays 

Determining who is the “successful party”
Comfort Management Pte Ltd v OGSP Engineering Pte Ltd – Claimant successful party even though he only recovered 26% of its claim, succeeded in two out of six issues and partially succeeded in another two 
- Held: Plaintiff was the “successful party” in the action as the First Defendant must pay a substantial sum to the Plaintiff and the Plaintiff would not have recovered that substantial sum if it had not succeeded in claims against the First Defendant  
1. Even though:
a. Plaintiff recovered only 26% of its $1.5mil claim
b. Plaintiff had secured an unqualified success on only two of the six issues and partial success on another two  
c. The two issues were minor both in terms of quantum and costs 
Principle – not a numbers game, court will look at substance over form. E.g. number of days taken for a small-quantum issue may outweigh the value of a large-quantum issue that was decided within 1 day of trial 

Alfro-Asia Shipping v Da Zhong Investment – Defendant successful party as Claimant only managed to prove 1 out of 100 items of damage and could only recover 1% of its original claim 
- Held: Defendant was successful party in the action and was awarded 85% of his costs 
1. Plaintiff only manage to prove 1 out of the 100 items of damage that had been listed in its statement of claim 
2. Plaintiff would only be able to recover 1% of its original claim 
3. Defendants were thus the winning party in respect of the plaintiff’s claim against them  

*Step 2: Court may depart from the starting point in certain circumstances 
Court may disallow or reduce successful party’s entitlement to costs because of successful party’s poor conduct 
O 21 r 4
- The Court may disallow or reduce a successful party’s costs or order that party to pay costs, if:
a. Successful party unnecessarily increased amount of time, costs or complexity of proceedings: That party has failed to establish any claim or issue which that party has raised in any proceedings, thereby unnecessarily increasing the amount of time taken, the costs or the complexity of the proceedings;
b. Successful party did or omitted to do something unreasonably: That party has done or omitted to do anything unreasonably;
c. Successful party did not discharge duty to consider amicable resolution: That party has not discharged that party’s duty to consider amicable resolution of the dispute or to make an offer of amicable resolution in accordance with Order 5; or
=> Para 53 of Supreme Court PD: Solicitors duty to advise their clients on potential adverse costs for any unreasonable refusal to engage in ADR 
d. Successful party failed to comply with order of court or PD: That party has failed to comply with any order of court, any relevant pre?action protocol or any practice direction.

Case Law – Principles 
Principle 1: Costs should follow the event unless it appears to the Court that some other order should be made (Tullio Planeta v Maoro Andrew G) 

Principle 2: Costs should follow the event even if the successful party had raised issues or makes allegations on which he fails => Court should only deprive successful party of costs if he has caused a significant increase in length or costs of proceedings (Tullio Planeta v Maoro Andrew G)

Principle 3: Where successful party raises issues or makes improper or unreasonable allegations => Court may deprive successful party’s entitlement to costs and even order him to pay the unsuccessful party’s costs 
(Tullio Planeta v Maoro Andrew G)

E.g. Successful party unnecessarily increasing complexity of proceedings 
Raffles Town Club v Lim Eng Hock Peter and others (Tung Yu-Lien Margaret and others, third parties) 
- Rule: Court will consider whether successful party: 
a. Put forward and argued at length plainly unsustainable, unmeritorious or unreasonable issues 
b. Presented a false case or false evidence 
- Held: Peter was successful party but was deprived of PnP costs
1. There were significant evidence establishing that Peter Lim was a de facto director and beneficial shareholder of RTC => Peter Lim still denied
2. Peter also unnecessarily protracted and added to the complexity of the proceedings by concealing his beneficial shareholding of 40% in RTC and the very substantial payments of money and distribution of other benefits arising out of that large beneficial shareholding 

E.g. Successful party should not be deprived of its costs even though it failed on one ground 
MCST No. 473 v De Beers Jewelry Pte Ltd 
- Held: Successful party had failed on one ground, but it should not be deprived of its cost  
1. Trial had ended within allocated time 
2. Successful party relied on the same facts for the failed ground and successful ground and unsuccessful party relied on the same defenses for both grounds => There was no significant increase in cost of proceedings 

*Court can make adverse costs orders against solicitor in certain scenarios 
If solicitor incurs costs unreasonably in the proceedings => Court may make certain orders 
O 21 r 6(1)
- If the solicitor is responsible, either personally or through an employee or agent, for incurring costs unreasonably in the proceedings, the Court may:
a. Disallow S&C costs in whole or in part: Disallow the costs as between the solicitor and his or her client in whole or in part;
b. Order solicitor to reimburse P&P costs paid by client: Order the solicitor to repay to his or her client costs which the client has been ordered to pay in the proceedings; and
c. Order solicitor to indemnify any other party: Order the solicitor to indemnify any other party in the proceedings for costs payable by them.

Court must give solicitor a reasonable opportunity to be heard before ordering adverse costs orders against them 
O 21 r 6(2)
- Before the Court makes an order under paragraph (1), the Court must give the solicitor a reasonable opportunity to be heard, either by way of an oral hearing or by written submissions.

3-step test to determine if costs should be ordered against solicitors personally 
Nagaenthran a/l K Dharmalingam v AG and anor matter [2022] SGCA 44
- 3-step test to determine if costs should be ordered against solicitors personally: 
1. Has the solicitor acted improperly, unreasonably or negligently?
2. Did such conduct cause the applicant to incur unnecessary costs?
3. Is it in all the circumstances just to order the solicitors to compensate the applicant for the whole or any part of the relevant costs? 

- Held: Personal costs orders sought against both Mr Ravi and Ms Netto
1. The arguments advanced had factual weaknesses and problems which would have been apparent to any reasonable defense counsel 
2. Both solicitors drip-fed supposed evidence and arguments and their conduct amounted to blatant and egregious abuse of the court’s processes 
3. AGC had incurred unnecessary costs 

Note:
- O 21 r 6(3): The Court may direct that notice be given to the solicitor’s client concerning any proceedings or order against the solicitor under this Rule.
- O 21 r 6(4): The Court may, if the Court thinks fit, direct or authorise the Attorney?General to attend and take part in any proceedings or inquiry under this Rule, and may make such order as the Court thinks fit as to the payment of the Attorney?General’s costs

Court can make adverse costs orders against non-party in certain scenarios
O 21 r 5(1)
- Where it is just to do so, the Court may order costs against a non?party if the non?party has:
a. Scenario 1: Non-party assigned rights in action to a party in return for a share of any money which party may recover in the action: Assigned the non?party’s right in the action to a party in return for a share of any money or property which that party may recover in the action;
b. Scenario 2: Non-party contributed to a party’s legal cost in return for a share of money recovered in action: Contributed or agreed to contribute to a party’s costs in return for a share of any money or property which that party may recover in the action; or
c. Scenario 3: Non-party contributed to party’s legal costs and instigated party to continue with action: Contributed or agreed to contribute to a party’s costs and actively instigates or encourages that party to continue with the action.

Court must give non-party a reasonable opportunity to be heard before ordering adverse costs orders against them 
O 21 r 5(2)
- Before the Court makes an order under paragraph (1), the Court must give the non?party a reasonable opportunity to be heard, either by way of an oral hearing or by written submissions.

In multi-party actions, Court can order 2 parties’ costs to be set off against each other
- E.g. Claimant commences against 2 defendants but succeeds only against one and loses to another => Court can make: 
a. Bullock Order: Claimant ordered to pay winning defendant costs, but claimant is permitted to recover those costs together with his own costs from losing defendant  
b. Sanderson order:  Claimant and winning defendant both recover costs directly from losing defendant 

O 21 r 2(5)
- The Court may order 2 or more parties’ costs to be set off against one another so that only the balance has to be paid

Grains and Industrial Products Trading Pte Ltd 
- Bullock Order: Claimant ordered to pay winning defendant costs but claimant is permitted to recover those costs together with his own costs from losing defendant  
- Sanderson order:  Claimant and winning defendant both recover costs directly from losing defendant 
- Purpose: 
* Avoid injustice befalling to a claimant who reasonably did not know whom to pursue for the wrong done to him 
- Factors considered by Court:
a. Whether costs had been reasonably and properly incurred by the claimant between himself and the winning defendant => Whether the conduct of the unsuccessful defendant contributed to these costs being incurred by the claimant => Whether it would be unjust on an unsuccessful defendant to make him liable for the costs of another defendant against whom the claimant had failed 
b. Where claimant’s claim against two defendants are distinct and separate causes of action => No Bullock Order or Sanderson Order 

After party’s percentage entitlement to costs has been established
1. Whether costs are to be fixed or assessed
2. Where costs are to be assessed, whether this is done on a standard or indemnity basis 
3. When should costs be paid
4. Consequences for non-payment

Step 3: After determining each party’s percentage entitlement to costs, Court will assess whether the quantum of costs should be fixed or assessed 
Default is fixed unless Court thinks fit to direct assessment
O 21 r 2(3) 
- Subject to the provisions of this Order and any written law, the costs payable by any party to any other party in any matter must be fixed by the Court which heard the matter after an oral hearing or by way of written submissions from the parties, unless the Court thinks fit to direct an assessment of the costs

Step 4A: Where costs are fixed, how should it be done? 
Court’s considerations when fixing costs 
O 21 r 2(2) 
- In exercising its power to fix or assess costs, the Court must have regard to all relevant circumstances, including:
a. Efforts to reach amicable resolution: Efforts made by the parties at amicable resolution;
b. Complexity of case and novelty of questions: The complexity of the case and the difficulty or novelty of the questions involved;
c. Skill, specialized knowledge, time labor expended by solicitor: The skill, specialised knowledge and responsibility required of, and the time and labour expended by, the solicitor
d. Urgency and importance of action to parties: The urgency and importance of the action to the parties;
e. Number of solicitors involved: The number of solicitors involved in the case for each party;
f. Parties’ conduct: The conduct of the parties;
g. Proportionality: The principle of proportionality; and
h. Stage which proceedings conclude: The stage at which the proceedings were concluded

Cost order must be sought from same Court which heard matter
O 21 r 2(3)
- Subject to the provisions of this Order and any written law, the costs payable by any party to any other party in any matter must be fixed by the Court which heard the matter after an oral hearing or by way of written submissions from the parties, unless the Court thinks fit to direct an assessment of the costs.

Note: 
- Costs orders should be sought at the end of interlocutory stage or end of proceedings from the same court 
- Costs orders cannot be an independent cause of action if it is consequential on the results of another previous proceeding 

Procedure for fixing costs 
Part 1: Parties must file Cost Schedule (Form 34) with written closing submissions or before oral closing submissions (when there are no written closing submissions) 
Para 137 of Supreme Court PD 2021
- Applies to (a) trials of originating claims in open court, including originating claims that were ordered to be converted from originating applications and (b) originating applications involving cross-examination of any maker of an affidavit
- Parties file costs schedule: Each party is required to file costs schedule using Form 34 in Appendix B of PD 
- Time of filing: Costs schedule must be filed together with parties’ written closing submissions or where there are no written closing submissions, before parties’ oral closing submissions are presented 

Contents of costs schedule
Para 137 of Supreme Court PD 2021
- Costs Schedule should: 
* Set out with sufficient particularity the quantum of party-and-party costs and disbursements each party intends to claim in the event that the party succeeds 
* Parties should have regard to the Costs Guidelines 

Costs Guidelines 
Order 20 Appendix 1
O 21 r 10
- In the cases to which Appendix 1 to this Order applies, the amount of costs allowed are as set out in that Appendix, unless the Court otherwise orders

Order 20 Appendix 1
Para 1(1)
- Scale of costs set out in Part 2 applies to:
a. Cases in which the defendant pays the amount claimed within the time and in the manner required by the endorsement of the originating claim;
b. Cases in which the claimant obtains final judgment in default of a notice of intention to contest or not contest or in default of defence;
c. Cases in which:
(i) The claimant obtains final judgment under Order 9, Rule 17 unconditionally;
(ii) The Court dismisses an application for summary judgment under Order 9, Rule 17; or
(iii) The Court gives the defendant against whom an application for summary judgment under Order 9, Rule 17 is made unconditional permission to defend.

Note:
- Para 1(2): Part 2 does not apply where the claimant is also entitled under the judgment to damages to be assessed, or where the claimant claims any relief of the nature specified in Order 52, Rule 1
- In respect of the cases set out in sub?paragraph (1)(a) and (b), where the claimant is entitled under the judgment to costs on an indemnity basis, the scale of costs and disbursements set out in both Parts 2 and 3 of this Appendix apply.

Part 2 of Order 20 Appendix 1
- Basic Costs for situations in Para 1(1)(a), 1(1)(b), 1(1)(c)
- Costs for additional items 
- Costs for disbursements (Part 3)
Appendix G of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021
Provides guidelines for party-and-party costs in the Supreme Court (if transferred from HC to SICC, see CBX v CBZ)

Seems to apply to: 
a. Summons in full trial 
b. Full trials – Pre-trial work, Trial work, Post-trial work 
c. Cases settled before trial – Pre-trial work (Categorized by different areas of law)
d. General OAs and specific OAs
e. Appeals 

Appendix G: 
a. If contested summons is not listed in Part IIB (Specific Costs Guidelines), reference should be made to Part IIA (General Costs Guidelines) 
b. If contested summons is listed in Part IIB, reference should be made to the costs range inside 
(i) If the contested summons is simple and the lower end of the costs range set out in Part IIB is higher than the lower end of the costs range set out in Part IIA, reference may be made to Part IIA costs range
(ii) If the contested summons is complex and the upper end of the costs range set out in Part IIB is lower than the upper end of the costs range set out in Part IIA, reference may be made to the Part IIA costs range

Part 2: Court will conduct oral hearing or by way of written submissions 
O 21 r 2(3)
- Court hears parties by oral hearing or by way of written submissions 

Practically: 
- Court will start from the Costs Schedules filed by parties and make adjustments after hearing from parties whether costs orders should tend to the upper or lower limit of the range 

Step 4B: Where costs are assessed, how should it be done? 
Part 1: Court must determine if assessment of costs should be done on a standard basis or indemnity basis 
Costs assessed on standard basis – All costs not allowed unless they are reasonable
O 21 r 22(2)
- Assessment of costs on standard basis:
* Costs are a reasonable amount in respect of all costs reasonably incurred; and
* Any doubts which the Registrar may have as to whether the costs were reasonably incurred or were reasonable in amount are to be resolved in favor of the paying party

Costs assessed on indemnity basis – All costs allowed unless they are unreasonable 
O 21 r 22(3) 
- Assessment of costs on indemnity basis: 
* Costs are all costs except insofar as they are of an unreasonable amount or have been unreasonably incurred; and 
* Any doubts which the Registrar may have as to whether the costs were reasonably incurred or were reasonable in amount are to be resolved in favor of the receiving party  

Part 2: The default position is assessment on a standard basis 
O 21 r 22(1)
- Subject to the other provisions of these Rules, the amount of costs which any party is entitled to recover is the amount allowed after assessment on the standard basis where:
a. An order is made that the costs of one party to the proceedings be paid by another party to those proceedings;
b. An order is made for the payment of costs out of any fund; or
c. No order for costs is required,
unless it appears to the Court to be appropriate to order costs to be assessed on the indemnity basis.

O 21 r 22(4)
- Where the Court makes an order for assessment of costs without indicating the basis of assessment or on any basis other than the standard basis or the indemnity basis, the costs are to be assessed on the standard basis

Part 3: Court will depart from default position and order assessment on an indemnity basis if party’s conduct is poor 
Indemnity basis ordered if party’s conduct evinced dishonesty, impropriety or abuse of judicial process
Raffles Town Club Pte Ltd v Lim Eng Hock Peter and others (Tung Yu-Lien Margaret and others, third parties) 
- Rule: Indemnity basis will be ordered where party’s conduct evinced such a degree of dishonesty, impropriety or abuse of judicial process to warrant a departure from the usual basis of costs
- Held: Standard basis was ordered
1. Even though RTC’s conduct merited criticism as they relied on claims inconsistent with positions previously taken, relied on documents which were inadmissible in law and adopted tactics that caused embarrassment to Peter Lim => RTC’s conduct did not reach the threshold for indemnity basis to be ordered

Tjong Very Sumio v Antia Investments Pte Ltd 
- Held: Indemnity costs was ordered
1. Party deliberately ignored an arbitration or a jurisdiction clause to derive from its own breach of contract an unjustifiable procedural advantage 
2. Party’s act constitutes a breach of contract and a misuse of judicial facilities offered by English courts or a foreign court 

Situations where Court will order assessment on an indemnity basis 
Tan Chin Yew Joseph v Saxo Capital Markets Pte Ltd 
- Court would order assessment on indemnity basis against a litigant who: 
a. Bring case with ulterior or improper motive: Brings his case with an ulterior motive or for an improper purpose
b. Conduct case in bad faith or as personal vendetta: Conducts his cases in bad faith or as a personal vendetta
c. Conduct case in improper or oppressive manner: Conducts their case in an improper or oppressive manner
d. Caused costs to be incurred irrationally: Causes costs to be incurred irrationally or out of all proportion as to what is at stake 
e. Pursues speculative, weak or thin claim: Chooses to pursue a claim that is speculative, weak, opportunistic or thin 
f. Makes and extensively publicizes unsuccessful allegations: Makes unsuccessful allegations which are the subject of extensive publicity courted by the unsuccessful litigant
g. Aggressively pursues allegations of dishonesty and impropriety: Advances and aggressively pursues serious and wide ranging allegations of dishonesty or impropriety over an extended period of time;
h. Aggressively pursues allegations despite lack of documentary evidence: Advances and aggressively pursues such allegations, despite the lack of any foundation in the documentary evidence for those allegations, and maintains the allegations, without apology, to the bitter end;
i. Actively seeks to court publicity for its serious allegations both before and during the trial in the international, and national and local media;
j. By its conduct, turns a case into an unprecedented factual enquiry by the pursuit of an unjustified case;
k. Pursues a claim which is, to put it most charitably, thin and, in some respects, far-fetched;
l. Pursues a claim which is irreconcilable with the contemporaneous documents;
m. Commences and pursues large-scale and expensive litigation in circumstances calculated to exert commercial pressure on a defendant, and during the course of the trial of the action, the claimant resorts to advancing a constantly changing case in order to justify the allegations which it has made, only then to suffer a resounding defeat.
- Held: Standard basis was ordered 
1. Even though plaintiff’s case was thin and ultimately irreconcilable with contemporaneous documents and the law + plaintiff’s case was merely an afterthought which was dispatched quickly during cross-examination 
2. However, plaintiff’s conduct did not reach the threshold for indemnity basis to be ordered: 
a. Plaintiff had no ulterior motive in bringing proceedings and it was a good faith attempt to secure compensation to which he genuinely thought he was entitled
b. Plaintiff had not conducted his case in an oppressive or unreasonable manner or caused costs to be incurred irrationally or disproportionately 
c. Plaintiff’s lawyer did not protract cross-examination of defendant’s witness by pursuing hopeless aspects of the plaintiff’s case or side issues of marginal relevance 
d. Cross-examination of four defense witnesses were done efficiently in a little over a day 

Part 4: Ultimately, Court will also consider the relevant circumstances in O 21 r 2(2) when assessing costs 
O 21 r 2(2) 
- In exercising its power to fix or assess costs, the Court must have regard to all relevant circumstances, including:
a. Efforts to reach amicable resolution: Efforts made by the parties at amicable resolution;
b. Complexity of case and novelty of questions: The complexity of the case and the difficulty or novelty of the questions involved;
c. Skill, specialized knowledge, time labor expended by solicitor: The skill, specialised knowledge and responsibility required of, and the time and labour expended by, the solicitor
d. Urgency and importance of action to parties: The urgency and importance of the action to the parties;
e. Number of solicitors involved: The number of solicitors involved in the case for each party;
f. Parties’ conduct: The conduct of the parties;
g. Proportionality: The principle of proportionality; and
h. Stage which proceedings conclude: The stage at which the proceedings were concluded

Step 5: When should costs be paid? 
Costs are payable from date of Court’s costs order
O 21 r 2(4) 
- The costs in any matter are payable from the date of the Court’s order unless the parties otherwise agree

Note:
- In practice: Parties might agree to defer payment of costs especially when the case is on appeal and there is a chance that the costs order would be substantially different based on the outcome of the appeal 

Step 6: What are consequences for non-payment?
If party refuses to pay costs from previous proceedings => Court can prevent party from commencing or continuing new proceedings 
O 21 r 2(6)
- If party refuses or refuses or neglects to pay any costs ordered within the specified time, whether the costs were ordered in the present proceedings or in some related proceedings => The Court may stay or dismiss any application, action or appeal or make any other order as the Court deems fit 

If party refuses to pay costs => Costs will carry interests at 5.33% per year from the date mentioned in second column 
O 21 r 29(1)
- The costs mentioned in the first column of the following table carry interest at 5.33% per year from the date mentioned in the second column of the table until payment:



Procedure for assessed P&P costs (for information only) 
Step 1: Party entitled to cost (Applicant) will file Bill of Costs (BC) 
Applicant must file BC within 12 months after matter is finally disposed 
O 21 r 17 
- A party entitled to have any costs assessed must file the bill of costs within 12 months after the date on which the entire cause or matter is finally disposed of, including any appeals arising, unless the Court otherwise orders.

Step 2: Registrar gives date of assessment hearing
Registrar will give Applicant 14 days’ notice of date and time of assessment hearing
O 21 r 18 
- Where the bill of costs has been filed in accordance with Rule 17, the Registrar must give to the party beginning the proceedings at least 14 days’ notice of the date and time appointed for assessment.

Step 3: Applicant must send BC to every party entitled to be heard
Applicant must send BC to every party entitled to be heard within 2 days after receiving notice from Registrar
O 21 r 19(1)
- A party whose costs are to be assessed in any assessment proceedings must, within 2 days after receiving a notice of the date and time under Rule 18, send a copy of the party’s bill of costs to every other party entitled to be heard in the proceedings.

Applicant need not send BC to parties who did not contest in main proceedings 
O 21 r 19(2)
- Notice need not be given to any party who has not filed and served a notice of intention to contest or not contest or taken any part in the proceedings which gave rise to the assessment proceedings.

Step 4: Anyone objecting to BC must (Respondent) must file and serve Notice of Dispute 
Respondent must file and serve Notice of Dispute (Form 33) at least 14 days before assessment hearing 
Para 133(1) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Any objections in principle or as to quantum of the items claimed in a bill of costs must be indicated by the filing and service of a Notice of Dispute in Form 33 of Appendix B of these Practice Directions at least 14 days before the date fixed by the Registrar for the assessment of the bill of costs

Respondent must file Notice of Dispute through Electronic Filing Service => Respondent must include Notice of Dispute summary where Respondent must state the amount of costs the Applicant should be awarded
Para 133(2) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021
- The Notice of Dispute must be filed through the Electronic Filing Service in Portable Document Format (PDF) and be accompanied by a Notice of Dispute summary, the electronic form of which will be composed online through the Electronic Filing Service. 
- The information required by the Electronic Filing Service to compose the Notice of Dispute summary includes the amounts of costs to be awarded under Sections 1, 2 and 3 of the bill of costs according to the paying party

Step 5: Applicant and Respondent must exchange submissions and documents before assessment hearing
Applicant and Respondent must serve written submissions and bundle of authorities to the Court and to each other at least 1 clear day before assessment hearing 
Para 105(1) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- For any contested special date hearing and any hearing of an originating application before a Judge sitting in the General Division, subject to the Rules of Court 2021 and unless otherwise directed by the Court, each party must:
a. Submit to the Court and serve on the other party a hard copy of the following documents at least 1 clear day before the hearing:
(i) Written submissions (with a cover page and a table of contents); and
(ii) Bundle of authorities (which are in compliance with the requirements under paragraph 101(5) to (11) of these Practice Directions); and
b. File written submissions: File a soft copy of the written submissions into the electronic case file using the Electronic Filing Service at least 1 clear day before the hearing.

Instructions for written submissions 
Para 105(2) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Written submissions for originating applications should not exceed 35 pages (including the cover page, table of contents and all annexes and appendices). All written submissions for originating applications must include a cover page and a table of contents. Parties are to note that where the Court allows the prescribed page limit to be exceeded, fees are payable under the Fourth Schedule to the Rules of Court 2021

Instructions for bundle of authorities 
Para 105(1) of Supreme Court Practice Directions 2021 
- Bundle of authorities must comply with the requirements under paragraph 101(5) to (11) of these Practice Directions

Step 6: After assessment hearing and Registrar has decided on the amount of costs payable by Respondent to Applicant => Applicant must apply for certificate and enforcement order 
After assessment hearing and Registrar has decided, Applicant must apply for certificate
O 21 r 25
- When the bill of costs has been assessed, the solicitor must apply for the certificate of the Registrar for the amount of costs allowed by the Registrar.

After obtaining certificate, Applicant can apply for enforcement order within 48 hours if no application for review
O 21 r 26
- Upon the assessment of the bill of costs, the certificate of the Registrar, unless set aside, is conclusive as to the amount of costs allowed, and, where the order contains a submission to pay, the solicitor may after 48 hours, if there is no application for review, apply for an enforcement order to be issued in respect of the costs allowed

Step 7: Dissatisfied party can apply for review 
O 21 r 27
(1) Dissatisfied party can apply to Judge to review assessment decision: Any party to any assessment proceedings who is dissatisfied with the decision of the Registrar in respect of any item may apply to a Judge to review the assessment as to that item or part of an item, as the case may be.
(2) Dissatisfied party must make application for review within 14 days after assessment decision: An application under this Rule for review of the Registrar’s decision may be made at any time within 14 days after that decision.
(3) Application for review must be made by summons and heard in chambers: An application under this Rule must be made by summons and must be heard in chambers, unless the Judge otherwise orders.
(4) Application for review does not prejudice power of Registrar to issue an interim certificate for items not under review: An application under this Rule for review of the Registrar’s decision in respect of any item does not prejudice the power of the Registrar under Rule 14 to issue an interim certificate in respect of the items of the Registrar’s decision which are not the subject of the review.
(5) Definition of Judge – DJ, GDHC, ADHC, CA: In this Rule and Rule 28, “Judge” means a Judge sitting in the General Division or a District Judge and includes a judge sitting in the Appellate Division or the Court of Appeal where appropriate.

O 21 r 28 
(1) Parties cannot produce further evidence on review unless Judge directs so: Unless the Judge otherwise directs, no further evidence may be received on the hearing of the review of the Registrar’s decision by the Judge, but except as mentioned, on the hearing of the review, the Judge may exercise all such powers and discretion as are vested in the Registrar in relation to the subject matter of the application.
(2) Judge on review can order amendment of Registrar’s certificate or can remit matter back to Registrar for assessment (remission only allowed if the item under review is not as to amount): At the conclusion of the review, the Judge may make such order as the circumstances require, and in particular may order the Registrar’s certificate to be amended or (except where the dispute as to the item under review is as to amount only) order the item to be remitted to the Registrar for assessment.

Judge on review is hearing matter de novo
Judge on review is not fettered by Registrar’s decision even though he ought to give appropriate weightage to the Registrar’s decision 
Shorvon Simon v Singapore Medical Council 
- When judge on review hears matter de novo, he is not fettered by the discretion exercised by the Registrar and may substitute his discretion for that of the Registrar although he ought to give appropriate weightage to the Registrar’s decision on the quantum allowed
- Different from an appellate Court hearing the matter as an appellate Court would be reluctant to interfere with a lower Court’s exercise of discretion unless the decision was manifestly wrong or exercised on wrong principles 

Step 8: Dissatisfied party can also appeal 
Note:
- Dissatisfied party can either appeal costs order without review or apply for review then appeal decision on review 





Preparation of Bill of Costs 
Context
- Bill of Costs can be used in assessment hearings for S&C costs and P&P costs 
- For P&P costs: When Court orders assessment of costs, parties will have to attend a taxation hearing at a later date for Court to assess amount of P&P costs payable
- At taxation hearing, the receiving party will submit a Bill of Costs to decide the quantum of P&P costs payable by the paying party 
- Bill of Costs includes fees, charges, expenses and remuneration for: 
1. Work done in civil case
2. Work done for and in the taxation of costs
3. All disbursements made in the civil case

Bill of Costs has 3 sections 
1. Section 1 costs: Costs of main proceedings
2. Section 2 costs: Costs of assessment proceedings 
3. Section 3 costs: Disbursements 

Section 1 Costs
O 21 r 20(1)(a) 
- Work done in the cause or matter, except for assessment of costs

Court’s consideration of Section 1 Costs
O 21 r 2(2) 
- In exercising its power to fix or assess costs, the Court must have regard to all relevant circumstances, including:
i. Efforts to reach amicable resolution: Efforts made by the parties at amicable resolution;
j. Complexity of case and novelty of questions: The complexity of the case and the difficulty or novelty of the questions involved;
k. Skill, specialized knowledge, time labor expended by solicitor: The skill, specialised knowledge and responsibility required of, and the time and labour expended by, the solicitor
l. Urgency and importance of action to parties: The urgency and importance of the action to the parties;
m. Number of solicitors involved: The number of solicitors involved in the case for each party;
n. Parties’ conduct: The conduct of the parties;
o. Proportionality: The principle of proportionality; and
p. Stage which proceedings conclude: The stage at which the proceedings were concluded

Principle of proportionality and Framework 
Lin Jian Wei v Lim Eng Hock Peter
- When quantifying costs, Court has to consider:
a. Principle of proportionality: Costs should be proportionate to the amount or value at stake 
b. Framework: 
(i) Assess relative complexity of the matter and the work supposedly done against what was reasonably required in the prevailing circumstances, the reasonableness and proportionality of the amounts claimed on an item by item basis
(ii) Assess proportionality of the resulting aggregate costs
(iii) Consider costs guidelines 
(iv) No single consideration should ordinarily take precedence 

Section 2 Costs 
O 21 r 20(1)(b)
- Work done for and in the assessment of costs

Court’s consideration of Section 2 Costs
O 21 r 13
(1) Party entitled to costs of main proceedings also entitled to costs of assessment proceedings: Subject to the provisions of any written law, the party whose bill is being assessed is entitled to the party’s costs of the assessment proceedings.
(2) Party liable for cost may make written offer to settle costs of assessment proceedings any time 14 days after BC is delivered: The party liable to pay the costs of the proceedings which gave rise to the assessment proceedings may make a written offer to pay a specific sum in satisfaction of those costs which is expressed to be “without prejudice except as to the costs of the assessment of costs”, at any time before the expiration of 14 days after the delivery to the party of a copy of the bill of costs under Rule 19 and, where such an offer is made, the fact that it has been made must not be communicated to the Registrar until the question of the costs of the assessment proceedings falls to be decided.
(3) Registrar can consider written offer when assessing costs of assessment proceedings: The Registrar may take into account any offer made under paragraph (2) which has been brought to the Registrar’s attention.

Note:
- In practice: BC respondent can make an offer to settle costs of assessment proceedings with BC applicant and mark the offer as “without prejudice except as to the costs of the assessment of costs” => Means that the offer would not be shown to the Registrar contemporaneously => If the offer is accepted then there will be no issue on the costs of assessment hearing => If the offer is not accepted, Registrar assessing the costs of assessment hearing can consider the offer which was given by the BC respondent => If Registrar takes the view that the BC applicant should have accepted the offer and done away with the need for assessment hearing => Registrar can reduce the amount of costs of assessment hearing which the BC applicant is entitled to 

Section 3 Costs
O 21 r 20(1)(c)
- All disbursements made in the cause or matter

Scope of disbursements claimable
Ong Jane Rebecca v Lim Lie Hoa 
- General rule: Disbursements claimed should be expenses that have been paid 
- Disbursements include: 
a. Actual expenses paid out 
b. Loans or debts incurred by a party for the purpose of litigation provided that it was proven that the loans or debts were due and payable after taxation 
- Evidence such as invoices or bills must be tendered to prove disbursements 

S&C costs (information only) 
- S&C costs can be based on agreement between solicitor and client => If either solicitor or client dissatisfied, can apply for taxation: judicial process to determine reasonableness of legal fee => Taxation has two basis i.e. standard basis and indemnity basis 
- Solicitor can tax his own bills of costs if client disputes the quantum => Solicitor can only present bill for taxation one month from delivery of bill => After bills of cost is taxed, solicitor can sue client on the taxed costs 

S&C costs based on agreement between solicitor and client 
S111 of LPA 
(1) Agreement either by a gross sum or otherwise and at any rate: Subject to the provisions of any other written law, a solicitor or law corporation or limited liability law partnership may make an agreement in writing with any client respecting the amount and manner of payment for the whole or any part of his, her or its costs in respect of contentious business done or to be done by the solicitor or law corporation or limited liability law partnership, either by a gross sum or otherwise, and at either the same rate as or a greater or a lesser rate than that at which the solicitor or the law corporation or limited liability law partnership would otherwise be entitled to be remunerated.
(2) Agreement signed by client: Every such agreement must be signed by the client and is subject to the provisions and conditions contained in this Part.

S&C costs based on Registrar’s assessment – By application by anyone liable to pay bill/solicitor 
Part 1: Anyone liable to pay bill or Solicitor can apply to Court for an order for S&C costs to be assessed 
a. Client can make application to Court by OA or by summons (if there is a pending action) within 12 months after Solicitor delivers bill to client 
b. Solicitor can make application to Court by OA or by summons (if there is a pending action) after 1 month but within 12 months after he delivers bill to client 
S120(1) of LPA
- An order for the assessment of a bill of costs delivered by any solicitor may be obtained on an application made by originating application or, where there is a pending action, by summons by the party chargeable therewith, or by any person liable to pay the bill either to the party chargeable or to the solicitor, at any time within 12 months from the delivery of the bill, or, by the solicitor, after the expiry of one calendar month and within 12 months from the delivery of the bill

Part 2: Restriction – Court cannot make order for S&C costs to be assessed after payment of bill or expiry of 12 months from delivery of bill
S122 of LPA
- No order for assessment of S&C costs can be made after the expiry of 12 months from the delivery of a bill or costs or after payment of the bill 
- Except upon notice to solicitor and under special circumstances to be proved to the satisfaction of the Court 

Part 3: After Court orders that S&C costs to be assessed=> Any aggrieved party can apply by summons to amend or vary the order 
S120(2) of LPA
- The order is to contain such directions and conditions as the court thinks proper, and any party aggrieved by any such order may apply by summons that the order be amended or varied.

Part 4: Basis of Court’s assessment pursuant to O 21 r 23(2)
S126 of LPA
- Bills of costs for assessment as between solicitor and client must be drawn in the manner provided by the Rules of Court, and the assessment is governed by those rules 

Costs are to be assessed on indemnity basis 
O 21 r 23(2) 
- Costs are to be assessed on the indemnity basis 

Costs assessed on indemnity basis – All costs allowed unless they are unreasonable 
Client has burden of showing that any particular item is of unreasonable amount or unreasonably incurred and therefore disallowed 
O 21 r 22(3) 
- Assessment of costs on indemnity basis: 
* Costs are all costs except insofar as they are of an unreasonable amount or have been unreasonably incurred; and 
* Any doubts which the Registrar may have as to whether the costs were reasonably incurred or were reasonable in amount are to be resolved in favor of the receiving party  

Presumptions on reasonableness of costs incurred
O 21 r 23(2) 
- Costs are presumed:
a. Costs presumed to be reasonably incurred if incurred with client approved: To have been reasonably incurred if they were incurred with the express or implied approval of the client;
b. Costs presumed to be a reasonable amount if amount has client approval: To have been reasonable in amount if their amount was, expressly or impliedly, approved by the client; and
c. Costs presumed to be unreasonably incurred if they are unusual => Unless solicitor informed client that costs might not be recoverable on assessment of P&P costs: To have been unreasonably incurred if, in the circumstances of the case, they are of an unusual nature unless the solicitor satisfies the Registrar that prior to their being incurred the solicitor informed the client that they might not be allowed on an assessment of costs between the parties to the proceedings.

Definition of “client” 
O 21 r 23(3)
- In paragraph (2), references to the client are to be construed:
a. If the client at the material time lacked capacity within the meaning of the Mental Capacity Act and was represented by a person acting as litigation representative, as references to that person acting, where necessary, with the authority of the Court; and
b. If the client was at the material time a minor and represented by a person acting as litigation representative, as references to that person

S&C costs based on Registrar’s assessment – By solicitor and client consent  
Part 1: Solicitor and client can consent to allow Registrar to assess S&C Costs  
S120(3) of LPA
- In any case where a solicitor and his or her client consent to assessment of a solicitor’s bill, the Registrar may proceed to assess the bill even though there is no order therefor.
 
Part 2: Basis of Court’s assessment pursuant to O 21 r 23(2)
S126 of LPA
- Bills of costs for assessment as between solicitor and client must be drawn in the manner provided by the Rules of Court, and the assessment is governed by those rules 

Costs are to be assessed on indemnity basis 
O 21 r 23(2) 
- Costs are to be assessed on the indemnity basis 

Costs assessed on indemnity basis – All costs allowed unless they are unreasonable 
O 21 r 22(3) 
- Assessment of costs on indemnity basis: 
* Costs are all costs except insofar as they are of an unreasonable amount or have been unreasonably incurred; and 
* Any doubts which the Registrar may have as to whether the costs were reasonably incurred or were reasonable in amount are to be resolved in favor of the receiving party  

Presumptions on reasonableness of costs incurred
O 21 r 23(2) 
- Costs are presumed:
a. Costs presumed to be reasonably incurred if incurred with client approved: To have been reasonably incurred if they were incurred with the express or implied approval of the client;
b. Costs presumed to be a reasonable amount if amount has client approval: To have been reasonable in amount if their amount was, expressly or impliedly, approved by the client; and
c. Costs presumed to be unreasonably incurred if they are unusual => Unless solicitor informed client that costs might not be recoverable on assessment of P&P costs: To have been unreasonably incurred if, in the circumstances of the case, they are of an unusual nature unless the solicitor satisfies the Registrar that prior to their being incurred the solicitor informed the client that they might not be allowed on an assessment of costs between the parties to the proceedings

Definition of “client” 
O 21 r 23(3)
- In paragraph (2), references to the client are to be construed:
c. If the client at the material time lacked capacity within the meaning of the Mental Capacity Act and was represented by a person acting as litigation representative, as references to that person acting, where necessary, with the authority of the Court; and
d. If the client was at the material time a minor and represented by a person acting as litigation representative, as references to that person

Special Situation: Interaction between assessed S&C costs and any previous bill presented by solicitor  
If solicitor delivers bill to client => Court orders S&C costs to be assessed => Solicitor is allowed to pay a deliver a larger bill of costs  
O 21 r 23(4) 
- The delivery of a bill of costs by a solicitor to the solicitor’s client does not preclude the solicitor from presenting a bill for a larger amount or otherwise for assessment, if assessment is ordered by the Court or is consented to by the solicitor and the solicitor’s client

If assessed S&C costs is higher than previous bill presented => Solicitor is entitled to the S&C costs 
O 21 r 23(5)
- Upon an assessment mentioned in paragraph (4), the solicitor is entitled to such amount as is allowed by the Registrar, although such amount may be more than that claimed in any previous bill of costs delivered to the solicitor’s client.

Special Situation: Legal costs incurred in Court’s assessment of S&C costs 
a. If assessed S&C costs is less than 1/6 of the bills of costs presented by solicitor => Solicitor will pay the legal costs incurred in Court’s assessment 
b. If assessed S&C costs is more than 1/6 of the bills of costs presented by solicitor => Client will be liable to pay the legal costs incurred in the Court’s assessment 
S128 of LPA 
(1) In case any order for assessment is made upon the application of the party chargeable or liable, or of the solicitor, the costs of the order and assessment, except when the order has been made after the expiry of 12 months, must be paid according to the event of the assessment:
a. If the bill when assessed is less by a sixth part than the bill delivered, then the solicitor must pay the costs; or
b. If the bill when assessed is not less by a sixth part, then the party chargeable or liable, if the application is made by the party, or if the party attends the assessment, must pay the costs.
(2) Every order for such a reference must direct the Registrar to assess the costs of the reference, and to certify what, upon the reference, is found to be due to or from the solicitor in respect of the bill, and of the costs of the reference, if payable.
(3) The Registrar may certify specially any circumstances relating to the bill or assessment.
(4) The court may thereupon make any such order as it thinks right, respecting the payment of the costs of the assessment.
(5) Where such a reference is made, when it is not authorised except under special circumstances, the court may give any special directions relative to the costs of the reference.


FAQ:
- Do not need to know form number and contents; just where to find them
- S 17(c) of the SCJA is under “original jurisdiction powers” as it relates to Registrar’s appeals --> these are NOT the exercise of appellate powers by the HC. 
o Some interlocutory applications are heard at first instance by a registrar and not judge, and it is possible to file appeal to a judge. 
o BUT both registrar and judge are exercising HC jurisdiction (and not appellate jurisdiction), so it is de novo jurisdiction (same tier of court)
o See s 20 SJCA for appellate jurisdiction of HC
- How do you know if Df can pay?
o Df may have no assets that you can enforce against; qn is should Pf have commenced proceedings from a commercial POV, even though they had a good case. Have to hire investigators and bear in mind commercial considerations of cost.
